Sayyida al-Hurra was a largely forgotten **sixteenth-century North African queen and military leader** who ruled the city of T√©touan for over three decades, from approximately 1510 to 1542 CE. She was of Andalusi descent and her titles, Sayyida and Hurra, signified "master" or "leader" and indicated freedom and independence, respectively. During her reign, Morocco faced significant challenges including political fragmentation and attacks from Portuguese and Spanish forces. Al-Hurra emerged as a well-trained and respected governor who employed conventional warfare, piracy, political marriages, and alliances with the Ottomans to defend her people and northern Morocco. This study, addressing a lack of information on al-Hurra especially in English, used a historical and ethnographic approach, drawing data from Arabic secondary literature, interviews with local Moroccan historians, and visits to historical sites. The Arabic materials, often unavailable in English, provided insights into her biography, family, influence, and the history of T√©touan and Chefchaouen. Interviews were conducted with prominent historians Ali Risouni and Fatima Bouchmal, as well as Mohammed ben Saaid, who managed a museum related to her father's qasbah.

Northern Morocco, with its strategic location and resources, was a frequent target for foreign powers. The first half of the sixteenth century was a turbulent period that shaped modern Morocco, marked by the fall of the Wattasid ruling family, the rise of the Saadi rulers, and Spanish and Portuguese assaults along the coast. The Portuguese, for instance, occupied Moroccan cities like Tangier, Asilah, and Ksar es-Seghir in 1471\. Amidst this upheaval, al-Hurra was born around 1495 CE in Chefchaouen, a rich intellectual and political environment. Her father, Moulay Ali ben Rashid, born around 1440, was a sharif, meaning he traced his ancestry to the Prophet Muhammed. He founded Chefchaouen in 1471 to defend Morocco against the Portuguese, after moving to Granada in 1460 to learn military skills. After the fall of Granada, he returned as a "prince of war" and built a qasbah (fortress) in a mountainous area, away from coastal attacks, which is now a museum.

Al-Hurra's respected lineage and family's political authority were instrumental in her acquiring leadership and cultural skills. She received a strong education from prominent scholars, including instruction in law, religion, and Arabic, and was fluent in Spanish and Portuguese. Her education also extended to military matters, allowing her to forge a clear vision for her people's future.

Her rise to power began with her first marriage in 1510 to Moulay Ali Al-Mandri, the governor of T√©touan. This marriage aimed to strengthen ties between the two main ruling families in the North and secure an ally against the Portuguese. During her husband's frequent absences due to warfare, al-Hurra was left in charge and managed city affairs, gaining experience in statecraft and naval skills, which were vital given the Al-Mandri family's naval power. After Al-Mandri's death in 1519, her brother, Moulay Ibrahim ben Rashid, appointed her as the **governor of T√©touan**, a position the people accepted due to her proven competence.

For three decades, al-Hurra governed from her qasbah, which served as the center of her government, housing the treasury and prison. She supervised military operations and made major decisions with the help of advisers, including her brother, Ibrahim ben Rashid. Al-Hurra established distinct policies, notably **collaborating with the Ottoman corsair Barbarossa**, allowing his ships to use Moroccan ports and strengthening an alliance against the Iberian threat. This also opened markets for goods acquired through piracy, boosting the economy.

Al-Hurra was celebrated in Morocco as a **hero, a "princess of jihad,"** who defended the North from foreign threats. In Europe, however, she was often labeled a **"pirate queen"**. Muslim countries viewed piracy as a legitimate response to the forced expulsion of Andalusi Muslims by the Spanish, seeing it as a religious obligation to protect Muslim lands, while European countries held a less positive view. She expanded the fleet she inherited, building shipyards and increasing vessels and sailors. Her fleet operated freely in the western Mediterranean and eastern Atlantic, posing a significant threat to Iberian trade and occupation. Her forces engaged in both defensive and offensive campaigns, attacking European ships and cities, taking cargo and thousands of prisoners, which generated revenue through ransom or sale into slavery. For instance, in 1528, her ships captured the Portuguese "prince of the sea," Portundo, and in 1541, she freed 340 Spanish prisoners for a ransom. Notably, the Portuguese, despite occupying many northern Moroccan cities, never conquered T√©touan under her rule. Al-Hurra also commanded land forces, participating in sieges of occupied Moroccan cities like Asilah and leading attacks on Ceuta.

In 1541, al-Hurra entered into her second marriage with Sultan Ahmad al-Wattasi, the Wattasid ruler, in a move that underscored her immense political standing. Unusually for the time, the Sultan traveled to T√©touan to marry her, after which she remained in her city while he returned to Fez. This demonstrated her family's status and her importance as a political leader in stabilizing the country, as the Wattasid power was weakening and limited mainly to the capital. This marriage granted her absolute authority and allowed her further leeway in foreign affairs, enabling her to forge more alliances with the Ottomans.

However, this marriage ultimately did not save the Sultan's reign. The Saadi family, based in Marrakech, overthrew the Wattasids in 1549, posing a direct threat to al-Hurra's rule. Her first husband‚Äôs family, the Al-Mandris, rebelled against the Wattasids and supported the Saadis in 1542\. Her younger brother also joined the Saadi rebellion, and the death of her main supporter, her older brother Moulay Ibrahim ben Rashid in 1539, left her isolated. Furthermore, a conflict arose between her continued aggressive stance against the Portuguese and her husband's desire for improved relations, which she did not heed. After launching a war against a Portuguese governor and facing betrayal from her younger brother, she was defeated by the Al-Mandris, stripped of her possessions, and forced to return to Chefchaouen, where she lived out the remainder of her life and was buried. Her downfall was a consequence of the broader shift in power across Morocco as the Saadis sought to unify the country and end smaller emirates. Ali Risouni noted that al-Hurra had no children or descendants, and her house after her defeat is now a zawiya, a Sufi institution, and her tomb's location there suggests she may be regarded as a saint.

Today, the qasbah built by her father in Chefchaouen is the primary place where information about Sayyida al-Hurra is displayed. Historian Fatima Bouchmal, who wrote the museum signs in 2011, noted that these displays emphasize al-Hurra's exceptional role as a Moroccan woman who excelled in politics and governance, breaking the stereotype of a princess and distinguishing herself as a strong political and military leader with diplomatic relations. Despite her historical importance, al-Hurra is rarely mentioned in local or regional Arabic history books, with Western sources often reducing her to a "pirate queen". Historians like Fatima Bouchmal attribute this historical marginalization to a patriarchal society that does not traditionally celebrate women in leadership, as well as the French occupation's efforts to suppress positive portrayals of Muslim women. However, there is a renewed interest in her figure, with local historians working to raise awareness about her life and legacy.

According to the available sources, the factors contributing to al-Hurra's exceptional leadership for over three decades included her **respected sharifian lineage, her family‚Äôs leadership in the region, her excellent education in various fields, her intelligence, flexibility, fluency in multiple languages, skill in naval warfare, and a strong personality**. Both of her marriages also served the political goal of bringing stability to northern Morocco and enhancing her personal power.

The Barghawatas were a Berber tribal confederation on Morocco's Atlantic coast, belonging to the Masmuda confederacy. They established an independent state between 744 and 1058 CE. Some historians believe the name "Barghawata" is a phonetic deformation of "Barbati," a nickname for their leader Tarif, who is thought to have been born near Barbate, Spain. Other historians, like J√©r√¥me Carcopino, suggest the name is older and refers to the Baquates tribe, who lived near Volubilis until the 7th century. Few details are definitively known about the Barghawata, as most historical sources are posterior to their rule and often present contradictory accounts. However, a tradition from C√≥rdoba, Spain, attributed to Abu Salih Zammur in the mid-10th century, is considered the most detailed concerning the Barghawata.

The Barghawatas participated in the Berber Revolt of 739 or 740, alongside the Ghomara and Miknasa tribes. This revolt was ignited by Sufri Kharijite preachers, a Muslim sect advocating total egalitarianism in opposition to the Umayyad Caliphate's aristocracy. The rebels, led by Maysara al-Matghari, successfully seized control of nearly all of present-day Morocco and inspired further rebellions. They annihilated a Syrian army dispatched by the Umayyad caliph at the Battle of Bagdoura, but the rebel army was later defeated near Kairouan, Ifriqiya, in 741\. Following this, the rebel alliance dissolved. The Barghawatas, as founders of the revolt, grew resentful of attempts by later adherents, particularly Zenata chieftains and authoritarian Sufri commissars, to control the rebellion's leadership. With their primary objective of liberation from Umayyad rule achieved and little prospect of its re-imposition, the Barghawatas saw no point in continued military campaigns. In 742 or 743, they withdrew from the alliance, retreated to the Tamesna region on the Atlantic coast of Morocco, founded their new independent state, and abandoned Sufri Kharijitism.

The Barghawatas ruled the Tamesna region (between Safi and Sal√©) for over three centuries (744‚Äì1058 CE). Under the leadership of Tarif al-Matghari and his successors, the tribal kingdom was consolidated. The kings included ·π¢ƒÅli·∏• ibn Tarƒ´f (declared himself prophet in 744), Ilyas ibn Salih (792-842), Yunus ibn Ilyas (842-888), Abu-Ghufayl Muhammad (888-917), Abu al-Ansar Abdullah (917-961), and Abu Mansur Isa (961-?). They maintained initially good relations with the Umayyad Caliphate of Cordoba, but this broke down at the end of the 10th century, leading to Barghawata fending off Umayyad and Fatimid incursions. From the 11th century, they engaged in intensive guerrilla warfare with the Banu Ifran, which weakened them. Despite this, they successfully repelled Almoravid attacks, even causing the death of the Almoravid spiritual leader, Abdallah ibn Yasin, in battle on July 7, 1058\. The Barghawata were ultimately eliminated as a political and religious group by the Almohads in 1149\.

The Barghawata developed a **syncretic religion** inspired by Islam. This religion incorporated elements of Sunni, Shi'a, and Kharijite Islam, mixed with astrological and traditional Berber mythology. Their unique beliefs included a taboo surrounding eating eggs and chickens, and the belief that the saliva of Salih and his family contained *baraka* (blessedness). The Barghawata confederacy consisted of 29 tribes, with 12 adopting this specific Barghawata religion and 17 adhering to Islam. Some of the tribes adopting the Barghawata religion included Gerawa, Zouagha, Bran√®s, and Matghara, while Khariji Muslim tribes included Zenata-Jbal, Banu Ifren, and Trara. Notably, some constituent tribes, like Bran√®s, Matmata, Ifren, and Trara, were fractions of much larger tribal groups, and only their Tamesna-based segments joined the confederacy. It is also possible that the Barghawata had a Judeo-Berber background, although accounts of entire Berber tribes practicing Judaism are considered unreliable and appear later.

Arabic-language sources provide very sparse details about the Barghawata rulers, particularly for the period after 961 CE, with most accounts relying on later historians quoting older reports, often from Andalusi writers. Few names beyond the early dynastic rulers are preserved in Arabic historiography.

According to Arabic scholarship, the known chronology of Barghawata rulers includes **Salih ibn Tarif** (mid-8th century), **Ilyas ibn Salih** (approximately 792‚Äì842), **Yunus ibn Ilyas** (approximately 842‚Äì888), and **Abu Ghufayl Muhammad** (approximately 888‚Äì913). Salih ibn Tarif is well-attested in Arabic texts, while Ilyas ibn Salih and Yunus ibn Ilyas are mentioned in both Arabic and European reports and by Arabic and Andalusi chroniclers, respectively. Abu Ghufayl Muhammad is identified as the final named ruler before the historical record falls silent.

After Abu Ghufayl Muhammad, around 913 CE, Arabic sources provide no individual names for rulers until the fall of the Barghawata state in 1058 CE. Older chronicle traditions, such as those from al-Bakri, Ibn Hazm, and Ibn Khaldun, preserve some mid-10th-century reports, but after that, records become silent, with no Arabic inscriptions or documents found naming rulers from about 913 to 1058\. For this later period, Arabic sources only refer generally to **unnamed tribal chiefs** or **unnamed kings** who opposed the rising Almoravids.

The lack of names after 913 CE may be due to factors such as internal clan rotation or decentralized leadership, as suggested by Arabic historians like Ibn Khaldun. Additionally, the Barghawata‚Äôs own internal records and Tamazight-language narratives have not survived. Arabic historians often considered the Barghawata heretical and therefore frequently dismissed them without detailed records. Consequently, any claims of specific names for rulers in the period between 961 and 1058 CE likely originate from speculative reconstructions or oral tradition rather than verified Arabic documentation.

Amazigh tattooing, known as *oucham*, *tichrad*, or *tagezzayt*, is a sophisticated visual language historically inscribed on the skin of indigenous Amazigh communities in North Africa for millennia. It functions as a complex, non-literate system of communication, a visible archive of cultural memory, and a profound expression of identity, spirituality, and social standing. Primarily practiced by and for women, these tattoos constitute a powerful nexus of cultural heritage and female agency, making the decorated female body a public and permanent symbol of a distinct identity. In a culture where history was often transmitted orally, the body itself served as a text, conveying tribal affiliation, personal milestones, and deeply held beliefs about the cosmos. The practice was not uniform across the vast Amazigh territory, Tamazgha, but rather formed a dynamic lexicon of symbols and patterns that varied by tribe, region, and family, reflecting the rich diversity of Amazigh groups. This tradition operated as a parallel system of cultural inheritance, a matrilineal archive passed from mother to daughter, preserving a uniquely female-centric worldview focused on protection, fertility, community, and resilience, outside of patriarchal textual traditions.

The tradition of Amazigh tattooing has profound antiquity, rooted in the pre-Islamic era of North Africa and stretching back thousands of years, as evidenced by tattooed mummies in the region. Scholarly inquiry connects its geometric motifs to abstract symbols found in Neolithic rock art from the Capsian period (circa 7500‚Äì4000 BC). These archaic geometrical designs, featuring dots, circles, chevrons, and rectilinear combinations, are considered by some prehistorians to be the genesis of a symbolic writing system that may have given rise to the Libyco-Berber alphabet, Tifinagh. This suggests tattooing is a vital branch of a comprehensive system of Amazigh graphic communication, sharing an ancestral vocabulary with patterns on pottery, textiles, and jewelry.

Beyond aesthetics, Amazigh tattoos primarily served an apotropaic function, acting as "permanent amulets" (*h'erz*) to ward off evil spirits (*jnoun*) and deflect the evil eye. This belief dictated strategic placement, often near potential spiritual entry points like the eyes, nostrils, and mouth, or on vulnerable areas like the hands, feet, and ankles. The practice was also deeply intertwined with magico-medical rituals and therapeutic healing, believed to have curative properties for specific physical ailments or to prevent disease. For example, neck patterns were thought to protect against goiter, and ankle markings could treat leg numbness. The *Dem en-nfasse* ("blood of childbirth") tattoo on external ankles was believed to facilitate easy delivery.

The application of an Amazigh tattoo was a sacred, female-centric ritual marking significant life transitions. It was overseen by a *tatoueuse*, typically an elder woman respected for her knowledge of symbols and rituals. Simple tools like a needle or sharpened Barbary fig thorn were used. The desired pattern was first drawn with soot or charcoal, then meticulously pricked into the skin until blood was drawn. The pigment, creating a characteristic blue-green hue, was a mixture of natural ingredients like soot, carbon black, *khol* (antimony), or gunpowder, rubbed into the fresh wounds. A paste called *tizegzawt*, made from macerated plants like bean leaves or black nightshade, was repeatedly applied to enhance and fix the color as the skin healed. The pain endured during this process was an integral component of the rite of passage, signifying a woman's strength and readiness for the next stage of life. This process marked key milestones: a girl's entry into puberty, her marriage, and the birth of her children. The first facial tattoo, the *siyala*‚Äîa vertical line on the chin‚Äîwas typically applied at the onset of menstruation, signifying the transition from girl to woman, capable of motherhood and carrying tribal heritage. The permanent mark was a badge of honor, symbolizing resilience and full integration into the community's social and spiritual fabric, charging the "permanent amulet" with the wearer's fortitude and life force.

The symbolic language of Amazigh tattoos is a rich vocabulary drawn from geometry, nature, the cosmos, and human experience, forming a cohesive aesthetic system present across all forms of female-created art, including textiles, pottery, and jewelry. Fundamental geometric shapes form the core of this lexicon, each imbued with layers of meaning. The **`diamond (lozenge ‚Øà)`** is the quintessential representation of femininity, the womb, protection for the family, and the home. It is often associated with the serpent, symbolizing a union of feminine and masculine/virile principles. The **triangle (‚µú or Œî)** is a potent symbol of fertility and life. An upward-pointing triangle signifies fire, masculinity, and virility, while a downward-pointing triangle symbolizes water, femininity, and life-giving forces. Their combination forms a hexagram, representing harmony. **Lines and dots** carry significant weight; straight lines often represent the path of life, family lineage, or the connection between terrestrial and divine. Dots frequently symbolize seeds, a metaphor for fertility and new life, and can map out genealogies or the endurance of a family line. The **cross (+ or ‚µç)**, predating Christian influence, represents the four cardinal directions, serving as a sign of guidance, spiritual protection, and balance. It can also symbolize the eye of God.

The Amazigh worldview is deeply connected to the natural and cosmic environment, reflected in their symbolic vocabulary. **Fauna** symbols include the **serpent (‚µñ)**, representing virility, regeneration, healing, and protection against evil spirits. The **scorpion (ìÜô)** is a paradoxical symbol of danger and death, yet also a powerful protector and symbol of defense and resilience. **Birds (ìÖÉ)** are seen as messengers between heaven and earth, symbolizing freedom, vision, and agility, with the "Partridge Eye" motif representing feminine beauty and grace. The **fish (ìÜà)**, living in water, symbolizes abundance, prosperity, safety, and protection against the evil eye. The **frog** and **spider** are feminine symbols; the frog is associated with fertility and magical rituals, while the spider represents patient, laborious, and harmonious domestic life. **Flora** symbols include the **palm tree** (*siyala*), the most iconic facial tattoo (a vertical line on the chin), directly invoking fertility and life, strongly associated with the ancient goddess Tanit. **Seeds (‚µá)** represent male seed, potential, new beginnings, and the life cycle. **Wheat or barley** symbolize both life and sustenance, and the cyclical nature of harvest. **Cosmology** is represented by the **sun (‚òÄ)** and **moon (‚òæ)**, signifying fundamental duality; the sun symbolizes life, energy, enlightenment, and the masculine principle, while the moon represents femininity, regeneration, the cycles of life and death, and the passage of time.

Beyond the natural world, symbols also serve as direct markers of identity, status, and spiritual defense. The **Yaz (‚µ£)**, resembling the Tifinagh letter for "Z," is the most recognizable emblem of Amazigh identity, representing the "Free Man" (*Amazigh*), freedom, resilience, and cultural pride. The **Eye (‚µÑ)** is a direct apotropaic mark for protection against the evil eye, often with a cross within it to deflect malevolent forces. The **Hand of Fatima (Khamsa or ‚µõ)**, though shared with other cultures, is widely used in Amazigh art for protection, especially for women and children. The **weaving comb**, an essential tool for women's textile creation, is a feminine symbol of creativity, fertility, order, and harmonious movement.

The Souss region of southern Morocco, the heartland of the Chleuh people (the largest Amazigh group), maintains a distinctive culture characterized by the Tashelhit language, unique customs, and rich artistic heritage. Women have traditionally been primary artisans, their creative endeavors providing economic independence and acting as the main vehicle for transmitting cultural symbols. Despite this cultural richness, direct and detailed textual descriptions of specific Souss tattoo patterns are notably scarce in ethnographic records, likely due to oral and matrilineal transmission and potential biases in historical research. Understanding Souss tattoo patterns requires analyzing visual evidence, such as photographs of young women from the Ida Ou Zekri tribe bearing facial tattoos, and cross-referencing them with the general Amazigh symbolic lexicon. A comprehensive understanding can also be achieved by treating the region's broader material culture as a "Rosetta Stone". The women who received and administered tattoos were also the weavers of textiles and makers of symbolic jewelry, creating a unified visual language. The Souss, particularly around Tiznit, is renowned for its distinctive silver and enamel jewelry, which features geometric patterns like lozenges, triangles, chevrons, and dots and lines. The prevalence of the lozenge (diamond) shape, symbolizing femininity and protection, in Tiznit fibulae strongly suggests it was a dominant motif in Souss tattooing. Similarly, the geometric complexity and symbolic density in the region's carpets and textiles, exclusively women's work, reflect a sophisticated visual language centered on fertility, protection, and tribal identity.

The Doukkala region, a coastal plain south of Casablanca, presents a more complex and layered history of tattooing due to profound cultural transformation and syncretism. Historically, "Doukkala" referred to a powerful confederation of Amazigh tribes, predominantly Masmuda and Sanhaja groups, who formed a core part of the independent Barghawata confederation from 744 to 1058 AD. This deep history establishes an undeniable Amazigh cultural substrate in the region that would have included ancient tattooing traditions. The 12th century marked a pivotal and violent turning point with the Almohad conquest. After defeating the Doukkala tribes who sided with the Almoravids, Abd al-Mu'min purged populations and settled large numbers of Arab Bedouin tribes, notably the Banu Hilal, fundamentally altering the demographic and cultural landscape. This led to centuries of linguistic and cultural mixing and gradual Arabization of the remaining Amazigh population.

Despite this history of Arabization, tattooing persisted in Doukkala well into the 20th century, understood as a syncretic blend, an "Arabo-Amazigh" cultural fusion. A 1949 study in *Hesp√©ris* confirmed the practice, noting that "Among the Doukkala... these tattoos are common: 36 on the right shoulder, 10 on the left shoulder, out of 89 tattooed individuals," providing specific data on placement. For tribes near El Jadida, such as the Oulad Bouaziz (descendants of Hilalian migrations), their cultural practices, including tattooing, would have been part of this syncretic regional tradition, likely including a mix of ancient Amazigh geometric patterns absorbed from original inhabitants and motifs characteristic of Bedouin Arab traditions. The challenge in defining a purely "Amazigh" tattoo lexicon for Doukkala lies in this assimilation and the nature of its documentation; French colonial ethnography often miscategorized the local tattooing tradition as simply "Arab" or a general "rural" custom, overlooking surviving Amazigh elements. This has resulted in an "ethnographic silence" where the specific Amazigh symbolic language was transformed, assimilated, and miscategorized, rather than entirely vanishing.

The ancient tradition of Amazigh tattooing has experienced a sharp decline since the mid-20th century, bringing it to the brink of disappearance. This decline is attributed to a confluence of social, religious, and political forces. **Religious proscription** is a primary driver, with stricter interpretations of Sunni Islam widely considering tattooing *haram* (forbidden) as an alteration of God's creation. This discourse gained traction from the 1970s, creating social pressure and shame, leading many women to regret their tattoos or attempt painful removal. **Modernization and urbanization** eroded the social context of tattoos; a tattoo signifying tribal membership or readiness for marriage lost its function in urban centers. **The colonial gaze and shifting beauty standards** also played a role. While some Amazigh women reportedly used tattoos as a form of resistance against colonial soldiers, French colonial ethnography often framed tattooing as "primitive". Furthermore, the French establishment of brothels, sometimes involving abducted tattooed rural Amazigh women, created a damaging association between tattoos and prostitution in the urban colonial mindset. This stigma, coupled with post-independence globalized beauty standards, marginalized traditional tattoos as outdated.

Despite decades of decline, the 21st century has witnessed a powerful renaissance of Amazigh tattoo symbolism, driven by a new generation of Moroccans connecting with their ancestral heritage. This revival adapts the ancient symbolic lexicon to the modern art of ink tattooing, rather than returning to past ritualistic practices. Tattoo studios in major Moroccan cities are increasingly receiving requests for designs based on traditional Amazigh motifs, with artists becoming new custodians, researching meanings and creating contemporary designs. For this new generation, symbols represent empowerment, pride, and a public declaration of Amazigh identity. The Yaz (‚µ£), in particular, has become an iconic symbol of this movement, transforming the tattoo from a tribal marker into a broader statement of belonging to a transnational Amazigh culture, linking past and present, and celebrating human expression.

The tradition of Amazigh tattooing is a profound and complex cultural phenomenon, a visual language inscribed on the body that serves as a living document of history, spirituality, and female-centric heritage. These markings, rooted in the Neolithic period, functioned as permanent amulets for protection, sacred rites of passage, and a matrilineal archive for transmitting cultural values. Regional variations are evident; in the Souss Valley, tattoos are part of a cohesive Amazigh artistic heritage shared across textiles, jewelry, and skin, with its essence reconstructible through rich material culture. In contrast, the Doukkala plains show a cultural layering where an ancient Amazigh practice was transformed by centuries of Arabization, obscuring original elements in historical records. The modern era, with religious orthodoxy, urbanization, and shifting beauty standards, brought the tradition to near disappearance. However, the enduring power of these symbols is evident in their contemporary renaissance, reclaimed by a new generation for cultural pride, identity, and empowerment.

For individuals seeking a deeper connection with this ancestral heritage, consulting key ethnographic texts like *Amazigh Arts in Morocco* by Cynthia J. Becker, articles on tattooing by Susan Searight, and historical archives of the journal *Hesp√©ris* is recommended. Engaging with contemporary artists, who are the new custodians and interpreters of this art form, is also a direct way to connect with the evolving tradition. Finally, exploring material culture collections, such as the Mus√©e Pierre Berg√© des arts berb√®res in Marrakech, which holds a significant collection of Amazigh artifacts including textiles, jewelry, pottery, and woodwork, provides an immersive education in the geometric patterns and symbolic motifs that once adorned the skin.

The chronology of Barghawata rulers, spanning from 744 to 1058 CE, can be organized by their historical reliability. **Salih ibn Tarif**, the founder, is considered reliable by ancient Arabic sources such as Ibn Khaldun and al-Bakri, and he is noted for claiming prophecy and establishing a unique sacred book around 744 to 792 CE. His successor, **Ilyas ibn Salih**, who reigned from approximately 792 to 842 CE, is also reliably attested and is known for emphasizing the Barghawata creed and persecuting Muslims.

Following Ilyas ibn Salih was **Yunus ibn Ilyas**, whose rule from 842 to 888 CE is reliably documented. He is noted for moderating the persecution and favoring stability. The state's military power is said to have reached its peak under **Abu Ghufayl Muhammad ibn Yunus**, whose reign from 888 to 917 CE is also considered reliable.

After Abu Ghufayl Muhammad, the historical record becomes less definitive. **Abu al-Ansar Abdullah** is mentioned in popular sources and later Arabic allusions as a ruler from 917 to 961 CE, said to have restored the state's strength. Subsequently, **Abu Mansur Isa ibn Abi al-Ansar** is considered semi-reliable for a period after 961 CE, notably due to an embassy sent to al-Hakam al-Mustansir Billah in Andalusia in 963 CE, which historically confirms his existence.

For the period after Abu Mansur Isa until the fall of the Barghawata state in 1058 CE, the available sources mention a gap in specific named rulers. While **Abu Hafs Abdullah** is sometimes mentioned in popular or unproven accounts as a later leader, and an **"Ibn Abi al-Ansar"** without a specific name also appears in some blogs, these names are not explicitly supported by classical Arabic sources. Ibn Khaldun's accounts for this later period only refer to **unnamed kings** who fought against the Almoravids, indicating a lack of specific names for the final rulers. The Barghawata state ultimately fell in 1058 CE at the hands of the Almoravids, led by Abdallah ibn Yasin, without any named final rulers in the historical record.

The history of human habitation in Morocco extends to the Lower Paleolithic period, with archaeological evidence showing hominids present at least 400,000 years ago in Sal√©, and early *Homo sapiens* dating back 300,000 years at Jebel Irhoud. Small perforated seashell beads discovered in Taforalt are 82,000 years old, representing the earliest known evidence of personal adornment. During Mesolithic times (20,000 to 5000 years ago), Morocco was a savanna environment, hospitable to hunters and gatherers of the Capsian culture. The Neolithic period saw hunters and herders flourish, with the domestication of cattle and cultivation of crops. The coastal regions shared in the Cardium pottery culture, common throughout the Mediterranean. In the Chalcolithic or copper age, the Beaker culture reached Morocco's north coast.

Recorded history began with the **Phoenician colonization** of the Moroccan coast between the 8th and 6th centuries BCE. Phoenician traders established depots for salt and ore along the coast and rivers, with major early settlements at Chellah, Lixus, and Mogador. By the 5th century BCE, **Carthage** extended its hegemony over much of North Africa, developing commercial relations with Berber tribes of the interior and paying them tribute for raw materials. This period marked the beginning of centuries of foreign rule in northern Morocco.

The independent tribal Berber kingdom of **Mauretania** emerged around the 3rd century BCE on the Mediterranean coast of North Africa, corresponding to northern modern-day Morocco. Early recorded history mentions Phoenician and Carthaginian settlements, but indigenous Berber kings ruled inland territories. Mauretania became a client state of the Roman Empire in 33 BCE and a full province, Mauretania Tingitana, around 39 or 40 CE, with Volubilis as its capital. Roman control was primarily alliances with tribes rather than military occupation, extending only to economically useful or defensible areas. During Emperor Augustus's reign, three Roman citizen colonies were founded near the Atlantic coast. Christianity was introduced in the 2nd century CE and gained converts, leading to Christianization of Romanized areas by the late 4th century. The region also had a substantial Jewish population.

The region was conquered by Muslims in the early 8th century CE, bringing Arabic and Islam to the area. Morocco was initially a subsidiary province of Ifriqiya, with local governors appointed from Kairouan. Indigenous Berber tribes adopted Islam but retained customary laws and paid taxes to the new administration. In 740 CE, spurred by Kharijite agitators, the Berber population revolted against the ruling Umayyad Caliphate. The **Berber Revolt** spread quickly, and by 742 CE, Morocco had broken away from Umayyad and Abbasid control, fragmenting into small, independent Berber states.

Among these independent states were the **Barghawata** (744‚Äì1058), a confederation of Berber groups inhabiting the Atlantic coast, who established an independent state in the Tamesna region under Tarif al-Matghari after allying with the Sufri Kharijite rebellion. The **Emirate of Sijilmasa** (757‚Äì976) was founded by the Midrarid dynasty in the Tafilalt region, becoming an important trade center at the northern edge of the Sahara. The **Kingdom of Nekor** (710‚Äì1019) was an emirate in the Rif area, founded by Salih I ibn Mansur and ruled by the Ban≈´ SƒÅlih dynasty. It even faced a Viking incursion in 859\.

The **Idrisid dynasty** (788‚Äì974), founded by Idris I, is considered the founder of the first Moroccan state. Idris I, a great-grandchild of Hasan ibn Ali, fled to the Maghreb after a battle near Mecca and settled in Volubilis in 788\. The powerful Awraba Berbers welcomed him as their 'imam' and he began asserting authority, founding a settlement called Medinat Fas in 789\. After Idris I was poisoned in 791, his son Idris II continued his work. Idris II (791‚Äì828) weakened Awraba power by inviting Arab settlers and moving his seat of government to Fes, which he developed and gave a more Arab character. By his death, the Idrisid state spanned from western Algeria to the Sous in southern Morocco, becoming the leading state, though Sijilmasa, Barghawata, and Nekor remained outside their control.

Following Idris II, the dynasty's power declined due to divisions among his sons, forming eight Idrisid statelets. During this time, Islamic and Arabic culture gained a stronghold in towns like Fes, which became an important religious center. However, the Idrisids primarily ruled the towns, with most of Morocco's population still speaking Berber languages and adhering to various Islamic doctrines. The decline accelerated after Yahya I's death in 863, with further divisions and Berber Khariji-Sufri tribes forming a front against them. The **Fatimids** from the east and **Umayyads** of Cordoba intervened, with the Miknasa tribe acting on behalf of the Fatimids. After various shifts in allegiance and internal conflicts, the Idrisids were ultimately expelled from their territories by the Umayyads in 974, bringing an end to the dynasty. Despite their fall, the Idrisids spawned many sharifian families, and their tombs became important pilgrimage sites in Fes and Moulay Idriss.

The **Almoravid dynasty** (c.1060‚Äì1147) originated among the Lamtuna nomadic Berber tribe of the Sanhaja. They unified Morocco in the late 10th century, annexing the Emirate of Sijilmasa and the Barghawata. Under Yusuf ibn Tashfin, they were invited to Al-Andalus to defend against Christian kingdoms, successfully repelling Christian forces and annexing most of the major *taifas*. The Almoravid power began to decline in the first half of the 12th century after a defeat at the Battle of Ourique and the rise of the Almohads. Their dynasty fell with the Almohad conquest of Marrakech in 1147\. The Almoravid movement was fueled by the puritan zeal of Abdallah ibn Yasin, who advocated for conquest as a necessary extension of Islamicization, identifying tribalism as an obstacle to the unity of Muslims under sacred law.

The **Almohad Caliphate** (c. 1121‚Äì1269) was founded in the 12th century by Ibn Tumart among the Masmuda Berber tribes in the Atlas Mountains of southern Morocco. Ibn Tumart developed a strict unitarian doctrine (*tawhid*) in revolt against anthropomorphism in Muslim orthodoxy, and his followers were known as *al-Muwa·∏•·∏•id≈´n* ("those who affirm the unity of God"). Around 1120, Ibn Tumart established a Berber state in Tinmel, serving as both a spiritual and military center. Despite a devastating defeat at the Battle of al-Buhayra in 1130, the movement survived under Abd al-Mu'min, who became Caliph after Ibn Tumart's death. Abd al-Mu'min (1130‚Äì1163) not only eliminated the Almoravids but extended Almohad power across North Africa as far as Egypt and into the Iberian Peninsula (Al-Andalus). The Almohads transferred the capital of Muslim Iberia from C√≥rdoba to Seville, where they built a great mosque and a palace. However, the Almohad advance was broken by a coalition of Christian kings at the Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa in 1212\. Internal coups and the inability to protect Andalusian cities led to the end of the Almohad era in Spain by 1228, followed by a massive Christian reconquista. In Africa, the Almohads lost territories due to tribal revolts, particularly from the Banu Marin (Marinids). The last Almohad ruler, Idris II, was murdered in Marrakech in 1269, marking the fall of the dynasty.

The **Marinid dynasty** (c. 1244‚Äì1465) was of Berber origin, although they claimed Arab ancestry. They entered Morocco in the early 13th century, initially submitting to the Almohads before rising as a political power. They gradually seized major cities like Taza, Rabat, Sal√©, Meknes, and Fez from the weakened Almohads, capturing Marrakech in 1269\. The Marinids actively participated in conflicts in Iberia, aiming to control the Strait of Gibraltar, and occupied several Spanish towns. In 1276, they founded Fes Jdid, making it their administrative and military center, and the Marinid period marked Fes's golden age as an intellectual center, with the establishment of the first madrasas in the city and country. At their height under Abu al-Hasan Ali (r. 1331‚Äì1348), the Marinids briefly reunited the Maghreb, controlling a vast territory from southern Morocco to Tripoli. However, the dynasty declined after Abu Inan Faris's death in 1358, with power shifting to viziers, political anarchy, division of the country, and European attacks on the coast. A financial crisis further weakened the state, leading to a massacre of the Wattasid family by Sultan Abdalhaqq II in 1459, who was then murdered in the 1465 revolt, ending the Marinid dynasty.

The **Wattasid dynasty** (c. 1472‚Äì1554) assumed power when Morocco was in decline. They had been autonomous governors of the eastern Rif and had close ties to the Marinids. After a power struggle, Abu Abd Allah al-Sheikh Muhammad ibn Yahya founded the Kingdom of Fez and established the dynasty. However, the Wattasid rulers failed to protect Morocco from foreign incursions, and the Portuguese increased their presence on the coast. This contrasted with the military successes of the rising **Saadian dynasty** in the south, which seized Marrakech in 1524 and defeated the Portuguese at Agadir in 1537\. The Saadians were regarded as heroes by the people, enabling them to retake Portuguese strongholds. They eventually attacked and overthrew the Wattasids, killing their last sultan in 1554\.

The **Saadi dynasty** (1549‚Äì1659) was the first of the Sharifian dynasties, claiming descent from Prophet Muhammad. They initially controlled only southern areas but their growing power led to the Wattasids recognizing their rule over southern Morocco by 1528\. From 1549, they ruled the entire region. In 1590, Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur's expedition to the Songhai Empire resulted in its collapse and the establishment of the Pashalik of Timbuktu. The Saadi dynasty fell in 1659, when Mohammed al-Hajj ibn Abu Bakr al-Dila'i was proclaimed sultan.

The **Republic of Sal√©** (1624‚Äì1668) originated in the early 17th century with the arrival of Moriscos from Spain, who settled in Rabat across the Bou Regreg river from Sal√©. These communities thrived as pirates, expanding operations in the Mediterranean and Atlantic. In 1624, the Dutchman Jan Janszoon became "Grand Admiral" and President of the Corsair Republic. After Janszoon left, the Moriscos declared a Republic, ruled by a council. By 1641, the Zaouia of Dila imposed religious hegemony, and eventually, Sultan Al-Rashid of the Alawi dynasty seized Rabat and Sal√©, ending its independence by 1668\.

The **Alawi dynasty** (since 1666\) is the current Moroccan royal family, claiming descent from Prophet Muhammad through his daughter Fatima az-Zahrah. Sharif ibn Ali became Prince of Tafilalt in 1631, and his son Mulay Al-Rashid (1664‚Äì1672) unified the country. The Alawi kingdom was consolidated by Ismail Ibn Sharif (1672‚Äì1727), who created a unified state through an army of slaves, reoccupying Tangiers in 1684 and driving the Spanish from Larache in 1689\. However, after his death, power struggles resumed, and the kingdom was only unified again under Muhammad III (1757‚Äì1790), who allowed tribes to preserve their autonomy. On December 20, 1777, Morocco became the first state to recognize the sovereignty of the newly independent United States. During the reigns of Muhammad IV (1859‚Äì1873) and Hassan I (1873‚Äì1894), the Alawis fostered trade links, modernized the army and administration, but Morocco's independence was increasingly influenced by European powers, leading to the Madrid Conference of 1880 and the Moroccan Crises of 1905‚Äì1906 and 1911\.

**European influence** intensified from the mid-19th century. After the French conquest of Algeria, the Franco-Moroccan War occurred in 1844\. The Anglo-Moroccan Treaty of 1856 opened up Morocco to foreign trade, reducing the Makhzen's control. The Hispano-Moroccan War (1859‚Äì1860) led to Morocco taking a large British loan to pay war debts. Morocco's instability and economic concessions led to increased European intervention. French influence grew significantly, opposed by Germany, but the Algeciras Conference of 1906 formalized France's "special position". A civil war, the Hafidiya (1907), saw Abd al-Hafid seize the throne from his brother, supported by different European factions.

The **French and Spanish protectorate** was established by the Treaty of Fez on March 30, 1912, after the Agadir Crisis. Spain was granted a zone of influence in northern and southern Morocco. Tangier received special status as an international zone. Under the protectorate, the sultan remained a figurehead, but real power lay with the colonial administration, which focused on exploiting Morocco's mineral wealth, developing infrastructure, and promoting a modern agricultural sector. Tens of thousands of colonists acquired large tracts of land.

**Nationalism and anti-colonial resistance** emerged. The independent **Republic of the Rif** (1921‚Äì1926), led by Abd el-Krim, existed in the Spanish Protectorate, but was eventually suppressed by Spanish and French troops. Nationalist groups like the *Comit√© d'Action Marocaine* (CAM) proposed reforms and later formed political parties, but faced French suppression. The **Istiqlal Party's Manifesto of Independence** in January 1944 established Sultan Muhammad V as a symbol of the nationalist struggle. The French regime faced opposition from tribes, and the exile of Sultan Muhammad V in 1953 sparked active opposition and rising violence. Faced with Moroccan demands and a deteriorating situation in Algeria, the French brought Muhammad V back to Morocco, leading to negotiations for independence.

**Independent Morocco** began in late 1955, with Sultan Mohammed V negotiating gradual restoration of independence. Morocco acquired limited home rule in February 1956, and full independence was formalized with the French-Moroccan Agreement on March 22, 1956\. Spain relinquished its protectorate in April 1956, and Tangier was reintegrated in October 1956\. Mohammed V established a modern governmental structure under a constitutional monarchy, assuming the title Mohammed V in 1957\. His son, **King Hassan II** (1961‚Äì1999), faced significant political unrest, known as "the years of lead." He oversaw new constitutions and parliamentary government, but also military coups. Border skirmishes with Algeria escalated into the **Sand War** in 1963\. In 1973, Hassan II announced the **Moroccanization** policy, transferring foreign-owned businesses and assets to political loyalists. Morocco also participated in the Yom Kippur War in 1973, deploying troops to the Syrian front.

The **Western Sahara conflict** began with Spain ceding administration of the territory to Morocco and Mauritania in early 1976\. Morocco took control of the northern two-thirds, while the **Polisario Front** declared the Saharawi Arab Democratic Republic (SADR) and formed a government-in-exile. Morocco sent combat forces to confront the Polisario, leading to intensive warfare. Mauritania renounced its claim in 1979, and Morocco annexed the entire territory, building a 2,500-kilometer sand berm. A UN peace plan and cease-fire went into effect in 1991, but the status of the territory remains unresolved. Political reforms in the 1990s led to a new bicameral legislature.

Upon the death of Hassan II in 1999, **King Mohammed VI** (since 1999\) enacted reforms, improving human rights, releasing political prisoners, and establishing a commission for compensation. Berber-language instruction was introduced in primary schools, and a new family law (Mudawana) was enacted in 2004, addressing some women's rights demands. Morocco faced a crisis with Spain over the island of Perejil/Toura in 2002\. In May 2003, Islamist suicide bombers struck Casablanca, leading to a crackdown on extremists. The US designated Morocco a major non-NATO ally in recognition of its counter-terrorism efforts. Free trade agreements were signed with the US and EU.

In February 2011, protests calling for political reform and a new constitution emerged during the "Arab Spring." A bombing in Marrakesh occurred in April 2011\. A constitutional referendum was approved in July 2011, recognizing Amazigh as an official language. The **Hirak Rif Movement** began in October 2016 following the death of a fish seller. In 2017, Morocco rejoined the African Union. The COVID-19 pandemic began in March 2020\. In December 2020, the United States recognized Morocco's claims over Western Sahara as part of the **Israel‚ÄìMorocco normalization agreement**. Diplomatic crises with Spain and Algeria occurred in 2021\. The Amazigh New Year was declared an official national holiday in May 2023\. A 6.8 magnitude earthquake struck Morocco in September 2023, causing significant loss of life and injury.

This document explores the history, culture, and science in Morocco from the 11th to the 14th centuries, particularly focusing on the role of the Almoravids and Almohads, and the scientific contributions in mathematics, astronomy, and geography. It highlights Morocco's significant but often overlooked role in elevating the human condition and serving as a crucial route for the exchange of ideas and people between East and West, especially after Sicily became unsafe for Muslim travelers in the late 11th century.

One illustration of Morocco's transit role is the spread of paper manufacturing. Originating in China and brought to the Islamic lands, paper production moved from Baghdad to Syria, then Palestine, reaching Egypt around 850 CE, Morocco by the early 10th century, and Spain by 950 CE. From Spain and Sicily, paper-making then spread to Italy and the rest of Europe.

There was a close relationship between Moroccan territories and Al-Andalus (Muslim Spain). Maghribi students, for instance, considered a stay in Cordova, Murcia, or Valencia necessary to complete their studies until the 13th century. Economic links were also strong, with Morocco serving as a source of raw materials and a market for finished goods from Al-Andalus. Under the Almoravid and Almohad dynasties, which unified Islamic Spain with the Maghrib, Moroccan cities like Marrakech and Fes became extensions of the Andalusi urban economy, facilitating free movement of merchants and goods. Jewish families, heavily involved in Muslim trade, often had homes on both sides of the Strait of Gibraltar to ease commercial arrangements.

Morocco's main contribution to the history and geopolitics of the Islamic western region was the rise of two powerful Muslim forces: the Almoravid and Almohad dynasties. These dynasties are often negatively portrayed in historical literature, such as in the film *El-Cid*, where Almoravids are depicted as evil and cruel, contrasting with a romanticized El-Cid, who was in reality a mercenary. The Almohads, too, are frequently described as fanatical and orthodox barbarians who harmed Muslim civilization.

The **Almoravids** intervened in Spanish affairs in 1086, a period when Muslim Spain was fragmented into feuding "Taifas de Reyes" (small kingdoms), exploited by Christian neighbors for the "Reconquista". The taking of Barbastro by combined Christian forces in 1065 marked a significant turning point. Facing intensified threats, the Taifa rulers, under pressure from their populations, appealed to the Almoravids for help. In 1086, Almoravid tribesmen from southern Morocco crossed into Spain and inflicted a decisive defeat on Christian forces at Zallaqa near Badajoz, intimidating them with their continuous drumming and swift maneuvers. Alfonso VI of Castile barely escaped, with thousands of his best knights perishing.

Initially, the Almoravids' puritanical faith was not welcomed by the more liberal Andalusians, and Yusuf Ibn Tashfin, their ruler, was even plotted against. However, after two previous interventions, in 1090, Ibn Tashfin returned, removed the Taifa rulers, and established Almoravid rule over all of Muslim Spain. Despite their crucial role, the Almoravids suffered from historical prejudices, with contemporary historians like Al-Bakri wrongly regarding them as "savages" and their conquest as a "bloody holy war waged by fanatical disciples of a strange religion".

In reality, the Almoravids conquered Morocco patiently, and their establishment in Marrakech and rapid expansion demonstrated a considerable degree of cultural evolution and assimilation. They secured prosperity for the region by drawing on African goldmines for coinage and were the first to unify Spain and Northwest Africa, which had profound economic and cultural repercussions. More importantly, the Almoravid intervention kept Spain in Muslim hands for another half-century (until their fall in 1147\) and halted the Castilian advance, allowing Muslim civilization to prosper for an extended period.

The **Almohads** continued this legacy, extending Muslim political survival in Al-Andalus and the prosperity of Muslim civilization until nearly the mid-13th century. Eminent scholars and thinkers, such as the herbalists Al-Ghafiqi and Ibn al-Baytar, the traveler Ibn Jubayr, the philosopher Ibn Rushd, and the astronomer Jabir Ibn Aflah, lived under Almoravid and Almohad rule.

After Ibn Tashfin‚Äôs death, the Almoravid kingdom succumbed to corruption and anarchy, with their once-hardy warriors losing their martial habits to a life of ease and luxury. This led to renewed chaos and infighting in Muslim Spain, which fragmented again into warring city-states. Christian forces, under King Alfonso I of Aragon, resumed their assaults, capturing towns and cities.

The Almohads, another Berber dynasty from deeper into Morocco, crossed into Spain and once again saved Muslim Spain, defeating Castilian armies. In 1196, the Almohad ruler Abu Yusuf Ya‚Äôqub inflicted a crushing defeat on Alfonso VIII of Castile at Alarcos. Abu Yusuf was notably generous in victory, freeing twenty thousand Christian prisoners without ransom. Though he was forced to cut short his operations in Spain due to a rebellion in Africa, his actions secured Spain and North Africa for Muslim rule for a while longer.

Abu Ya‚Äôqub, the father of Abu Yusuf Ya'qub al-Mansur, brought peace and security and improved irrigation systems and embellished cities with fine buildings in both Spain and North-West Africa. He made Seville his capital in 1170-1171, restoring the Alcazar and building the main mosque, whose minaret is now known as the Giralda, which also served as an observatory.

In Morocco, Almohad rule was a period of great prosperity and brilliance of learning. They built the Marrakech Kutubiya Mosque, which accommodated 25,000 people and was famed for its extensive collection of books, manuscripts, libraries, and bookshops. Abu Ya‚Äôqub was a great collector of books and founded a public library in Marrakech, managed by the most erudite scholars. Some books in the library were considered as prized as precious metals. During the Crusades, the Almohads dispatched 180 vessels to aid Muslims in the East. Almohad sovereigns encouraged the construction of schools and libraries, sponsored scholars, and even attended their funerals. Illustrious physicians, philosophers, and scholars such as Ibn Rushd, Ibn Tufayl, and Ibn Zuhr thrived under the Almohad court. Libraries, including private collections, flourished, with some requiring five camel loads to transport.

However, Almohad rule eventually met the same fate as its predecessors. Abu Yusuf Al-Mansur was succeeded by his son Al-Nasir (1199-1214), who was indifferent to science and religion and focused on pleasure. At the decisive Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa in 1212, Al-Nasir's superior but disunited army was crushed by a smaller, unified Spanish force. This marked the beginning of the end for Muslim Spain, with Cordova (1236), Valencia (1238), and Seville (1248) falling, leaving only Granada until 1492\. Without a strong North African army to intervene, the Spanish and Portuguese began thrusting into North Africa in the 15th century, only to be met by the rising power of the Ottomans.

Morocco was a significant center of **mathematical studies**. Two famous scholars, Al-Murrakushi and Ibn al-Banna, contributed to arithmetic, astronomy, geometry, algebra, chemistry, and history. While Al-Murrakushi's career flourished in Egypt, his contributions reflect his Moroccan education. Ibn al-Banna, a fully Moroccan scientist, led an original scientific school in the late medieval Islamic west.

**Abd al-Wahid Al-Murrakushi**, born in Marrakech in 1185, studied in Marrakech, Fes, and Spain before moving to Egypt in 1217\. In 1224, he completed a history of the Almohad dynasty, *Kitab al-mu‚Äôjib fi talkhis akhbar ahl al-Maghrib*. His main scientific work is *Jami‚Äô al-Mabadi‚Äô wa-‚Äòl-ghayat fi ‚Äòilm al-miqat* (The compendium of principles and results in the science of timekeeping), completed around 1229-1230. This work is a compilation of practical knowledge on astronomical instruments, trigonometry, and gnomonics. Sarton praised it as the most elaborate trigonometrical treatise of the Western caliphate and the best medieval treatise on practical astronomy and graphical methods, including studies of various sundial surfaces for every latitude. Al-Murrakushi included tables of sines, versed sines, and arc sines, and showed familiarity with the works of prominent Islamic mathematicians and astronomers. His work demonstrates that advanced trigonometry existed centuries before it was believed to have developed in 14th-century Oxford and France.

**Ibn al-Banna**, whose full name was Abu ‚Äòl-Abbas Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Uthman al-Azdi, was born in Marrakech in 1256 and spent most of his life in Morocco. He lived and taught in Fes, which became the capital of the Merinids after the fall of the Almohads. At the university in Fes, he taught arithmetic, algebra, geometry, and astronomy. Ibn al-Banna was highly prolific, with over 100 titles across disciplines like linguistics, rhetoric, astrology, grammar, logic, mathematics, and astronomy. Honored by the Merinids, he even published *Tanbih al-albab*, a book containing mathematical solutions to everyday problems such as medicament composition, irrigation canal calculations, and detecting measurement frauds. In mathematics, he is credited with being the first to consider a fraction as a ratio and to use the term "almanac". His works *Talkhis a‚Äômal al-hisab* and its commentary *Raf‚Äô al-Hijab* introduced mathematical notation, leading some to believe he was among the first in Islam to develop algebraic symbolism.

Morocco also produced several renowned **geographers and travelers**, including Al-Idrisi and Ibn Battuta, both from northern Morocco.

**Muhammad Al-Idrisi** was born in Ceuta (Morocco) in 1099-1100 CE and studied in Cordoba, though he worked at the Norman court of Palermo. He traveled extensively through Asia Minor, Morocco, Spain, southern France, and even visited England. His descriptions of most of Western Europe and the Balkans were lively and accurate, although for other regions, he relied on existing writings. Al-Idrisi was an original geographer, creatively using a system of cylindrical projection of the Earth‚Äôs surface centuries before Gerard Mercator. His map also accurately placed the sources of the Nile in the equatorial highlands of Africa, a discovery often attributed to the 19th century. His major work, *Al-Kitab al-Rujari* (Roger‚Äôs book), also known as *Nuzhat al-mushtaq fi ikhtiraq al-afaq* (Pleasures of Men and Delights of Souls), was a large geographical encyclopedia that took him fifteen years to complete. This work, accompanied by a silver planisphere (now lost), is considered a "great monument of Arabic and Muslim geography".

**Ibn Battuta**, born in Tangier on February 24, 1304, embarked on his pilgrimage to Mecca in 1325 and returned to Morocco nearly 25 years later in 1349\. He then traveled to Spain, and extensively through the Mali Madinka state, including Timbuktu and Gao, returning to Morocco in 1354\. He dictated the story of his travels, known as *Rihla*, to Ibn Juzayy‚Äô, a scholar at the Merinid court in Fes. His *Rihla* provides unique information on 14th-century India, the Maldives, southern Russia, and Black Africa, detailing his experiences and observations. He described the Nile's unique rise during hot weather and the intensive cultivation along its banks. He noted the Turks' strict laws against theft and the exceptional skills of the Chinese, including their ability to draw accurate portraits of strangers, which amazed him.

Another notable geographer from the Islamic west, **Ali ibn Musa Ibn Sa‚Äôid al-Maghribi** (1213-1286), grew up in Marrakesh and was an indefatigable traveler. He authored *Kitab bast al-ardh* in 1250 and *Kitab al-jagrfiya*, which incorporated his extensive travels across the Muslim world, the Indian Ocean shores, and parts of northern Europe, including Iceland. His *Rayat al-mubarrizin wa-ghayat al-mumayyizin* (Banners of the Champions), written in 1243, is a well-known anthology of poetry. Ibn Sa‚Äôid's work included new information not found in Al-Idrisi, such as details about the Senegal River.

**Marrakech** was founded around 1070 by the Almoravids as their army headquarters and became the Almohad capital in 1147\. It attracted Maghribi scholars and some from Spain, becoming a center for power and knowledge. Ibn Rushd conducted astronomical observations there in 1153 and was associated with the Almohad court. The city was famous for the Kutubiya Mosque, known for its extensive libraries and bookshops, where numerous librarians and traders facilitated the copying and exchange of manuscripts. Marrakech also had a strong tradition of astrolabe constructors. Historians like Abu Bakr al-Sanhadji, who wrote extensively on the Almohads, flourished in Marrakech. Al-Murrakushi also penned his *Kitab al-Mu‚Äôjib* in Marrakech. The city's historical attractions include the walled Agdal Gardens, dating from Almohad times and irrigated by subterranean canals. One of the greatest accomplishments of Almohad rule was the Marrakech hospital, the Bimaristan of Amir al-Muminin al-Mansur abu Yusuf, which was described as unparalleled in the world for its extensive facilities, beautiful decorations, gardens, flowing water, and provision of comprehensive care for both rich and poor patients.

**Fes** is regarded as the spiritual capital of Morocco, renowned for its beauty and the expertise of Muslim art. Founded in the early 9th century by the Idrisids, Fes quickly grew, attracting people of diverse origins including Arabs, Berbers, Jews, and Andalusians. It became a strong center of scientific tradition and, under the Merinids, the political capital, attracting students from across the country to colleges and madrassas around the Qarawiyyin mosque.

The **Qarawiyyin mosque and university**, the city‚Äôs greatest intellectual claim, was first built in 859 CE by Fatima bint Muhammad al-Fihri. It is believed to have developed into a university during the Almoravid reign and became the principal center of higher learning in Morocco. Its body of scholars had a great impact on learning across the Mediterranean and Europe, with notable figures like Ibn Khaldun, Ibn al-Khatib, Al-Bitruji, and Leo Africanus studying and teaching there. The Qarawiyyin offered courses in grammar, rhetoric, logic, elements of mathematics, astronomy, and possibly history, geography, and chemistry. A contemporary chronicler highlighted Fes's richness and splendor during the Almohad reign, detailing its numerous mosques, zawiyas, public baths, mills, dwelling houses, warehouses, hotels, shops, workshops, tanneries, dye works, ovens, and notably, 400 paper-making shops.

Fes also housed remarkable **clocks**. One water-clock, made in 1286/87 by Ibn al-Habbak al-Tilimsani and restored in 1346-48, was located in the minaret of the Qarawiyyin Mosque. This clock, which incorporated an astrolabic rete that rotated once every 24 hours, imitated the apparent daily rotation of the heavens and dropped metal balls every hour. Another water-clock was constructed in the 14th century next to the Abu ‚ÄòInaniyya Madrasa by Abu ‚Äòl-Hasan Ibn Ali Ahmad al-Tilimsani, a *muwaqqit* (time keeper). This weight-powered water clock, inaugurated on May 6, 1357, had 12 windows and platforms with brass bowls, into which metal balls would fall hourly, chiming the time. The Abu ‚ÄòInaniyya Madrasa itself, founded by the Marinid Sultan Abu Inan Faris, is a marvel of Marinid architecture and served as both an educational institute and a congregational mosque. These Fes clocks are the sole surviving Islamic examples of such astrolabic clocks, with similar designs described by Al-Jazari in the early 13th century.

Despite Morocco's rich heritage and significant contributions to Muslim civilization, few studies are devoted to it in English, particularly in electronic form. The intense academic interest in Islamic Spain often overshadows Morocco's intimately connected history and influence. There is a recognized need to present Morocco's scientific tradition, historical events, architecture, arts, education, mechanics, and engineering to an international audience.

Morocco was the **last North African country to establish a Pasteur Institute**, with institutes opening nearly two decades later than in Tunisia and Algeria. This delayed and duplicated establishment, with the first institute in Tangier in 1913 and a second in Casablanca in 1932, reflected significant challenges for Pastorians seeking a solid foothold in the country since the late 19th century. These issues stemmed from several factors, including a pre-existing Spanish-Moroccan bacteriological tradition, uncoordinated initiatives from Paris and Algiers, and the enduring solidity of the previously independent Moroccan state, which disrupted the French takeover. The failure of France to impose its colonial rule over the entire country, symbolized by Tangier's international regime, directly led to the need for a second center in Casablanca.

Before French Pastorianism gained traction, a modest bacteriological tradition had already taken root in Morocco, particularly in Tangier, driven by Sultan Hassan I's modernization efforts and Spanish imperial interests. From the mid-1880s, Spain sought to "regenerate Morocco" through public health initiatives. The International Sanitary Council of Tangier, established in 1840, was reformed and developed. In 1888, Doctor Severo Cenarro led the creation of a **Hygiene Commission**, promoting urban hygiene in Tangier and other port towns. Concurrently, Felipe √ìvilo opened a **School of Medicine in Tangier in 1886**, which became a training center for Moroccan army doctors. These institutions fostered early bacteriological efforts, including research, vaccine/serum production, and disinfection, ensuring some Moroccan participation and benefit. For instance, √ìvilo isolated the **Koch bacillus** in Tangier in September 1895 during a cholera epidemic. The Hygiene Commission also established a deposit of "cowpox lymph" by 1897 and anti-diphtheritic serum by 1898, which doctors Ram√≥n Fiol and Samuel Mobily G√ºitta (a Moroccan-born, Spanish-naturalized Jew) successfully used. Disinfection procedures were also set up at the Mogador Island lazaretto by early 1896\.

When French bacteriology, associated with Louis Pasteur, arrived in Morocco in the mid-1890s as part of the Third Republic‚Äôs "civilizing mission," it encountered this existing local context. This meant Pastorianism, which typically thrived within a colonial health scheme like those in Algeria or Tonkin, was initially **forced to operate outside the local administration** in Morocco, constrained by the French Legation and Ministry of Foreign Affairs. Initiatives originated from the distant Pasteur Institute of Paris (PIP) and Algiers (PIA), leading to a split between metropolitan and Algerian-oriented projects. These factors hindered Pastorian efforts, with competition often yielding unfavorable results against the established Spanish-Moroccan developments. For example, the PIP failed to monopolize the supply of anti-diphtheritic serum to the Hygiene Commission, which established its own local reserve by 1898\. Furthermore, in 1895, Dr. Henri Souli√©, co-founder of the PIA, was sent to investigate a cholera epidemic but his findings, which aimed to absolve a French steamer, were discredited by Dr. Cenarro and publicly criticized by √ìvilo.

After Spain's influence waned following the loss of Cuba and the Philippines in 1898, the situation shifted. Although Pastorians could not immediately deploy a unified colonial program, they gradually displaced Spanish doctors from prominent positions. For instance, French doctors, like Lucien Raynaud, health inspector of Algiers, were appointed to direct the pilgrims‚Äô quarantine at Mogador Island from 1899, replacing the ousted Dr. Joaqu√≠n Cort√©s. By 1905, the PIP took over this task, with Dr. Maire appointed quarantine director until 1911\. Pastorian research also progressed, notably with Dr. Henri Foley, who, appointed by General Lyautey, established a "Saharan laboratory" in Beni-Ounif near Morocco‚Äôs Figuig oases in 1906\. Here, Foley conducted pioneering research on recurrent fever and tuberculosis affecting Eastern Moroccan populations, in collaboration with Edmond Sergent. His laboratory became an official branch of the PIA in 1909\.

The **creation of the Pasteur Institute of Tangier (PIT)** was greatly advanced by the intensification of French intervention in Morocco in the early 20th century. Secret agreements with Great Britain and Spain in 1902 and 1904 solidified French hegemony, further formalized by the Algeciras Conference of 1906\. By 1912, French troops had occupied key imperial cities, leading to the signing of a Protectorate Treaty in March 1912 and Lyautey‚Äôs appointment as R√©sident G√©n√©ral. This period spurred Pastorian initiatives, with Eug√®ne Regnault, head of the Tangier Legation, advocating for a "model establishment" to study hygiene and secure French influence. The PIT was envisioned to unify Pastorian efforts and form the core of a new French-made health administration, under the authority of the French Legation and directed by an all-French commission. The primary motivation for its creation was the **growing threat of plague**, which escalated into a massive epidemic in the Dukkala region by 1911\. Regnault secured land and funding from the PIP and the French Home Office (Fonds des jeux), and architect Henri Saladin, with input from √âmile Roux and Albert Calmette of the PIP, began construction in late 1910 or early 1911\. **Paul Remlinger**, an army bacteriologist turned Pastorian, was appointed director in July 1911 and immediately began studying the plague epidemic on the ground. The PIT started operations before construction was complete, replacing the French Hospital as a supplier of sera and opening its rabies vaccine section on May 25, 1913\. The institute was officially inaugurated on **July 14, 1913**, costing 300,000 francs for construction and equipment.

However, Remlinger immediately recognized a critical flaw: the **partition of Morocco into three zones**‚Äîa French Protectorate, a Spanish Protectorate, and an international Tangier‚Äîwhich had not been foreseen. This rendered the PIT legally trapped beyond French control and geographically isolated from French Morocco, undermining its intended role as a national institution. He stated that if this division had been known, Tangier would not have been chosen for the institute. Consequently, the PIT, despite being officially named *Institut Pasteur du Maroc* in 1914, was a purely French institution, its contract signed with the Ministry of Foreign Affairs, not the French Protectorate. Its operations were supervised by a Paris-based *Comit√© de contr√¥le*, and the continued existence of Tangier's Sanitary Council and Hygiene Commission created obstacles to its autonomous development.

The new political landscape also prevented the PIT from subsuming the activities of Algerian-based Pastorians. Lyautey continued to support Foley's research on the Algerian-Moroccan border and sought to establish a new Pasteur institute in Rabat, the Protectorate's nascent capital. By 1913, Roux agreed to the concept of a "Pasteur Institute of Rabat". Lyautey aimed to integrate this institute within the Protectorate's health administration as the "keystone of a well-conceived Assistance M√©dicale Indig√®ne". Although World War I brought a virtual shutdown to the PIT, with Remlinger and other army doctors mobilized, Lyautey created an *Institut Antirabique* (1915) and an *Institut Vaccinog√®ne* (1913) in Rabat, which also included a *Laboratoire de Bact√©riologie*. These centers, largely products of the army's bacteriological tradition, were eventually brought under a unified administration in 1916\. Jules Colombani, promoted as the strongman of the Protectorate's public health, envisioned creating a "true Pasteur Institute in Casablanca" to concentrate treatment and research against epidemic diseases.

In the mid-1920s, a convergence of these disparate initiatives seemed possible. Remlinger worked to build ties between the PIT and the French Protectorate health administration, securing increased subventions and an assistant, Jacques Bailly. Land was purchased in Casablanca for the construction of a new *Institut Pasteur du Maroc*, planned to be adjacent to new civil, military, and native hospitals. This coincided with French ambitions to extend control over international Tangier and the Spanish Protectorate, including demands for Tangier's annexation at the Versailles Peace Conference. The Rif War (1921‚Äì1926) further destabilized the Spanish Protectorate, allowing France to advance its "pacification" efforts. However, Abdelkrim's offensive ultimately led to Lyautey's dismissal in July 1925 and the abandonment of French pan-Moroccan policies.

Remlinger's plans for a unified Moroccan Pasteur Institute then collapsed. He lamented the shift in policy, realizing that Colombani and his assistant, Dr. Maurice Gaud, intended to create a state-controlled Pasteur institute in Casablanca, largely independent of the PIP, serving primarily as a "useful laboratory" for vaccine production rather than a "big research institute". This vision materialized in the **Institut d‚ÄôHygi√®ne du Maroc**, inaugurated in Rabat on **December 30, 1930**. This new institution, housed in a costly building designed by Pierre Bousquet and equipped with "first class" German instruments, was organized into numerous departments (Bacteriology, Pathological Anatomy, Parasitology, Malaria, etc.) and offered training courses. Despite its impressive facilities, it remained subordinate to the civil health administration, with Pastorians having no control.

The creation of the Rabat institute cemented the split of Pastorian projects in Morocco. The **Pasteur Institute of Casablanca (PIC)** was finally opened in **June 1932**, but largely as a subsidiary product of non-Moroccan North African Pastorianism. Edmond Sergent from the PIA drafted its contract, designed its building, and supervised its construction, with Georges Blanc, a disciple of the Tunis institute director Charles Nicolle, appointed as director. Though located in the French Protectorate, the PIC lacked real influence in health policies, primarily supplying BCG and smallpox vaccines and operating as an anti-rabies vaccination service. Its research program reflected ongoing lines from Algiers and Tunis rather than specific Moroccan pathologies.

Meanwhile, the Tangier Institute continued its "unstoppable decline". By April 1956, months before Morocco regained independence, a visiting researcher, Robert Neel, found the institute in a severe state of decay, its equipment outdated and buildings dilapidated. Paul Remlinger, the PIT's only director, remained in Tangier until his death in 1964, embodying France's diminishing influence in the city. The troubled early histories of both the Tangier and Casablanca Pasteur Institutes highlight that France's colonial hegemony in Morocco was less pervasive than often believed, forcing it to contend with a Spanish Protectorate and an international Tangier. Pastorianism, which required a purely French colonial environment to flourish, found this condition unfulfilled in Morocco, leading to its complex and fragmented development.

The Archives du Maroc, Morocco's new national archives, opened in 2013 in Rabat in a building previously constructed by the French to house the General Library. Its inauguration two years prior was hailed by the head of the Moroccan National Council for Human Rights as a vital institution for developing a "rational and pluralistic history". This institution's creation was mandated by law, following a strong recommendation from the **Truth and Reconciliation Commission (TRC)**, which operated from 2004 to 2005\.

The TRC investigated gross human rights violations, including forced disappearances, arbitrary detention, torture, sexual abuse, and coerced exile, that occurred between 1956 and the end of 1999 under the reign of King Hassan II. A significant impediment to the TRC's work was the "deplorable state of national archives". The lack of formal, organized archives, reliable records, and academic studies, coupled with limited access to written records from state ministries and police files, severely hampered its research and ability to collect evidence against perpetrators. This precarious documentation situation was a primary reason the TRC opted for a reconciliation framework rather than pursuing criminal charges. Consequently, the Committee often had to rely on **alternative forms of documentation**, such as prison memoirs, autobiographies, fictional texts, and even theatrical plays, to reconstruct events. The frustration among Committee members due to "archival silences," uncooperative officials, and "fragile" oral testimonies was evident. The Commission also faced challenges with a non-regulated legal framework and limited access to official records, including military archives, due to hesitations, slow delivery, or the short duration of its mandate.

The TRC prescribed the creation of a national archives to house its own records, viewing them as crucial for understanding its processes and for citizens to access a "hitherto hidden" part of the country's memory. It emphasized that such an institution was the "only institution that can ensure the protection and preservation of all national archival heritages". Slyomovics notes that the archives produced by the TRC's investigations are among its most significant contributions, providing "raw material for writing the history of Morocco" and narratives previously "out of the reach of many or forbidden and taboo". The Committee strongly recommended the opening of a formal National Archives, alongside other "institutional initiatives" like a new Master's degree in contemporary Moroccan history.
