The 2011 inauguration of the Archives du Maroc was not the country's first attempt to establish a national archives; historically, the creation of archives and training of historians in Morocco have been "flawed processes". During the colonial era, French forces, after decades of ethnographic documentation, sought a centralized location for their records. This "Moroccan colonial archive," as termed by Burke, aimed to create an "objective record" of Moroccan life, customs, and laws, which ultimately served to justify the French protectorate. The modern Moroccan National Library (BNRM) evolved from earlier iterations, including the General Library of the Protectorate, conceived by French General Lyautey in 1919 as a "modern public library". The French deliberately used the term "general" instead of "national" to avoid "political sensitivities" related to colonialism. This general library incorporated collections from colonial research initiatives like the Institute of Advanced Moroccan Studies (IHEM) and private collections acquired or confiscated by the French. French legislation in 1926 and 1954 addressed state records management, but noncompliance was common.

Following independence in 1956, Moroccan intellectuals sought to redefine their national identity and history, moving away from colonial narratives. Despite the ruling elite often being historians and intellectuals, the establishment of a post-colonial national archives was significantly delayed, opening only in 2013\. This delay raises questions, especially since establishing a national archives was seen as a marker of a modern nation and postcolonial state development. One explanation is that Moroccan nationalists, rejecting the narratives of the colonial archive, focused instead on locating and collecting indigenous records to construct new post-colonial narratives. The existence of a French-established "national" library and archives from the 1920s might have also reduced the perceived urgency for another formal institution.

In this context, the **Hassan II Prize for Manuscripts and Archival Documents** was established in 1969 by the first post-colonial Ministry of Culture. This prize aimed to locate and preserve records held privately, incentivizing their disclosure through public recognition and cash rewards, thereby valorizing them as national documentary heritage. The Prize was an initiative of the IstiqlƒÅl Party, led by Arab nationalists, and represented a "top-down custodial" attempt to "decolonise archives 'at the level of the source'". This program was part of a broader effort during a "particular historical moment when Morocco as a young independent nation was working to redefine what it meant to be Moroccan". It served as an **alternative post-colonial national archives**, supporting a master narrative of an Arab-Islamic identity for the country, in contrast to European colonial histories. Records submitted to the Prize, such as notarial documents, judicial advice, and learned people's notebooks, supported the narrative of an erudite nation connected to Andalusia and the Arab East. The Prize has received over 35,000 submissions, many of which have been microfilmed or digitized and stored at the National Library of Morocco (BNRM), with copies also kept at the new Archives du Maroc since 2015\. This focus on indigenous documents was reflected in the early 1970s, when the General Library's European language Moroccana became outdated, while Arabic materials were kept current.

The state of Moroccan documentary heritage was a significant concern at the inaugural 1966 colloquium of the Moroccan Historical Society, where a "national archives service" was deemed necessary for centralizing documents and training archivists. That same year, the Moroccan government invited **UNESCO** to appraise its archives and provide recommendations. In 1968, Yves Perotin of UNESCO visited Morocco, similar to a previous evaluation he conducted in Algeria. Over 82 days, Perotin surveyed the archival situation, noting the existence of "considerable importance" in private archival collections and observing "amicable steps" (likely referring to the nascent Hassan II Prize) to value them. His report provided a snapshot of post-colonial Moroccan archival collections, highlighting how some important local and regional records were jeopardized by restructuring during the final years of colonization and early independence. Perotin proposed modern reforms, including short-term preservation and archival training abroad, and long-term restructuring, emphasizing the development of a national team of specialists. Notably, the press conference announcing the Hassan II Prize occurred as Perotin was concluding his visit in February 1969\.

The Archives du Maroc, despite its late establishment, opened primarily with a **colonial fonds**, comprising records from the French colonial period (1912-1956). This Fonds Protectorat contains "management documents" related to the colonial economy, public works, and administrative or legislative matters that were not repatriated to France. The distinction between "management" and "sovereignty" records, conceived by French archivists, is considered arbitrary. The archives also hold files from the Direction des Affaires Indig√®nes and publications from other colonial projects, making it a "storehouse of the Moroccan colonial archive". This colonial archive, while a "formidable intellectual achievement," was "marked by the deforming lens of Orientalism" and used to justify the protectorate. The new national archives has been criticized for "seemingly accepting the colonial archive, its documentation methods, and the records it produced unquestionably," perpetuating colonial categories and units of analysis. This is exemplified by an exhibit on Henri de Castries, a French military officer and historian whose work, *The Unedited Sources on the History of Morocco*, aggregated documents from European libraries. While celebrated by some for his contributions to Moroccan history, Moroccan historian Germain Ayache criticized his reliance on European archives rather than actual Moroccan sources. Despite these issues, the opening of the Archives du Maroc, even with its colonial fonds, represents a breakthrough given that research on the colonial period was "off limits" until the late 1990s. These colonial documents, while revealing, must be critically examined to resist "ingesting it whole" without contemplation, as they provide clues that may extend beyond their architects' intentions. The "persistent significance of colonial-era representations" complicates the post-colonial struggle to define collective identities in Morocco.

Questions persist regarding the state and accessibility of post-colonial government records. The TRC found that a "part of the public and private archives was degraded" and that there was an "absence of a national modern institutional framework" for managing archives. While the new national archives aims to become a "true national archive" encompassing records from all state agencies, this is challenging due to a cultural lack of "archival responsibility" and a "disdain for the ‚Äòpaper trail‚Äô". Morocco is undergoing "archival and historical memory recovery," a process advanced by the TRC's mandate. However, the new national archives faces limitations due to the "unbalanced weight of colonial era records" and an inadequate budget, staff capacity, or physical space. The decades-long delay in its opening can be attributed to a lack of political will and the efforts of nationalist and post-colonial historians who sought to radically reshape historical narratives by prioritizing indigenous documents, as exemplified by the Hassan II Prize. Despite the current challenges, there is hope that pre-colonial and post-colonial collections will eventually move beyond the shadow of the colonial fonds to accurately represent the nation. Jama√¢ Baida, the head of the Archives du Maroc, views the new institution as a "proof of concept" to raise public awareness about the importance of archives for healthy societies.

Morocco in the 16th century was a complex and fractured entity, characterized by the waning power of the **Wattasid dynasty**, largely confined to Fez, the powerful **Saadian empire** rising from the Sous region, and a significant **Portuguese presence on the Atlantic coast**. This tripartite division of power defines the historical context of the time. Amidst this, figures like Abdelkader l'Gharnati, a Moorish scholar who fled Granada in 1502, represent the influx of Andalusi refugees into Morocco following the fall of Granada in 1492 and the Spanish Inquisition. The period also saw Moroccan resistance against European influence. Challenges in Moroccan historiography, including distortions during the French occupation regarding women and Islamic aspects, and the importance of alternative national archives valuing local knowledge, provide nuance to understanding this era.

The city of **Mazagan** (modern-day El Jadida), located in the **Doukkala plains**, was a critical setting during this period. It was a **formidable, star-shaped Renaissance military fortress** built by an international team of engineers. Its **Manueline architectural style**, evident in civil and religious buildings like the Church of the Assumption and the famous subterranean cistern, provided distinct visual elements. Mazagan was perceived as a "self-contained, claustrophobic society, a tiny European island on a hostile African coast". Beyond Mazagan, other Doukkala cities like Azemmour showcased the Saadian "renaissance" style, emphasizing **intricate interior decoration, *zellij* tilework, carved stucco, cedarwood, *muqarnas*, and horseshoe arches**. Rural Doukkala featured diverse settlements, including traditional dry stone *Tazotas*, courtyard houses, and semi-nomadic tents, with the region known as an agricultural heartland growing wheat, olives, and sugar cane. Fez also held historical significance, notably with the **Al-Qarawiyyin mosque**.

The cultural immersion of 16th-century Morocco was rich in architectural artistry, including the use of **imported Italian Carrara marble** for elite Saadian buildings, intricately carved and painted **cedarwood**, fine **stucco (*geps*)**, and **vibrant mosaic tilework (*zellij*)**. Book arts from the Marinid and Wattasid periods (c. 1250-1550) featured **parchment and paper**‚Äîwith Fez being a major center for paper mills‚Äîalong with Maghrebi calligraphy and distinct **Morocco leather bindings with geometric interlace patterns**, often incorporating eight-pointed stars and central medallions. Music genres such as **Malhun and Tarab al-Andalusi**, as well as **Chaabi and Aita**, were prominent. The **Aicha Kandicha legend** and Sufi practices provided unique folklore elements. Elite fashion included the **kaftan, takchita, and mansouria**, made from luxurious fabrics like silk brocade, velvet, or imported "Brown Blues" woolen cloth, while the traditional *haik* represented common attire. Daily life revolved around local markets (*souqs*) with specialized artisan guilds, the importance of trans-Saharan trade routes for **gold and salt**, and agricultural products like sugar cane.

Military and conflict dynamics were central to 16th-century Morocco. The **Portuguese garrison's evolution in arms and armor** included Renaissance-era pikemen and arquebusiers, with their military doctrine involving siegecraft, fortress defense, and asymmetrical warfare tactics known as *cavalgadas* and *salto*. The Saadian army, in contrast, employed a mix of **traditional mobile cavalry** and **professional infantry armed with modern arquebuses and Turkish weaponry**, demonstrating a "reciprocal evolution" where both sides adapted to each other's strengths. Portuguese fleets were crucial for sustaining Mazagan, and corsair vessels like **galiots** and **fustas** were used for raiding, suggesting potential naval conflicts.

Morocco's identity has deep roots, with human habitation spanning the Lower Paleolithic period, evidenced by the oldest known *Homo sapiens* fossils (300,000 years old) at Jebel Irhoud. Early cultural expressions include 82,000-year-old perforated seashell beads found in Taforalt, the earliest known personal adornment. The indigenous **Amazigh communities** have a sophisticated visual language, notably in tattooing (*oucham*, *tichrad*, or *tagezzayt*), a non-literate system of communication, cultural memory, and expression of identity and spirituality. Amazigh tattoo motifs are linked to Neolithic rock art from the Capsian period (7500‚Äì4000 BC), suggesting an ancestral connection to the Libyco-Berber alphabet, Tifinagh. Moroccan identity is a tapestry of Arab, Amazigh, and European influences, with historical studies, such as Abdallah Laroui's *History of the Maghrib*, emphasizing the importance of understanding long-term continuities beyond dynastic or conqueror-centric narratives.

The evolution of the Moroccan state is marked by successive dynasties and external pressures. After the Muslim conquest in the early 8th century, the **Berber Revolt of 740 CE** led to Morocco breaking away from the Umayyad Caliphate. The **Barghawata**, a Masmuda Berber confederation, established an independent state (744-1058 CE) in Tamesna, near the Atlantic coast. Their distinct, syncretic religion, including a Berber Quran and a God named *Yakouch*, was viewed as heretical, leading to "holy war against them". Despite this, their military aptitude and strategic position allowed them to resist for three centuries before being eliminated by the Almohads in 1149\. The **Idrisid dynasty** (789-974), founded by Idris I (an Arab who married a Berber and lived among the Ourabas), established the first Moroccan state. Fez, founded by Idris II, became their capital and a significant religious center.

Later, the **Almoravids** (c. 1060-1147) and **Almohads** (c. 1121-1269) formed successive Berber empires that reunified Morocco and projected power into Al-Andalus. The Almoravids intervened in Spain in 1086, defeating Christian forces at Zallaqa and eventually establishing rule over Muslim Spain, halting the Castilian advance and allowing Muslim civilization to prosper \[History, Culture and Science in Morocco: 11th-14th Centuries \- Muslim Heritage\]. They are sometimes historically misrepresented as "savages" despite their significant cultural evolution and unification of Spain and Northwest Africa \[History, Culture and Science in Morocco: 11th-14th Centuries \- Muslim Heritage\]. The Almohads, founded by Ibn Tumart, a Masmuda Berber scholar and self-proclaimed Mahdi, preached "absolute divine unity (*Tawhid*)" and launched a holy war against the Almoravids. Their empire became "the largest ever to be ruled from Morocco," extending across the Maghreb and Muslim al-Andalus. They were known for cultural achievements, including the construction of the Kutubiya Mosque in Marrakech and the Giralda in Seville (which also served as an observatory), and fostering scholars like Ibn Rushd \[History, Culture and Science in Morocco: 11th-14th Centuries \- Muslim Heritage\]. However, Almohad rule declined after the decisive Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa in 1212, which marked "the beginning of the end of Muslim Spain" \[9, History, Culture and Science in Morocco: 11th-14th Centuries \- Muslim Heritage\].

The **Marinid dynasty** (c. 1244-1465) established the *madrasa* system in Morocco to centralize power and create a loyal elite. The **Wattasid dynasty** (c. 1472-1554) followed, but saw their power decline amidst internal strife and European incursions. The **Saadian dynasty** (1549-1659), claiming Sharifian lineage, rose by leading a *jihad* against Portuguese enclaves. They were military innovators, embracing gunpowder technology, professionalizing their army, and maintaining Moroccan sovereignty against the Ottoman threat, which was "fundamental in forging a distinct and fiercely independent Moroccan national identity". The Battle of the Three Kings (1578) was a pivotal victory against Portugal, enriching Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur and boosting his legitimacy. The current ruling dynasty, the **Alaouites** (since 1666), maintained a distinction between *Bled el-Makhzen* (direct governmental control) and *Bled es-Siba* (autonomous tribal regions) until the 20th century.

The 19th and 20th centuries saw increasing European influence, leading to French and Spanish protectorates. The French Protectorate (established 1912\) implemented policies to divide Arab and Berber populations, which was met with resistance. The Berber Dahir of 1930, aiming to codify customary law in Berber areas instead of Sharia, sparked a massive nationalist protest, uniting Moroccans against colonial policies. Sultan Muhammad V supported the nationalist movement, culminating in Morocco gaining independence from France in 1956 and Spain in 1956\. Post-independence, challenges include an "executive monarchy" where the king holds significant prerogatives, economic disparities benefiting a wealthy minority, and human rights concerns regarding freedom of expression and arbitrary detention. The **Western Sahara conflict**, ongoing since 1975, involves the Polisario Front and Morocco, with the UN affirming the Sahrawi people's right to self-determination, while Morocco advocates for autonomy under its sovereignty. Debates on cultural identity continue regarding the role of Arabic, Amazigh (which was incorporated into the constitution in 2011), and foreign languages.

Morocco has been a vibrant center of intellectual, artistic, and scientific endeavors. Cities like Fez and Marrakech were major learning hubs, with the **Qarawiyyin mosque in Fez**, established in the 9th century, depicted as the "oldest college in the world". Marinid *madrasas* (e.g., Bou Inania, Al-Attarine in Fez) are masterpieces of Hispano-Moorish style. Notable scholars and travelers include **Ibn Battuta** (d. 1369), whose *Rihla* details his extensive journeys; **Al-Idrisi** (d. 1166), a renowned geographer who created a scientifically accurate silver planisphere; **Ibn al-Banna** (d. 1321), a great mathematician and astronomer; and **Ibn Khaldun** (d. 1406), whose *al-Muqaddimah* laid foundations of sociology and philosophy of history \[12, History, Culture and Science in Morocco: 11th-14th Centuries \- Muslim Heritage\]. Marinid-era Moroccan books were known for their robust bindings, iron-gall ink, Maghrebi scripts, and intricate geometric and arabesque patterns. Architectural heritage includes Almohad minarets (Giralda, Kutubiyya, Hass√¢n Tower) and Marinid *madrasas*. **The Atlas Lion** is a powerful symbol of strength and royalty, while **Aicha Kandicha** is a prominent jinn-like mythological figure, possibly linked to historical resistance against the Portuguese. Oral traditions like Sufi poetry and folk tales were crucial for expression and knowledge transmission, particularly for women.

Morocco's economy and military history reflect strategic resource management and adaptation to warfare technologies. The Barb horse was essential for Saadian cavalry, and the Dromedary camel was crucial for trans-Saharan transport and projecting military power. The Saadian dynasty revolutionized Moroccan warfare by establishing a professional, standing army, embracing gunpowder technology (including domestically produced cannons), and employing sophisticated combined-arms tactics. Early Portuguese incursions had highlighted Moroccan military weaknesses, attributed to the "weakness and sloth of the Wattasid sultan" against superior Portuguese artillery. Moroccan markets (*souqs*) were vital hubs for trade, with Portuguese enclaves like Mazagan creating a complex environment where official hostility coexisted with pragmatic, unofficial trade. The Saadians monopolized sugar exports and taxed trans-Saharan trade to fund military campaigns, also seeking trade relations with European powers for firearms. In the mid-16th century, both Wattasid and Saadian dynasties minted their own currencies (silver *dirham*, copper *falus*), alongside Portuguese *r√©is* and Spanish coins. In the 19th century, Moroccan sultans increasingly relied on European loans, leading to economic capture by European finance.

This study investigates how a pre-modern Moroccan nation is constructed in middle school social studies textbooks, focusing on the cultural symbols used to form this national identity. Using an ethno-symbolist conceptual framework developed by Anthony Smith, the research highlights the significant role of textbooks in nation-building. The study suggests that these textbooks construct a pre-modern nation of Morocco primarily out of an Arab-Muslim cultural repertoire. Given Morocco's recent political changes, including the 2011 constitution that recognized the nation's multicultural nature, the article aims to assess whether this multicultural spirit is reflected in the textbooks. The research recommends changes to textbook content to adopt a multicultural approach that accommodates the cultural contributions of Amazigh and Jewish ethnic groups in national narratives.

Anthony Smith's ethno-symbolist approach to nation formation defines a nation as a "named and self-defining human community whose members cultivate shared memories, symbols, myths, traditions, and values, inhabit and are attached to historic territories or 'homelands,' create and disseminate a distinctive public culture, and observe shared customs and standardized laws". This framework allows for the emergence of nations centuries before the 18th century, which is relevant as Moroccan school textbooks refer to the establishment of the first Moroccan state in the 8th century. Smith proposes five social processes for a community to be forged into a nation: self-definition (through myths of ancestry), myth and memory cultivation (through myths of election), territorialization (through the sanctification of a homeland), creating a distinctive public culture (through language and religion), and creating shared customs and standardized laws. Nations are believed to be created over a long duration and have ethnic origins, progressing through phases from ethnie to pre-modern nation to modern nation.

The decisive role of education in nation-building is emphasized in nationalism studies. Scholars like Renan highlight the importance of collective memory, Gellner stresses cultural standardization for societal transitions, and Smith points to educational institutions for creating national narratives. Anderson, defining nations as "imagined communities," believes print-capitalism, including books and textbooks, plays a significant role in unifying national communities. International research supports the link between school textbooks and national construction; for instance, Jordanian history textbooks use exclusion strategies to build national identity, Bulgarian primary school textbooks portray an ethnically homogenous Christian nation, and Iranian middle school history textbooks make Islam the most prominent element of national identity. In Morocco, previous research on history textbooks has shown an evolution from Arabization of national identity (first generation) to incorporating Amazigh history and a non-Arab perception of Islamic history (later generations). One study found that national identity discourse in Moroccan textbooks does not adopt a pan-Arabism stance and depicts Amazigh people as defenders who helped create the first Moroccan Islamic state. However, another study analyzing French textbooks found a weak discourse on national identity, with Islamic education textbooks regarding Muslim religious identity as the basis for all other identities.

This study specifically analyzed second-year middle school social studies textbooks: *Annajah Fi Al Ijtimaiayte*, *Faddaa Al Ijtimaiyate*, and *Manar Al Ijtimaiyate*, all approved by the Moroccan Ministry of Education and covering the pre-modern era. The analysis used a qualitative, and to a lesser extent quantitative, approach to identify cultural symbols and their corresponding nation-formation processes.

**The Process of Self-Definition:** The textbooks narrate genealogical ties to Prophet Muhammad, or a "myth of ancestry," for the ruling elites of certain dynasties, thereby self-defining pre-modern Morocco as an Arabo-Islamic nation. This myth is strongly stressed for the Idrissid, Saadi, and Alaouite dynasties, which are portrayed as *Ashraf* (descendants of the Prophet) and originating from Arabia (Alhijaz). The Idrissids are introduced as descendants of Ali Ben Abi Talib, the Prophet's son-in-law. The Saadis are defined as *Ashraf* from the prophetic house. The Alaouites are traced back to Hassan Ben Alkacem, brought from Alhijaz, and are explicitly stated to be "offspring of Fatima, the daughter of prophet Muhammad" and Ali Bin Abi Talib. This consistent narration of an Arab ethnic origin and prophetic blood ties serves as a permanent cultural feature symbolizing the nation's genesis and persistence, fusing religion with ethnicity to define Morocco as an Arab-Muslim nation. In contrast, the Almoravid and Merinid dynasties, noted to be of Amazigh origin, are not ascribed this myth of common ancestry. While the Almohad leader, Ibn Tumart, is mentioned as having genealogical ties to Hassan Bin Ali Bin Abi Talib, two textbooks cast doubt on this by stressing his claim to be the Mahdi, which could undermine his credibility.

**The Process of Myth and Memory Cultivation:** The textbooks present the "pledge of allegiance" (Al Bayaa) as a "myth of missionary election," cultivating memory necessary for the genesis of the nation. Al Bayaa, defined as an Islamic concept going back to Prophet Muhammad's era, is a contract between the pledge makers and the appointee (Sultan, Khalifa, or king) to manage public affairs based on Islamic teachings, implying a mission to implement Islamic law. This concept is omnipresent throughout the pre-modern era, marking the genesis and persistence of the nation through various ruling states. The frequency of using Al Bayaa-related words is highest for the Idrissid and Alaouite states, indicating the nation's continuity from the first to the last dynasties. The emphasis on Al Bayaa during the Idrissid dynasty highlights the birth of the nation and the religious unity between Arab and Amazigh groups.

**The Process of Territorialization:** The textbooks sanctify Fez as a "sacred homeland" for the pre-modern Moroccan nation, prioritizing it over other historical cities like Marrakech. This is achieved by narrating its foundation by the "noble" Idriss II, son of Idriss I (who had prophetic blood ties), and by highlighting the holiness of its religious center, Al Qarawiyyin. Idriss II is referred to as "Imam Idriss" and "Maoula," lending him sacred ethno-religious legitimacy and making Fez a place of saint-hood activities. The Al Qarawiyyin mosque, depicted as the "oldest college in the world" and older than Al Azhar University in Egypt, is presented as the nation's religious center. Its foundation by Fatima Al Fihriya during Ramadan is portrayed as a sacred act, helping to set boundaries between pre-modern Morocco as an independent nation and the rest of the Islamic world.

**The Process of Creating a Distinctive Public Culture:** Islam is cultivated in the textbooks to generate a typical public culture for pre-modern Morocco, with Friday prayer and the pledge of allegiance serving as primary cultural symbols, especially during the Idrissid dynasty. The weekly Friday prayer (Salat Aljumuaah) symbolizes communal gathering and a sacred union between the Imam (Idriss I) and his subjects through the sermon. The pledge of allegiance, besides providing a myth of missionary election, also functions as a public ritual held during changes in state leadership, fostering a communion between rulers and their people based on a contract to uphold Islamic law.

**The Process of Creating Shared Customs and Standardized Laws:** The textbooks depict both the state and religious institutions as guardians of shared customs and standardized laws in pre-modern Morocco. Dynasties like the Idrissids, Almoravids, Almohads, Merinids, Saadis, and Alaouites are consistently labeled as "states" (Addawla), forming a historical continuity of the Moroccan state, unlike the Barghawata dynasty. These states, established through the concept of Al Bayaa, ensure the observance of shared customs and standardized laws, framed within Islamic teachings, including Sharia Law. For example, Yusuf Ben Tashfine (Almoravid leader) is noted for imposing only religiously sanctioned taxes like Zakat, which is significant for defining the Muslim community and fostering fraternity. The religious institution of Islam thus plays a vital role by endowing rulers with sacred laws to enforce. Additionally, state leaders like Mly Suleiman (Alaouite dynasty) imposed restrictions supported by *Fukaha* (Muslim scholars) who issued *Fatwas*, strengthening the Sultan's power to enforce laws on the community.

In conclusion, the analysis of middle school social studies textbooks reveals that the pre-modern Moroccan nation is constructed over a long period based on dominant Arab-Muslim cultural resources. This construction involves the processes of self-definition, myth and memory cultivation, territorialization, creating a distinctive public culture, and establishing shared customs and standardized laws. The cultural symbols employed include Arab ancestry ties to Prophet Muhammad, the pledge of allegiance (Al Bayaa), Fez as the sacred homeland, and the Islamic religion. This portrayal highlights the ethno-religious character of pre-modern Morocco as an Arab-Muslim nation. The study recommends that textbooks adopt a multicultural approach to accommodate the cultural symbols of Amazigh and Jewish ethnic groups, aligning with Morocco's 2011 constitutional reforms promoting national identity pluralism.

This study investigates how a pre-modern Moroccan nation is constructed in middle school social studies textbooks, focusing on the cultural symbols used to form this national identity. Using an ethno-symbolist conceptual framework developed by Anthony Smith, the research highlights the significant role of textbooks in nation-building. The study suggests that these textbooks construct a pre-modern nation of Morocco primarily out of an Arab-Muslim cultural repertoire. Given Morocco's recent political changes, including the 2011 constitution that recognized the nation's multicultural nature, the article aims to assess whether this multicultural spirit is reflected in the textbooks. The research recommends changes to textbook content to adopt a multicultural approach that accommodates the cultural contributions of Amazigh and Jewish ethnic groups in national narratives.

Anthony Smith's ethno-symbolist approach to nation formation defines a nation as a "named and self-defining human community whose members cultivate shared memories, symbols, myths, traditions, and values, inhabit and are attached to historic territories or 'homelands,' create and disseminate a distinctive public culture, and observe shared customs and standardized laws". This framework allows for the emergence of nations centuries before the 18th century, which is relevant as Moroccan school textbooks refer to the establishment of the first Moroccan state in the 8th century. Smith proposes five social processes for a community to be forged into a nation: self-definition (through myths of ancestry), myth and memory cultivation (through myths of election), territorialization (through the sanctification of a homeland), creating a distinctive public culture (through language and religion), and creating shared customs and standardized laws. Nations are believed to be created over a long duration and have ethnic origins, progressing through phases from ethnie to pre-modern nation to modern nation.

The decisive role of education in nation-building is emphasized in nationalism studies. Scholars like Renan highlight the importance of collective memory, Gellner stresses cultural standardization for societal transitions, and Smith points to educational institutions for creating national narratives. Anderson, defining nations as "imagined communities," believes print-capitalism, including books and textbooks, plays a significant role in unifying national communities. International research supports the link between school textbooks and national construction; for instance, Jordanian history textbooks use exclusion strategies to build national identity, Bulgarian primary school textbooks portray an ethnically homogenous Christian nation, and Iranian middle school history textbooks make Islam the most prominent element of national identity. In Morocco, previous research on history textbooks has shown an evolution from Arabization of national identity (first generation) to incorporating Amazigh history and a non-Arab perception of Islamic history (later generations). One study found that national identity discourse in Moroccan textbooks does not adopt a pan-Arabism stance and depicts Amazigh people as defenders who helped create the first Moroccan Islamic state. However, another study analyzing French textbooks found a weak discourse on national identity, with Islamic education textbooks regarding Muslim religious identity as the basis for all other identities.

This study specifically analyzed second-year middle school social studies textbooks: *Annajah Fi Al Ijtimaiayte*, *Faddaa Al Ijtimaiyate*, and *Manar Al Ijtimaiyate*, all approved by the Moroccan Ministry of Education and covering the pre-modern era. The cultural symbols employed include Arab ancestry ties to Prophet Muhammad, the pledge of allegiance (Al Bayaa), Fez as the sacred homeland, and the Islamic religion. This portrayal highlights the ethno-religious character of pre-modern Morocco as an Arab-Muslim nation. The study recommends that textbooks adopt a multicultural approach to accommodate the cultural symbols of Amazigh and Jewish ethnic groups, aligning with Morocco's 2011 constitutional reforms promoting national identity pluralism.

The Barghawatas were a Berber tribal confederation belonging to the Masmuda division, inhabiting the Atlantic coast of Morocco in the Tamesna region, located between Safi and Sal√©, an area that today includes Casablanca and Rabat. They established a powerful, independent Berber state from 744 to 1058 CE, which was characterized by its own distinct religion and a formidable army. Despite their significant political role in North Africa until the arrival of the Almoravids in the second half of the 11th century, medieval Arabic history often depicted them with increasing ambivalence due to their unique religious practices, ultimately vilifying and condemning them. Some historians, like J√©r√¥me Carcopino, suggest the name "Barghawata" might derive from the ancient Roman Baquates tribe near Volubilis, while others believe it's a phonetic deformation of "Barbati," a nickname for their leader Tarif, who might have been born near Barbate, Spain.

The Barghawatas played a crucial role in the Berber Revolt of 739 or 740 CE, allying with other Amazigh groups like the Ghomara and Miknasa against the ruling Umayyad Caliphate. This revolt was fueled by Sufri Kharijite preachers who advocated for total egalitarianism in opposition to the Umayyad aristocracy, and by the Umayyads' mistreatment of non-Arab Muslims, including heavy taxation and enslavement. Led by Maysara al-Matghari, the rebels successfully took control of most of present-day Morocco. Key victories included the Battle of the Nobles (Ghazwat al-Ashraf) in December 740 CE near Tangier, where many Arab aristocrats were massacred, and the Battle of Bagdoura in late 741 CE near Fes, which annihilated a powerful Syrian army and permanently broke the Umayyad grip on the far west Maghreb. The Barghawatas, as founders of the revolt, became resentful when later adherents, particularly Zenata chieftains and authoritarian Sufri commissars, attempted to control the rebellion's leadership. Having achieved their primary objective of liberation from Umayyad rule, they saw no reason to continue military campaigns. In 742 or 743 CE, they withdrew from the rebel alliance, retreated to the Tamesna region, and founded their new independent state, abandoning Sufri Kharijitism. This cultural rebellion, expressed through a distinct Berber Islam, complemented their successful political liberation from the Arabo-Islamic order.

The Barghawata state was led by a succession of rulers. **Tarif al-Matghari** was the ancestor of its rulers and likely a Kharijite. His son, **·π¢ƒÅli·∏• ibn Tarif** (744-?), declared himself a prophet in 744 CE, leaving at the age of 47 with a promise to return. He is believed to have introduced a Berber Quran. **Ilias ibn Salih** (792-842 CE) succeeded him, publicly professing Islam while secretly following ·π¢ƒÅli·∏•'s religion, and reigned for 50 years. **Younes ibn Ilias** (842-888 CE or 842-884 CE) formalized Salih's religion, notably undertaking a "radical Berberization of Islam" and executing those who refused to convert, reportedly killing 7,770 people. Younes, a calculating and charismatic leader, is sometimes credited with inventing this religion himself and attributing it to his respected grandfather to gain legitimacy. He is thought to have returned from a journey to the East with a new religious zeal and disdain for orthodox Islam, perceiving it as representing Arab rather than Amazigh identity. **Abou Ghafir Mohammed** (888-917 CE) was also called a prophet and died in the 29th year of his reign. **Abou an-Ansar Abdoullah** (917-961 CE) was buried in Ameslakht and died in the 44th year of his reign. **Abou Mansour Isa** (961-?) became king at 22\. Later, **Abu Hafs Abdullah** is mentioned in Arabic sources as an emir who fought against the Almoravids, leading to the death of their spiritual leader, Abdallah ibn Yasin, in 1058 CE.

The Barghawatas developed a distinctive **syncretic religion**, often referred to as Berber Islam, which was a "cultural rebellion" against Arab dominance. It was not a complete rejection of Islam, but rather a reinterpretation that aimed to contextualize the faith within the Amazigh milieu and assert their ethnic and cultural identity. This religion incorporated elements of Sunni, Shi'a, and Kharijite Islam, mixed with astrological and traditional Berber beliefs. They had their own **Berber Quran** of 80 suras, some of which were translations of the Arabic Quran, while others were unique, named after prophets like Salih and Younes. The Berber Quran considered the word of God (Yakouch) to be revealed to Salih ibn Tarif, making Salih the prophet of the Berbers (*waryawari* ‚Äì "one after whom no other prophet will come"), while Mohammed was recognized as the prophet of the Arabs. Their God was called **Yakouch**, not Allah. Specific practices included demanding ten daily prayers (compared to five in orthodox Islam), reducing the Ramadan fast to three days, and praying according to the cock crow rather than a fixed solar schedule. They also observed a taboo on eating eggs and chickens and believed in the *baraka* (blessedness) of the prophet Salih's saliva. These specific adaptations, which sometimes aligned with "magic" or pre-Islamic Berber traditions, served to affirm their ethnic identity and elevate Berber figures to religious prominence, counteracting the dominant Arabo-Islamic social order.

The longevity of the Barghawata dynasty for over three centuries (744-1058 CE) was attributed to several factors beyond their unique religion. They possessed a **redoubtable and well-structured army** capable of fielding over 12,000 cavalry, as reported by al-Bakri. Their military prowess was renowned, and they were led by cunning, intelligent, charismatic, and often ruthless rulers. Rulers like Younes ibn Ilias used military force to maintain order and prevent dissidence, while Abdallah Abou an-Ansar managed relations with neighboring tribes by annually assembling his army to threaten raids, securing gifts and promises of friendship. Geographically, their location on the Atlantic coast with a friendly Al-Andalus meant they could focus defenses on land borders. Their territory's complex topography with winding river valleys made invasion difficult, and fertile soil allowed for self-sufficiency, minimizing reliance on surrounding kingdoms. They also effectively used fire signals and local rivers for communication, giving them an advantage over invading forces. These combined strengths allowed them to resist external pressures from the Idrisids and Umayyads for generations.

The Barghawatas initially maintained good relations with the Umayyad Caliphate of Cordoba, but these relations broke down by the end of the 10th century. They successfully repelled two Umayyad incursions from Spain (977-978; 998-999) and a Fatimid attack from the East (982-983). From the 11th century, they engaged in intense guerrilla warfare with their Amazigh neighbors, the Banou Ifrens, which weakened them. Despite this, they famously repelled attacks from the rising Almoravids, even causing the death of the Almoravid spiritual leader, Abdallah ibn Yasin, in battle in 1058 CE. However, the Barghawatas were ultimately eliminated as a political and religious group by the **Almohads** in 1149 CE. The Almohad military leader Abdelmoumen conquered the Barghawata principality and "totally exterminated its 'heretical' population". The Tamesna region was then isolated for several decades before the Almohads resettled it with Arab tribes as a punitive measure.

Historically, the Barghawata dynasty remains controversial and is often marginalized in Moroccan history, frequently being incomplete or absent from school curricula. Their distinct religious doctrine, considered heterodox by orthodox Islam, led neighboring dynasties like the Idrisids, Zirids, Almoravids, and Almohads to declare holy war against them. Despite this, their history is crucial for understanding the contemporary Amazigh identity, as they asserted a platform of *Amazighitude*‚Äîthe idea that the indigenous population should be politically and culturally liberated from foreign influence. Their resistance challenged the common perception that North African Berbers passively accepted Arab rule and Islam, instead demonstrating a determination to adapt Islam to their own cultural context. Modern Moroccan deviations from orthodox Muslim practices, such as the veneration of saints (maraboutism), are seen by some as a continuation of this persistent Amazigh determination to practice Islam in their own way.

After the death of Idris II in 828 CE, the Idrissid state in Morocco fractured, with his sons dividing the region into numerous semi-independent principalities, such as Fes, Taza, and Basra. This political fragmentation left Morocco vulnerable to external influence, transforming it into a "chessboard" where two major powers, the Fatimids from Ifriqiya (modern-day Tunisia) and the Umayyads of al-Andalus (C√≥rdoba), vied for control. Both dynasties sought to exert influence over Morocco by utilizing vassals, governors, and client dynasties, leading to a period where tribes, cities, and local rulers constantly shifted their allegiance based on who offered the most protection or financial incentives.

Several key players emerged as "middlemen" or instruments for the Fatimids and Umayyads. By the 10th century, the **Idrissids** themselves were reduced to minor rulers in Fes and its environs, often switching sides. Some Idrissid emirs, due to their descent from Ahl al-Bayt and a Shi‚Äòi affinity, pledged loyalty to the Fatimids, while others allied with the Umayyads to resist Fatimid pressure. An example is al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn al-Qasim, an Idrissid of Fes, who was recognized by C√≥rdoba in 931 but later compelled to submit to the Fatimids. The **Miknasa**, a powerful Zenata Berber tribe in central and northern Morocco, were significant "swing men" of the era. Their chief, Musa ibn Abi‚Äôl-‚ÄòAfiya (d. \~938), initially served as a Fatimid vassal, even receiving the title of *amir al-Maghrib*, before defecting to the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba around 931‚Äì932. He eventually attempted to establish an independent rule, but his ambitions were ultimately crushed by shifting alliances.

The **Maghrawa**, another Zenata Berber group from the Middle Atlas and Fes, rose in power during the Idrissid decline. Their leaders, such as Muhammad ibn Khazar al-Maghrawi, allied firmly with the Umayyads and became C√≥rdoba‚Äôs primary clients in Morocco, eventually ruling Fes under Umayyad suzerainty. The **Barghawata**, an independent and "heretical" state on the Tamesna coast (744‚Äì1058), also played both sides opportunistically. Although they became a significant regional force by the mid-10th century, their religious isolation meant neither the Fatimids nor the Umayyads fully trusted them. Other Zenata confederations, including the HawwƒÅra and Ban≈´ Ifran in present-day Algeria and eastern Morocco, similarly shifted between Fatimid and Umayyad patronage.

The Fatimids typically appointed *amƒ´rs* or *wƒÅlƒ´s* over Morocco, often leveraging Zenata tribes like the Miknasa. In response, the Umayyads countered by bestowing titles, gold, and robes of honor (*khil‚Äòa*) upon rival tribes such as the Maghrawa and certain Idrissid princes. This dynamic led to a constant "tug-of-war," exemplified by C√≥rdoba‚Äôs Caliph Abd al-Rahman III, who in 931 dispatched armies into Morocco, compelling many Idrissid and Zenata leaders to switch their allegiance from Fatimid to Umayyad. This era was characterized by frequent "double allegiance," where a chieftain might mint coins in the name of C√≥rdoba one year and be recorded as a Fatimid governor the next.

By the mid-11th century, Morocco remained fragmented, with Idrissid remnants in the Rif, the Maghrawa in Fes under nominal Umayyad protection, the Barghawata on the Atlantic coast, and the Ban≈´ Ifran in Tlemcen and Sal√©. The collapse of the Umayyad Caliphate in C√≥rdoba in 1031 and the weakening of Fatimid influence left a power vacuum, with no clear overlord in the region. It was into this chaotic environment that the **Almoravids** (*al-MurƒÅbi·π≠≈´n*), founded in 1040, emerged. They swept through Morocco from 1058 to 1060, unifying the country militarily and religiously under Maliki Sunni orthodoxy. The Almoravids destroyed the Barghawata state, absorbed the Maghrawa, and ended the Idrissid remnants, establishing a true central power in Morocco for the first time since the early Idrissid period. This effectively ended the "Game of Thrones" scenario where various groups constantly shifted allegiance between C√≥rdoba and Kairouan, with the Almoravids imposing a unified order. Key vassal leaders from this tumultuous period include Musa ibn Abi‚Äôl-‚ÄòAfiya (Miknasa Zenata), Muhammad ibn Khazar (Maghrawa), al-Hasan ibn al-Qasim (Idrissid), and various Ban≈´ Ifran chiefs.

The mid-16th century saw the Portuguese maintain a formidable maritime presence on the Atlantic coast of Morocco, exemplified by the fortified city of Mazagan (modern El Jadida). This bastion was entirely conceived, constructed, and sustained by sea power, serving as a crucial European foothold that relied on open supply lines across the Atlantic. The decision to build a major fortification at Mazagan was a direct response to the rising power of the Saadian Sultanate, particularly after the capture of the Portuguese fortress of Santa Cruz (Agadir) in 1541\. King John III ordered the evacuation of less defensible positions to concentrate resources on Mazagan, which was built between 1541 and 1548 by a team of leading military architects including Jo√£o Ribeiro, Juan Castillo, and Benedetto da Ravenna. The fortress was a masterclass in Renaissance military engineering: a formidable star-shaped polygon, approximately 250 by 300 meters, featuring four massive bastions (Angel, St Sebastian, St Antoine, and Holy Ghost) with walls averaging 8 meters in height and 10 meters in thickness, designed to absorb cannon fire. A wide, seawater-filled moat completed its landward defenses. This design implicitly acknowledged that Portugal could not control the vast Moroccan interior but could render a land-based siege futile by leveraging its naval strength. The fortress's direct and protected "Seagate" provided continuous reinforcement and supply via the sea, even when surrounded by an enemy army, making it a static anchor for a dynamic and dominant naval force.

The naval forces sustaining Mazagan were drawn from the Portuguese maritime system, primarily consisting of three classes of ships: carracks, caravels, and galleons. The **carrack**, or *Nau*, was the quintessential vessel of the Age of Discovery, an armed cargo carrier capable of long voyages. By the mid-16th century, these were among the largest ships globally, often over 600 tons displacement and measuring around 37 meters in length with towering forecastles and aftcastles that provided a significant defensive height advantage. An exemplary carrack was the *Santa Catarina de Monte Sinai*, built in 1510 in Cochin, India, of teak, and known for its rich decoration, numerous flags, and substantial armament of deck cannons and smaller breech-loading swivel guns like *ber√ßos* and *falcaos*. These swivel guns were particularly effective against lightly armored corsair vessels. The **caravel** was a versatile scout, patrol vessel, and light warship, prized for its speed, agility, and ability to sail close to the wind. By the 16th century, the *caravela redonda* (square-rigged caravel) had emerged, a larger and more robust hybrid combining a square-rigged foremast for speed with three lateen-rigged masts aft for maneuverability. These *caravelas de armada* were true warships, forming the backbone of coastal defense fleets and ideal for escorting convoys and supporting besieged fortresses. The **galleon**, emerging in the mid-16th century, represented an evolutionary leap in warship design. It synthesized the seakeeping of the carrack with the agility of the caravel, resulting in a purpose-built gun platform with a longer, lower, and narrower hull, a flat square tuck stern, and the elimination of the towering forecastle in favor of a long, low beakhead. These innovations provided greater stability, speed, and maneuverability, making the galleon a superior platform for artillery, notably with continuous gun decks allowing for powerful broadsides. The *S√£o Jo√£o Baptista*, nicknamed the *Botafogo* ("Spitfire"), built in 1534, was an exceptionally large Portuguese galleon of 1,000 tons, reportedly armed with 366 guns, representing the pinnacle of naval technology at the time.

While the precise number of Portuguese ships off Morocco in 1552 is unknown, the relief of Mazagan during the Great Siege of 1562 offers insight into Portugal's dynamic naval mobilization system. This system operated on two tiers: a small, permanent patrol force, likely part of the *Armada do Estreito* (Strait Armada), composed primarily of fast *caravelas de armada* for anti-corsair duties, escort, and immediate support; and a much larger expeditionary capability that could be rapidly assembled in a crisis. The 1562 relief effort saw an initial response from Captain √Ålvaro de Carvalho, followed by a volunteer force from the Algarve, and finally a large, state-sponsored expedition from Lisbon, mobilizing no fewer than 20,000 men and requiring dozens, if not over a hundred, vessels of all sizes. Therefore, in a year of relative peace like 1552, the visible Portuguese presence would have been a small squadron of 5 to 10 patrol caravels and support craft, backed by the latent potential to summon a massive relief armada of powerful galleons, carracks, and requisitioned vessels within weeks.

The maritime opposition faced by the Portuguese at Mazagan was multifaceted, distinctly separating the Saadian Sultanate's formal military from the semi-autonomous Barbary corsair fleets. The **Saadian Sultanate**, despite controlling a long Atlantic coastline and successfully driving the Portuguese from many Moroccan holdings, did not possess a significant, state-organized navy capable of challenging European fleets in open combat during the mid-16th century. Their military strength was overwhelmingly terrestrial, focused on unifying tribes, defeating rivals like the Wattasids, and waging land wars against Portuguese fortresses using a gunpowder-equipped field army and modern artillery. This land-centric focus came at the expense of naval development, making it impossible for them to successfully besiege a sea-supplied fortress like Mazagan. Evidence of this naval weakness includes Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur negotiating with England in 1581 to import naval timber, indicating a desire to *begin* building a fleet, and the Saadians paying tribute to the Ottoman Regency of Algiers to protect Moroccan shipping from corsair attacks. Consequently, the Saadian maritime presence was limited to small, local craft like fishing boats and coastal traders, outsourcing the naval war against the Portuguese to the Barbary corsairs.

The **Barbary corsairs** represented the true naval threat, operating from bases across North Africa, particularly the Ottoman Regency of Algiers, as well as Moroccan ports like Sal√©. These corsairs, a mix of local Moors, Turkish adventurers, and European renegades, were masters of raiding warfare, with fleets designed for speed, surprise, and the capture of ships and people, rather than formal naval battles. Their primary vessels were oar-powered galleys and their smaller variants. The **galley** was the supreme warship of the Mediterranean, prioritizing speed and maneuverability, propelled by oarsmen (often Christian slaves). A typical large corsair galley was up to 55 meters long with a narrow 5-meter beam, armed with a bow battery of heavy guns firing forward over a *rambade*, supplemented by anti-personnel swivel guns. Its most potent weapon was its large contingent of fighting men for boarding actions. The **galiot** was a smaller, lighter, and faster version of the galley, highly favored by corsairs for its agility, with 16 to 24 rowing benches per side. The **fusta** was an even smaller, extremely fast oared vessel, essentially a miniature galiot, typically with 10 to 15 rowing benches per side, ideal for coastal raiding and surprise attacks due to its speed and shallow draft. An emerging hybrid design, the **xebec**, also originated in Algiers, combining a narrow, galley-like hull with a powerful three-masted lateen sail plan, making it an exceptional performer under wind power and capable of carrying substantial armament and crew. Corsair fleets were typically mixed squadrons, their tactics focused on profit, avoiding well-armed naval warships, and preying on less-defended merchant vessels using speed, deception, and overwhelming manpower for boarding actions. This created a tactical tension in the waters off Mazagan: high-sided, heavily gunned Portuguese carracks or galleons were formidable in open Atlantic waters with wind, while becalmed sailing ships were vulnerable to the swift, oar-powered corsair vessels that could attack from blind spots.

The broader strategic context around Mazagan was profoundly shaped by the two great Mediterranean superpowers: the Spanish and Ottoman Empires. The **Spanish Navy** maintained a significant presence, mirroring Portugal's in purpose and composition, defending its North African *presidios* (e.g., Melilla, Oran, Ceuta) and vital trade routes. Spain possessed one of the world's most powerful navies, a balanced force of galley squadrons for the Mediterranean and an ocean-going fleet of carracks and galleons for the Atlantic. Spanish ship types were largely identical to the Portuguese, with Spanish admirals Pedro Men√©ndez de Avil√©s and √Ålvaro de Baz√°n credited with the definitive, sleeker galleon design of the 1550s. While colonial rivals, Spain and Portugal were de facto allies against the common corsair threat in North Africa, leading to a convergence in naval technology and strategy, both adopting the heavily-armed, high-sided galleon to counter oared vessels.

The **Ottoman Imperial Fleet**, based primarily in Istanbul, was the most powerful naval force in the Mediterranean at its peak in the 16th century, cemented by victories at Preveza (1538) and Djerba (1560). Its core was the war galley, deployed for major strategic objectives such as the conquest of Rhodes, the siege of Malta, and the invasion of Cyprus. Ottoman expeditions into Morocco, however, were primarily land-based invasions launched from their province of Algiers, aiming to install vassal rulers in Fez, rather than sustained naval operations on the Atlantic coast, which posed immense logistical challenges for a galley fleet. Instead, the Ottoman Empire pursued a highly effective strategy of proxy warfare in the Western Mediterranean, empowering the Regency of Algiers to act as the forward arm of Ottoman sea power. Algiers became a self-funding military enterprise, sustained by piracy and the slave trade, serving the Sultan's strategic goals by waging a relentless war of attrition against Habsburg Spain and its Portuguese allies, tying down vast Iberian naval assets at minimal cost to the imperial treasury.

**Hayreddin Barbarossa** was the single most impactful individual on the 16th-century Western Mediterranean maritime world, serving as the architect of the very threat the Portuguese fortress of Mazagan was built to withstand. Originally Khi·∏çr Reis, he and his brother Oru√ß became leading corsairs, capturing Algiers in 1516 and pledging allegiance to Ottoman Sultan Selim I. This act transformed Algiers into the Regency of Algiers, an official, autonomous province of the Ottoman Empire, providing the corsairs with legitimacy, resources, and Turkish Janissaries, making Algiers the most powerful and feared naval base in the Western Mediterranean. Hayreddin, who became Grand Admiral (*Kapudan Pasha*) of the entire Ottoman Navy, led the Imperial Fleet to its greatest victory at the Battle of Preveza in 1538\. While his direct military actions focused on the heart of Habsburg power in the Mediterranean, his connection to Morocco was twofold: he represented a constant potential threat and rival to the Saadian Sultans, and he forged a strategic alliance with Sayyida al-Hurra, the female corsair ruler of T√©touan, effectively dividing the war against the Iberian powers. Barbarossa's indirect but profound influence on Mazagan stemmed from his institutionalization of Barbary piracy as an instrument of state policy backed by the Ottoman Empire, thereby creating the strategic problem of Algiers and shaping the nature of the sea war on the Barbary Coast.

The mid-16th century culminated over a hundred years of intense maritime conflict between Iberian Christian powers and North African Muslim states, characterized by sieges of coastal fortresses, amphibious landings, and vicious encounters between sailing warships and oared corsairs. The **Great Siege of Mazagan in 1562** serves as a definitive case study of this strategic stalemate, illustrating the interplay between a powerful land army and a fortress sustained by dominant sea power. Saadian Sultan Abdallah al-Ghalib besieged the fortress with an army of over 100,000 men and siege artillery, employing advanced siege techniques. However, the small Portuguese garrison, constantly replenished with reinforcements, food, ammunition, and military engineers arriving through the sea gate, repelled every assault. The Saadian army's inability to interdict sea supplies ultimately doomed their efforts, proving that a Renaissance fortress, properly supplied by sea, could hold out indefinitely against a numerically superior land army lacking a navy.

**The Battle off Albufeira in 1554** provides an excellent example of a typical anti-corsair action. A Portuguese naval force, primarily of sail-powered caravels, intercepted and pursued a corsair squadron of eight oared vessels from Algiers. The Portuguese ships, benefiting from Atlantic winds, closed the distance and, after a two-hour battle, captured the corsair flagship and another vessel, freeing one hundred Christian captives. This demonstrated the crucial role of permanent Portuguese patrol fleets in coastal security and their advantage in open water with favorable wind.

Though fought entirely on land, the **Battle of Alc√°cer Quibir in 1578** had decisive naval consequences, reshaping the entire maritime and political landscape of the region. King Sebastian I of Portugal led a massive expeditionary force of some 23,000 men, transported to Tangier by a great naval armada, into the Moroccan interior. On August 4, 1578, near Ksar el-Kebir, this army was annihilated by the Saadian army of Sultan Abd al-Malik, numbering over 50,000 men and well-equipped with gunpowder weapons. King Sebastian, his Moroccan ally, and Sultan Abd al-Malik all died in the battle. This catastrophe bankrupted the Portuguese crown, ended serious Portuguese territorial conquest in Morocco, and plunged Portugal into a succession crisis. This crisis was resolved in 1580 when King Philip II of Spain claimed the Portuguese throne, uniting the two Iberian crowns and their vast global maritime empires under a single ruler, creating an Atlantic superpower and fundamentally altering the balance of power against the Barbary corsairs. For the Saadian Sultanate, the victory elevated its prestige and cemented its independence from Ottoman ambitions.

For those seeking to recreate this world, accurate visual references and specifications for these vessels are crucial. The **Portuguese Carrack** *Santa Catarina de Monte Sinai* (c. 1540 painting) featured a high, rounded stern, towering stern/forecastles, a four-masted square and lateen rig with bonnets, and cannons firing over the rails. The **Portuguese Caravel** (*caravela redonda*, seen in a 1572 Lisbon engraving) had longer/narrower proportions, a relatively low forecastle/sterncastle, and a hybrid rig with a square-rigged foremast and three lateen-rigged masts aft. Early to mid-16th century **Galleons**, exemplified by the *S√£o Jo√£o Baptista* (1534), eliminated the high forecastle for a long, low beakhead, had a flat "square tuck" stern, a lower, integrated sterncastle, and critically, continuous main gun decks with lidded gunports for broadsides. **Barbary Galleys and Galiots** (19th-century engravings, *La Real* replica) were extremely long and narrow with low freeboard, propelled by a single tier of long oars from an outrigger, one or two lateen sails, a *rambade* gun platform at the bow, a central *corsia*, and a stern pavilion. The **Xebec** (16th-century French design) had a unique, graceful shape with pronounced, overhanging bow and stern, typically three lateen-rigged masts (foremast raked sharply forward), and could use oars for auxiliary propulsion.

Essential resources for further research and accurate modeling include the Museu de Marinha (Lisbon) for Portuguese maritime history, the Museu Mar√≠tim (Barcelona) for its replica of the 16th-century galley *La Real*, and the National Maritime Museum (Greenwich) for key iconographic evidence like "Portuguese Carracks off a Rocky Coast." The General Archive of the Indies (Seville) holds primary records for the Spanish Empire. Digital archives, while extensive, complement the limited direct archaeological evidence from 16th-century shipwrecks off Morocco, making surviving iconographic and archival evidence all the more critical for historical reconstruction. The maritime landscape off Mazagan circa 1552 was a complex frontier, an asymmetrical conflict between the blue-water, sail-driven firepower of the Portuguese and the oar-driven, raiding tactics of the Barbary corsairs, all shaped by the broader strategic struggles of the Spanish and Ottoman Empires.

The 16th century transformed the fertile coastal plains of Doukkala, Morocco, into a dynamic and contested frontier where the ambitions of the burgeoning Portuguese maritime empire and the ascendant Saadian Sultanate converged. The Portuguese established fortified trading posts, or *pra√ßas fortes*, like Safi, Azemmour, and eventually Mazagan, to control lucrative trade in goods such as wheat, horses, and textiles, essential for their gold and slave trading operations further south. This European encroachment occurred during a period of political fragmentation in Morocco, where the ruling Wattasid dynasty in Fez held tenuous authority, allowing the Saadian Sharifs from the Draa River valley to rise, consolidate power, and unify the country through military resistance against the Portuguese. The Doukkala plains, an agricultural heartland inhabited by Amazigh and Arab tribes, became the primary theater for these interactions, with local tribes forming fluid alliances with either the Portuguese (*mouros de pazes*) or the Saadians. The loss of Agadir to the Saadians in 1541 was a critical turning point that prompted the Portuguese to transform the modest 1514 citadel at Mazagan into a state-of-the-art Renaissance fortress between 1541 and 1548, signifying a strategic consolidation in the face of mounting pressure.

The Portuguese fortress of Mazagan (present-day El Jadida) was a transplanted piece of Europe, designed to function as an autonomous cultural and physical enclave. Its military design, an outstanding example of new European concepts in a colonial context, was entrusted to an international team of leading military engineers: the Italian Benedetto da Ravenna, the Spaniard Juan Castillo, and the Portuguese Joao Ribeiro. Conceived as a **star-shaped plan** (*trace italienne*), approximately 250 by 300 meters, it featured four angled bastions‚Äîthe Angel, St. Sebastian, St. Antoine, and Holy Ghost Bastions‚Äîengineered to eliminate defensive blind spots and create overlapping fields of fire for cannons. Its massive, battered walls, around 8 meters high and 10 meters thick, were designed to deflect cannonballs and enclosed a 2-meter-wide peripheral walkway. The fortress was physically isolated from the mainland by a wide, seawater-filled ditch, about 20 meters across and 3 meters deep, with access controlled by three gates, including a main land entrance and a protected Seagate. Within its formidable military shell, the principal buildings, such as the **Church of the Assumption** and the **Portuguese Cistern**, expressed a unique Portuguese cultural identity through the **Manueline style**, a florid, late Gothic architectural variant. The church showcased intricate ornamentation with maritime motifs, while the vast subterranean cistern, originally an armory or barracks, became renowned for its vaulted ceiling supported by stone columns, a masterpiece of late Gothic engineering later used by Orson Welles in his film *Othello*. These culturally specific buildings underscored Mazagan's dual purpose as a complete, self-contained Portuguese city designed for permanence.

Beyond Mazagan's ramparts, the cities and towns of Doukkala, such as Azemmour, were firmly within the cultural and political orbit of the Saadian Sultanate. The 16th century marked a "renaissance" of Moroccan-Andalusian art and architecture under the Saadians, characterized by intricate interior decoration, privacy, and a rich palette of materials. The Saadian architectural language blended Amazigh, Muslim al-Andalus, and Islamic Middle Eastern influences. **Luxurious materials** included imported Italian Carrara marble, intricately carved and painted **cedarwood** for ceilings (*berchla*) and doors, finely sculpted plaster (*geps* or stucco), and vibrant **mosaic tilework** (*zellij*). Decoration adhered to Islamic aniconism, featuring complex geometric motifs, often radiating star patterns in *zellij*, elaborate vegetal *arabesques* and three-dimensional honeycomb *muqarnas* in stucco, and Arabic calligraphy in wood. **Horseshoe arches** were ubiquitous, and square-shafted minarets remained standard in religious architecture. The principles of this aesthetic were domestically expressed in the urban elite residences, the *dar* or *riad*, designed with an inward focus to protect privacy (*hurma*). These homes featured austere, often windowless, exteriors, with a **bent or L-shaped entrance** (*skifa*) blocking direct views into the **central courtyard** (*wast ad-dar*), which served as the primary source of light and ventilation. Rooms were arranged symmetrically around this courtyard, often with a central fountain or a symmetrical four-part garden in a *riad*. Surviving Saadian residences in Marrakech, like Dar Cherifa, demonstrate this style, featuring characteristic courtyard layouts and lavish carved stucco and wood decorations similar to the Ben Youssef Madrasa. A comparative analysis highlights the Portuguese architecture's outward-facing military projection and separation, using cut stone and Renaissance influences, versus the Saadian's inward-facing domestic tranquility and integration, using rammed earth, mudbrick, stucco, wood, and tile, with pervasive *zellij* and carved decoration.

Beyond the cities, the rural Doukkala plains nurtured its own unique vernacular architectural tradition, driven by function, local materials, and communal knowledge. The most distinctive example is the **Tazota**, a form of dry-stone construction found exclusively in this region, concentrated around 20 km from El Jadida. *Tazotas* are built without mortar, mud, or any binding agent, using stones cleared from the land for farming. They typically take a spherical or frustum-of-a-cone shape, resembling solid stone mounds, with thick walls and a semi-spherical dome providing excellent thermal insulation. Originally used as primary dwellings and shelters for landowners, often with an underground gallery (*Toufri*) for grain storage, *Tazotas* later served as storage or animal shelters. While their precise origins are uncertain, local inhabitants suggest a 19th-century origin for nomadic populations transitioning to agriculture, though the timeless nature of such techniques implies they were likely an established part of the Doukkala landscape during the 16th century. The *Tazota*'s construction from the very landscape it occupies embodies a perfect cycle of sustainability, transforming farming obstacles into essential infrastructure.

The commercial activity in 16th-century Doukkala's coastal hubs like Azemmour and Safi necessitated a well-developed market infrastructure. The **traditional souk** was not a single building but a labyrinthine network of narrow, winding, and often covered alleyways deep within the city's medina. Its layout was highly organized, with main arteries connecting city gates to the central mosque, and trades clustered into specialized quarters (e.g., *Souk Haddadine* for blacksmiths, *Souk el Attarine* for spice merchants). A strict economic hierarchy placed valuable and "cleanest" goods near the mosque, while "noisier" trades were further out. Shops and workshops were typically small units on the ground floors of residential buildings, opening directly onto the alleyway. Streets were often covered for shade, and construction materials were local and functional, such as rammed earth, mudbrick, stone, and wood. Essential to this trading ecosystem was the **funduq**, or urban caravanserai, a multi-story building with a courtyard, providing stables and secure storage on the ground floor and lodging for traveling merchants on upper floors, serving as hotels, warehouses, and wholesale markets.

To visually contextualize this architectural landscape, the 1606 map ***Fessae et Marocchi Regna*** by the Dutch cartographer **Jodocus Hondius** offers an invaluable, albeit biased, European perspective. Published in the Mercator-Hondius Atlas, this hand-colored copper engraving covered the North African coast and was embellished with illustrations of sailing ships and a sea monster. The map reveals a distinct focus on the coastal areas‚Äîprimary zones of European trade, navigation, and military engagement‚Äîwhile the interior regions, the heartland of the Saadian Sultanate, are depicted with significantly less detail, often filled with generic mountains and forests. This cartographic bias highlights the limits of European knowledge and influence inland. Key settlements in the Doukkala region labeled on the map include "Duccala" for the entire region, "Mazagam" for the Portuguese fortress, "Azamor" (Azemmour) at the mouth of the Oum Er-Rbia river, and "Saffi" (Safi) further south. Smaller coastal settlements like "Tit" are also marked, but inland towns are largely unnamed and generically represented, confirming the European coastal focus.

In conclusion, the 16th-century built environment of the Doukkala plains presented a profound triad of architectural contrasts. The **Portuguese Renaissance Fortress** of Mazagan represented an architecture of imposition, technologically advanced, culturally sealed, and projecting power outward through its star-shaped bastions and massive walls, symbolizing a colonial ideology of isolation. The **Saadian Hispano-Moorish Style**, found in urban centers like Azemmour, was an architecture of opulence and introspection, rooted in Andalusian and Islamic artistic traditions, prioritizing lavishly decorated, private interior worlds through *zellij*, carved stucco, and cedarwood. Finally, the **Doukkala Vernacular Tradition**, exemplified by the unique *Tazota*, was an architecture of symbiosis‚Äîfunctional, unadorned, and built from local stones, representing an intimate and sustainable relationship with the agricultural landscape. These coexisting architectural paradigms vividly reflect the era's complex dynamics, serving as a powerful and enduring lens into Moroccan history at a pivotal crossroads.

The art of the book in Marinid and Wattasid Morocco (c. 1250-1550) involved sophisticated techniques and materials, reflecting a significant period of intellectual flourishing and artistic refinement. This era saw the gradual transition from parchment to paper as the dominant writing surface, the flourishing of distinct Maghrebi calligraphic styles, and the development of intricate binding decorations.

The choice of writing material was fundamental, with **parchment** dominating at the beginning of the Marinid period. This durable material, made from animal skin through a laborious process of soaking, scraping, and stretching on a wooden frame, was susceptible to environmental changes, necessitating heavy wooden covers and robust clasps to keep books flat. While **paper** was available earlier‚ÄîFez was a major paper manufacturing center with over 100 mills by the 11th century and 400 by the 12th century‚Äîparchment held a cultural and religious prestige, particularly for high-status and sacred texts like the Qur'an. The shift to paper for these texts became widespread only in the later Marinid period, driven by increased demand for books from a flourishing intellectual life in Fez. Regardless of material, surfaces were meticulously prepared with faint ruled lines for orderly writing, and paper sheets were often sized with wheat starch and burnished to prevent ink bleed.

The visual character of manuscripts was also defined by **inks and illumination**. Main texts were typically written in dark brown or black **iron-gall ink**, preferred for its permanence and crisp lines, made from vitriol and oak galls. **Colored inks**, derived from natural sources like cinnabar (red), azurite or indigo (blue), orpiment or verdigris (green), and realgar (yellow/orange), were used for headings, marks, and commentary, contributing to the page's aesthetic. **Illumination (*tadhhib*)**, the application of gold or silver leaf, was reserved for luxury manuscripts, especially Qur'ans. Gold leaf was affixed with adhesive and burnished to create a brilliant, mirror-like surface. Late North African manuscripts, particularly from the 15th century onward, were characterized by an exceptionally wide and vibrant range of colors‚Äîorange, red, yellow, green, pink, and blue‚Äîused alongside gold for intricate arabesques and stylized floral motifs in frontispieces, *surah* headings, and verse markers.

**Maghrebi calligraphy** was the soul of the manuscript, flourishing into a distinct tradition during the Marinid era. It evolved from ancient Kufic script in North Africa and al-Andalus, with Andalusi refugees in Fez further enriching its development. Manuscripts typically featured a clear hierarchy: **Maghrebi cursive** (broad, flowing, with deep, rounded curves) for the main text, usually in brown or black ink; and a more angular, decorative **Maghrebi Kufic** for high-impact elements like Qur'anic chapter headings, often executed in gold leaf. Calligraphy was a noble pursuit, even practiced by rulers like Sultan Abu al-Hasan, who personally copied four Qur'ans.

The **binding** served as the manuscript's protective shell and public face. Text blocks, formed from gatherings of parchment or paper, were sewn onto horizontal supports, typically double cords of hemp or leather thongs. The covers, made of wooden boards (for parchment) or pasteboard (for paper), were strongly attached by lacing the sewing supports directly through them. A defining feature of Islamic bindings from this region was the **fore-edge flap**, a pentagonal flap attached to the back cover that folded over the pages to protect them from dust and damage.

For luxury bindings, **Morocco leather** (high-quality goatskin) was the material of choice, prized for its strength, pliability, visible grain, dye affinity (deep reds, greens, blues, dark browns), and ability to take tooling. Wooden boards were common for high-status parchment manuscripts, while lighter pasteboard, made by laminating paper, was used for paper texts, particularly later in the period. **Protective features** such as metal clasps or leather straps were almost always fitted to parchment books to counteract the material's tendency to swell and warp with humidity, keeping pages flat. Decorative metal "furniture" like ornate corner pieces or central bosses could also be added for deluxe bindings. The shift to dimensionally stable paper reduced the need for these heavy fittings, with later paper manuscripts typically relying on lighter pasteboards and only the fore-edge flap for closure.

The **decoration of Moroccan book covers** followed a sophisticated "grammar of ornament" rooted in geometry and symmetry, shared across various Marinid artistic media. Designs were almost invariably centered on a prominent **medallion**, often a lozenge, an eight-pointed star, a mandorla (almond shape), or a circular motif. These central medallions were typically complemented by four corner-pieces and enclosed within decorative borders, with the design repeated on both covers. The dominant style was **complex geometric interlace**, often originating from an eight-pointed star, with interlacing bands forming a repeating network of stars, polygons, and crosses. This geometric vocabulary was a core component of Marinid artistic identity, seen in cedarwood carvings, stucco, and *zellij* tilework. **Arabesques** (fluid, organic patterns of scrolling foliage) filled the polygonal spaces within geometric interlace, providing a dynamic contrast.
