Stylized floral motifs were also incorporated, but these were abstract and integrated into scrollwork, differing from the more naturalistic floral designs of Ottoman art that gained influence only from the 17th century.

These intricate designs were brought to life using **tooling and gilding**. **Blind tooling** involved impressing patterns into dampened leather with heated metal tools, creating darker, burnished, permanent impressions. For luxury bindings, **gold tooling (gilding)** was employed, where a blind-tooled impression was coated with an adhesive (like egg white), covered with gold leaf, and then re-impressed with a heated tool to permanently adhere the gold. This technique of gold tooling is historically believed to have developed in North Africa before spreading to Mamluk Egypt, Persia, and eventually Europe. In some deluxe examples, particularly those influenced by the Mamluk tradition, gilded designs were subtly embellished with small areas of painted light blue pigment.

The intellectual content of books during this period was vast, reflecting a golden age of scholarship. A curated list of 30 significant texts available around 1550 includes foundational religious works like the **Qur'an** and **Hadith collections** (e.g., *·π¢a·∏•ƒ´·∏• al-BukhƒÅrƒ´*, *al-Muwa·π≠·π≠a æ*). **Biographies of the Prophet** (e.g., Qadi 'Iyad's *al-ShifƒÅ æ*) and **devotional texts** (e.g., al-Jazuli's *DalƒÅ æil al-KhayrƒÅt*, al-Busiri's *Qa·π£ƒ´dat al-Burdah*) were highly popular. **Historical and sociological works** included Ibn Khaldun's monumental *al-Muqaddimah* and chronicles of Moroccan history like Ibn Abi Zar's *Raw·∏ç al-Qir·π≠ƒÅs*. **Travelogues** by figures like Ibn Battuta (*Tu·∏•fat an-Nu·∫ì·∫ìƒÅr*) and **geographical works** by al-Idrisi (*Nuzhat al-MushtƒÅq*) were also key. **Legal texts** of the Maliki school, such as al-Wansharisi's *al-Mi øyƒÅr al-Mu ørib* and Khalil ibn Ishaq's *al-Mukhta·π£ar*, were foundational. **Scientific and philosophical works** covered mathematics (Ibn al-Banna's *Talkhƒ´·π£ a ømƒÅl al-·∏•isƒÅb*, al-Khwarizmi's *al-KitƒÅb al-mukhta·π£ar*, Euclid's *Elements*), astronomy (al-Murrakushi's *JƒÅmi ø al-mabƒÅdi æ...*, Ptolemy's *Almagest*), medicine (Ibn Sina's *al-QƒÅn≈´n fƒ´ al-·π¨ibb*, Ibn al-Baytar's *KitƒÅb al-jƒÅmi ø...*), and philosophy (Ibn Rushd's commentaries on Aristotle, Ibn Tufayl's *·∏§ayy ibn Yaq·∫ìƒÅn*). Theological, Sufi, and literary works by figures like al-Ghazali and al-Hariri, and grammar primers like Ibn 'Ajarrum's *al-ƒÄjurr≈´miyya*, further filled these libraries.

For modern artisans seeking to recreate these historical books, specific profiles guide historically faithful reproductions. A **Royal Marinid Qur'an (c. 1300-1350)** would feature high-quality parchment, large formal Maghrebi cursive with gold Kufic headings, lavish illumination including a double-page frontispiece, thick wooden boards covered in fine dyed Morocco goatskin, elaborate full-cover gold tooling with eight-pointed star geometric interlace, and heavy brass or bronze clasps. A **Scholar's Legal Text (c. 1450-1500)** would use good quality paper, functional Maghrebi script with red ink highlights, stiff pasteboard covers in sturdy Morocco or calfskin, entirely blind tooling with a central lozenge or mandorla medallion, and rely solely on the fore-edge flap for closure without clasps. A **Sufi's Devotional Book (c. 1470-1550)**, in a smaller portable paper format, would have clear Maghrebi cursive, modest gold illumination, pasteboard covers in good quality Morocco leather (perhaps green), and a tasteful mix of blind and gold tooling, possibly with arabesque elements, and no clasps.

In the mid-16th century, Morocco's geopolitical landscape was significantly shaped by the ascendancy of the Saadian dynasty. Having overthrown the Wattasids by 1554, the Saadians unified the country, successfully resisted the Ottoman Empire's expansion, and established Morocco as the sole North African power outside Ottoman control. This burgeoning influence was projected through a modernized military and command over lucrative trans-Saharan trade routes. The Saadian Sultanate's military might, economic prosperity, and symbolic authority were deeply intertwined with the animal kingdom that inhabited its domain. Three key animals played critical roles: the Barb horse and the Dromedary camel served as the living engines of the Saadian state, while the Atlas lion, though receding into the wild, functioned as a potent, living emblem of royal power and a deep connection to regional heritage. The formidable but elusive Atlas bear was also part of this rich ecological backdrop.

The Barb horse was an indispensable asset to the Saadian military. An ancient breed, likely tracing its lineage back 4,000 years, it was the quintessential mount of Berber tribes for millennia, with its name derived from the Barbary Coast itself. It is a relatively small but powerfully built horse, typically 13.3 to 15.2 hands high, characterized by a strong front end, a short robust back, high withers, and a low-set tail. Its facial profile is straight or slightly convex, distinguishing it from the Arabian horse. A notable genetic trait, potentially shared with the Arabian, is the presence of five lumbar vertebrae instead of the usual six, contributing to its exceptional stamina and weight-carrying ability. Beyond its physical form, the Barb possessed a "golden character," combining "hot-blooded" sensitivity with remarkable controllability, intelligence, patience, and docility. It was exceptionally trainable, hardy, sure-footed, and endured long distances with minimal food or water, capable of sprinting over short distances.

The Barb's temperament was crucial for the Saadian army's military revolution, which integrated gunpowder weapons like arquebuses and cannons into traditional tribal equestrian warfare. Unlike the Songhai Empire's cattle, which stampeded in terror at the Battle of Tondibi in 1591 due to the noise of Saadian firearms, the Barb's calm nature allowed the Saadians to successfully fuse cavalry mobility with modern firepower, giving them a decisive tactical advantage. Leo Africanus, a contemporary diplomat and geographer, noted that "good horses" for warfare and royal use were specifically sourced from Barbary. The Barb horse was more than a military tool; it was a symbol of Moroccan national heritage, vividly expressed in the *Tbourida* (Fantasia), an equestrian spectacle dating to the 16th century. Its influence extended globally, playing a foundational role in developing Iberian breeds like the Andalusian and Lusitano, and significantly contributing to the English Thoroughbred. King Henry VIII of England even imported Barbary horses in the 16th century to improve his royal stables.

If the Barb horse was the tactical soul, the Dromedary camel (*Camelus dromedarius*) was the strategic backbone of the Saadian state. This one-humped camel was uniquely capable of facilitating large-scale, long-distance movement across the Sahara Desert, transforming it into a conduit for commerce and conquest. The Dromedary is a masterpiece of biological engineering, adapted to arid habitats with features like two rows of long eyelashes, bushy eyebrows, closable nostrils, thick rubbery lips for thorny plants, and wide, soft feet for navigating sand. Internally, its hump stores up to 36 kg of fat, serving as a vital energy and water reserve. A thirsty camel can drink over 100 liters of water in ten minutes, aided by unique oval-shaped red blood cells that absorb this intake without rupturing. These adaptations made the Dromedary the engine of trans-Saharan caravan routes for centuries, linking Moroccan trade hubs like Sijilmasa to West African empires, transporting goods like salt, textiles, and manufactured items south in exchange for gold, ivory, and slaves.

The strategic importance of the camel to the Saadian state is best illustrated by Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur's 1591 invasion of the Songhai Empire. Driven by the desire to control the trans-Saharan gold and slave trade to finance his state-building projects, al-Mansur faced the immense logistical challenge of moving an army across the Sahara. This feat was accomplished with an enormous logistical train of an estimated 8,000 Dromedary camels, which transported thousands of soldiers, their food, water, and modern weaponry, including heavy arquebuses and cannons, over a grueling 135-day march. The camel thus functioned as a mobile logistics platform for heavy artillery, allowing the Saadians to project a technologically superior force across the continent and achieve complete strategic surprise against the Songhai ruler, who was unprepared for a desert attack. This campaign demonstrated how the camel transformed the Sahara into a viable theater of military operations, weaponizing logistics to extend the reach of the Moroccan state and fulfill its imperial ambitions.

While horses and camels were instruments of power, the Atlas lion (*Panthera leo leo*) was the living symbol of Saadian authority. This formidable predator, native to Morocco's mountains and forests, was physically distinct from its sub-Saharan relatives, being one of the largest lion subspecies with reports of males weighing up to 230 kg, and some 19th-century hunter claims reaching 270-300 kg. Its most striking feature was a magnificent dark, shaggy mane extending over its head, shoulders, chest, and sometimes belly. Adapted to the temperate, often colder, climate of the Atlas and Rif mountain ranges and Mediterranean forests, it likely lived a more solitary life or in small family units, unlike the large prides found in sub-Saharan Africa. Its diet primarily consisted of native large herbivores like the Barbary stag, gazelles, and wild boar, though as human settlement expanded, it increasingly preyed on domestic livestock, escalating human-lion conflict.

The history of the Barbary lion is one of tragic decline. Fossils dating back over 100,000 years near Essaouira indicate its ancient presence. The first major blow to its population occurred during the Roman Empire, when thousands were captured for gladiatorial games (*venationes*) in arenas like the Colosseum. By the mid-16th century Saadian era, the lion was scarcer but still a known and feared inhabitant of Morocco's wilder regions. Leo Africanus described annual pilgrimages to a holy man's tomb seeking protection from these great predators, indicating their continued danger to rural communities. The final chapter of its existence was swift and brutal, driven by the proliferation of modern firearms and systematic eradication campaigns encouraged by Turkish and later French colonial administrations. The last confirmed wild Barbary lion was shot in 1942 in the Moroccan Atlas Mountains near the Tizi n'Tichka pass, effectively vanishing from its natural realm, with its decline accelerating in lockstep with modern technology and colonial power.

For centuries, Moroccan sultans maintained royal menageries, with the Barbary lion as the prized collection piece. This tradition carried powerful dual symbolism: Berber tribes presented lion cubs as tribute, signifying their submission to the throne and the symbolic caging of the mountains' wildness. Conversely, during the 16th century under the Saadians, these captive lions were reportedly used as instruments of state terror, with prisoners and condemned criminals thrown to them in public displays of the sultan's absolute power over life and death. The royal lions were thus a complex political tool, embodying both fealty and justice. This royal collection forms the basis for the modern belief that the pure Barbary lion lineage may have survived in captivity, with descendants now found in zoos, notably the Rabat Zoo.

The physical extinction of the Atlas lion in the wild did not erase its presence from the Moroccan imagination; instead, it transformed into an enduring cultural emblem. Its journey from a symbol of 16th-century royal power to 21st-century national pride reflects Morocco's changing identity. The lion's status as a symbol of strength, courage, and royalty is ancient, with deep roots in Middle Eastern and Mediterranean cultures, appearing in Islamic art associated with kingship and revered religious figures like Ali ibn abi Talib ("Asadullah"). For the indigenous Amazigh peoples, it was an immediate and potent symbol of their homeland's untamed spirit. Saadian art and architecture elegantly navigated the tension between Islamic aniconism and the royal prerogative to use figural imagery to project power. While religious monuments like the Saadian Tombs were masterpieces of non-figural art, adorned with *zellij*, stucco arabesques, and calligraphy, the secular royal palace relaxed this rule. Notably, the monumental fountain in Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur's Dar al-Hana palace featured sculpted figures of a lion and leopards, a calculated political statement modeling his court on Al-Andalus, specifically the Nasrid Alhambra's Court of the Lions. This declaration visually positioned Marrakesh as the new Granada and the Saadian court as the inheritor of Moorish Spain's high culture. In the modern era, the lion was resurrected as a powerful symbol of Moroccan identity, with the national football team famously nicknamed "Lions of the Atlas" (*ÿ£ÿ≥ŸàÿØ ÿßŸÑÿ£ÿ∑ŸÑÿ≥*), linking the nation with the lion's strength, pride, courage, and majesty. This modern symbolism is deeply connected to the lion's extinction, largely carried out during the colonial period. Its post-colonial adoption as a national emblem represents an act of reclamation, symbolizing Moroccan resilience, independence, and pride, transforming a story of ecological tragedy into a narrative of enduring national spirit.

To illustrate the complex relationship between the Saadian dynasty and the Atlas lion, a conceptual scene for a narrative-driven video game is presented, titled "The Gilded Cage". The character is Zaydan, the fictional second son of Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur, who holds the prestigious but politically sidelined role of Keeper of the Royal Menagerie. Zaydan is depicted as a scholar and naturalist, caught between his genuine admiration for the animals and his duty to a court that views them as mere political tools. His internal conflict between the wild natural world and the rigid palace mirrors the dynasty's tension between its rugged Berber origins and its cosmopolitan imperial ambitions. The scene is set around 1585 in a secluded courtyard of the newly constructed El Badi Palace in Marrakesh, featuring intricate *zellij* tilework, the scent of orange blossoms, distant Atlas Mountains, a gentle fountain, and the musky odor of the menagerie. Zaydan encounters a massive, dark-maned Atlas lion in an ornate, strong cage of iron and cedarwood. The lion lies still, its gaze fixed on the distant mountains, its lost home. Zaydan approaches with awe and sorrow, seeing not a symbol of his father's power, but a king in exile. He marvels at the lion's size and mane, recalling stories of solitary mountain hunters, feeling guilt that his father views this magnificent creature as a future spectacle, a tool of terror for condemned prisoners. Zaydan feels a profound kinship with the lion; both are powerful beings yet captives of the Sultan's ambition, the lion having lost its freedom, and Zaydan his purpose. In a symbolic climax, Zaydan rests his hand on the cold iron of the cage, whispering an old Amazigh poem about mountain freedom. The lion turns its head, and in its golden gaze, Zaydan sees a reflection of his own soul‚Äîa captive royalty, a tragic grandeur. When the lion finally opens its mouth, the sound is not a roar of anger, but a deep, resonant call of profound and inconsolable loss.

In conclusion, animals were active participants and essential components in the construction of the mid-16th century Saadian state. The Barb horse provided the tactical agility to integrate new gunpowder technologies and dominate the battlefield, while the Dromedary camel furnished the strategic and logistical capacity for imperial expansion across the Sahara. Both animals' legacies are etched into historical records and remain vital elements of Moroccan cultural heritage. The Atlas lion, though physically extinct, has undergone a powerful transformation from a living creature and instrument of royal terror to an enduring national emblem. Its loss, accelerated by later technologies and politics, created a void from which a new symbolism of Moroccan identity, strength, and resilience has emerged, with its roar continuing to resonate in the heart of Morocco.

Moroccan history spans hundreds of thousands of years, with archaeological evidence indicating hominids were present at least 400,000 years ago in Sal√©, and early *Homo sapiens* bones dating back 300,000 years excavated at Jebel Irhoud in 1991\. The Capsian period (7500‚Äì4000 BCE) is noted for Neolithic rock art motifs that are linked to geometric patterns found in Amazigh tattoos, suggesting a continuity of symbolic communication.

Recorded history began with **Phoenician colonization** of the Moroccan coast between the 8th and 6th centuries BCE. Phoenician traders established depots and settlements, including Chellah, Lixus, and Mogador, marking the beginning of foreign rule in northern Morocco. By the 5th century BCE, Carthage extended its hegemony over these coastal areas, a presence that concluded by the late 3rd century BCE, with indigenous Berber monarchs continuing to rule the hinterland. From the 3rd century BCE to 40 CE, Berber monarchs governed the territory. Juba II was granted authority over Mauritania by Augustus in 25 BCE, overseeing the Romanization of northwest Africa and the development of Volubilis into a major city. Following Juba II's death in 23 CE, his son Ptolemy struggled with tribal unrest, leading to a Roman suppression of a rebellion led by Tacfarinas. In 40 CE, Emperor Caligula assassinated Ptolemy, and Mauritania was formally annexed, becoming the Roman province of Mauritania Tingitana. The region was later overrun by Vandals in the mid-5th century CE and reconquered by the Byzantine Empire in the 6th century CE.

The **Muslim conquest of Morocco** occurred in the early 8th century CE. However, in 740 CE, the **Berber Revolt** led to Morocco breaking away from the Umayyad Caliphate, initiating a period of independent Berber states. Among these was the **Barghawata**, a confederation of Masmuda Berber groups, which established an independent state in Tamesna near the Atlantic coast from 744 to 1058 CE. They resisted for three centuries before being eliminated by the Almohads. In 757 CE, the Midrarid dynasty founded the Emirate of Sijilmasa in the Tafilalt region.

The **Idrisid Dynasty** was established in 788 CE when Idris ibn Abdallah, a descendant of the Prophet Muhammad who fled the Arabian Peninsula in 786 CE, was welcomed by the Awraba Berber tribe in Volubilis. This marked the foundation of the first autonomous Islamic state in Morocco. Fez, founded by Idris II, son and successor of Idris I, became the Idrisid capital and a sacred religious center in the 9th century. The Qarawiyyin mosque, often depicted as the "oldest college in the world," was established in Fez during this period. Following Idris II's death in 828 CE, the kingdom was divided among seven of his sons, leading to the formation of eight Idrisid statelets. Muhammad, son of Idris II, ruled from 828 to 836 CE, during which the division occurred. His brothers Isa and Umar also held sway, with Isa revolting and controlling coastal Tamesna from Chellah, and Umar (d. 835 CE) punishing Isa and governing Tangier. Ali ibn Umar inherited his father Umar's domains. Ali ibn Muhammad (r. 836‚Äì849 CE) inherited Muhammad's position and ruled competently, followed by Yahya ibn Muhammad (Yahya I, r. 849‚Äì863 CE), who enjoyed a peaceful reign. However, the Fatimids, with their Miknasa Berber allies, drove the Idrisids from Fez in 921 CE, forcing them to retreat to the Rif Mountains. Idrisid rule formally ended in 985 CE when the last ruler was captured and executed by the Umayyads.

The **Almoravid Empire** (1040‚Äì1147 CE) began with the efforts of Yahya ibn Ibrahim, a Gudala chieftain, who went on pilgrimage to Mecca around 1040 and sought a Maliki teacher, leading him to Abdallah ibn Yasin in the Sous valley. Ibn Yasin, the spiritual leader, founded the Almoravid movement, with Yusuf ibn Tashfin (d. 1106 CE) as its military leader. Abdallah ibn Yasin established a *ribat* to instill strict Maliki Islamic doctrine. Almoravid forces, led by Yahya bin Umar (d. 1056 CE) and guided by Ibn Yasin, captured Sijilmasa from the Maghrawa in 1053 CE. The Almoravids reunified Morocco and extended their power into Al-Andalus. In 1085 CE, Christian King Alfonso VI of Castile (r. 1065-1109 CE) captured Toledo, prompting the Taifa kingdoms of al-Andalus to appeal to Yusuf ibn Tashfin for aid. In 1086 CE, Yusuf ibn Tashfin led Almoravid forces to a decisive victory over Castilian forces at the Battle of Sagrajas (al-Zallaqah), halting the Christian Reconquista for a generation. By 1090 CE, he deposed the Taifa kings and annexed al-Andalus to his empire, uniting the Maghreb and Islamic Spain.

The **Almohad Empire** (1121‚Äì1269 CE) began when Ibn Tumart (d. 1130 CE), a Masmuda Berber scholar, declared himself the Mahdi in 1121 CE. He preached divine unity and launched a holy war against the Almoravids. Abd al-Mu'min (r. 1133‚Äì1163 CE), the first Almohad Caliph, conquered Marrakesh in 1147 CE, ending Almoravid rule and leading to the destruction of Almoravid mosques in the city due to claims of incorrect orientation. He expanded the Almohad empire, creating the largest empire ever ruled from Morocco. His son, Abu Yaqub Yusuf I (r. 1163‚Äì1184 CE), succeeded him. Another son, Abu Yusuf Yaqub al-Mansur (r. 1184‚Äì1199 CE), became Caliph and was the victor of the Battle of Alarcos. In 1153 CE, the philosopher and astronomer Ibn Rushd (d. 1198 CE) began his astronomical observations in Marrakech, associated with the Almohad court. The second Kutubiyya Mosque, larger and with a huge minaret, was completed in Marrakesh around 1158 CE. In 1184 CE, Abu Yaqub Yusuf I built the Giralda, the minaret of the mosque in Seville. Muhammad al-Nasir (r. 1199‚Äì1213 CE), son of al-Mansur, suffered a decisive defeat at the Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa in 1212 CE against a coalition of Christian kingdoms in Spain, marking the beginning of the end for Muslim Spain and Almohad power in al-Andalus. His young son, Yusuf II "al-Mustansir" (r. 1213‚Äì1224 CE), was appointed caliph at age ten. The Almohad empire began to fragment, with Ifriqiya breaking away under Abu Zakariyya al-Hafs in 1230 CE. The Zayyanid state was founded in Tlemcen by Yaghmurasan ibn Abd al-Wadid in 1235 CE. The last Almohad caliph was killed in Marrakesh by the Marinids in 1269 CE, ending the dynasty.

During this broader period, renowned scholars contributed significantly. Abd al-Wahid Al-Murrakushi (born 1185 CE), a historian, studied in Marrakech, Fes, and Spain before moving to Egypt. He completed a history of the Almohad dynasty, *Kitab al-mu‚Äôjib fi talkhis akhbar ahl al-Maghrib*, in 1224 CE. His main scientific work was *Jami‚Äô al-Mabadi‚Äô wa-‚Äòl-ghayat fi ‚Äòilm al-miqat*, a compilation on astronomical instruments, trigonometry, and gnomonics. Ibn al-Banna (d. 1321 CE), a great Moroccan mathematician and astronomer from Marrakech, authored *Talkhƒ´·π£ a ømƒÅl al-·∏•isab* and *Tanbih al-albab*, the latter addressing mathematical problems in everyday life. Al-Idrisi (d. 1166 CE), a renowned geographer born in Ceuta, studied in Cordoba and created a scientifically accurate silver planisphere, authoring *Nuzhat al-MushtƒÅq...* ("Roger's Book"). Ibn Battuta (d. 1369 CE), a famous Moroccan traveler, documented his extensive journeys across Africa and Asia in his *Rihla* (travelogue). Ibn Khaldun (d. 1406 CE), though born in Tunis, was a major intellectual figure of the Marinid era who wrote *al-Muqaddimah*, laying the foundations of sociology and philosophy of history.

The **Marinid Dynasty** (c. 1244-1465 CE) took control of Fez in 1245 CE and eventually killed the last Almohad caliph in Marrakesh in 1269 CE, ending the Almohad dynasty. The Marinids ruled Morocco, establishing the *madrasa* system and fostering a golden age of culture in Fez. The first Marinid *madrasa* was built by Abu Yusuf Ya'qub between 1270 and 1280 CE. Fez Jdid was founded by the Marinids in 1276 CE as their administrative and military center. They occupied Spanish towns like Rota, Tarifa, and Gibraltar in 1294 CE to control trade in the Strait of Gibraltar. The Al-Attarine Madrasa, a masterpiece of Hispano-Moorish style, was built in Fez between 1323 and 1325 CE. The Bou Inania Madrasa, another masterpiece, was built in Fez between 1351 and 1356 CE. Water-clocks were significant; Ibn al-Habbak al-Tilimsani built one in the minaret of the Qarawiyyin Mosque in Fez in 1286/87 CE, which was restored in 1346-48 CE by Abu Abdallah al-'Arabi. Another weight-powered water clock was constructed next to the Abu 'Inaniyya Madrasa by Abu 'l-Hasan Ibn Ali Ahmad al-Tilimsani in 1357 CE. The Marinid decline began with Abu al-Hasan being deposed by his son Abu Inan Faris in 1348 CE, followed by Abu Inan Faris being strangled by his vizier in 1358 CE. Portugal began its expansion into North Africa by capturing Ceuta in 1415 CE. In 1437 CE, a Portuguese attempt to take Tangier failed, successfully defended by Abu Zakariyya Yahya al-Wattasi, though the Portuguese did occupy Ksar es-Seghir.

The period of **Marinid and Wattasid bookmaking** (c. 1150-1550 CE) saw significant activity in book production, including the use of parchment and paper (with Fez as a major center), Maghrebi calligraphy, and Morocco leather bindings with geometric interlace patterns.

The **Wattasid family** seized control of Fez from the Marinids in 1458 CE after a massacre. The **Wattasid Dynasty** formally ruled in Fez from 1472 to 1554 CE, but their power declined amidst internal strife and foreign incursions. Sayyida al-Hurra (c. 1440 \- after 1542 CE) was born in northern Morocco and successfully ruled T√©touan for over three decades (c. 1510-1542 CE). Her father, Moulay Ali ben Rashid (born pre-1471 CE), founded Chefchaouen in 1471 CE to defend against the Portuguese. He was of sharif descent and had learned military skills in Granada. Sayyida al-Hurra's mother was Lala Zahra, born in Cadiz, who converted to Islam after marrying Moulay Ali ben Rashid. Sayyida al-Hurra married Moulay Ali Al-Mandri (Governor 1504-1519 CE), the governor of T√©touan, in 1510 CE, a marriage aimed at strengthening ties and creating an ally against the Portuguese. Her oldest brother was Moulay Ibrahim.

The **fall of Granada** in 1492 CE led to large waves of Muslim and Jewish emigrants and refugees to Morocco. Spanish forces captured Melilla in 1497 CE, and the Portuguese took Massa in the south that same year. Portugal also established an early foothold on the Atlantic coast by capturing Mazagan in 1502 CE. Wattasid Mohammed al-Burtuqali (r. 1504 CE) succeeded Abu Abd Allah al-Sheikh Muhammad ibn Yahya as sultan. The Portuguese further expanded their presence, occupying Tangier, Asilah, and Ksar es-Seghir in 1471 CE. Ali Abu Hassun (d. 1554 CE) was the last Wattasid sultan, who briefly retook Fez in 1554 with Ottoman help before being defeated and killed by Saadian forces.

The **Saadian Dynasty** (1549-1659 CE) began its rise in 1510 CE when Muhammad al-Qa'im (d. 1517 CE) was recognized as leader of the tribes of the Sous valley. He moved his base to Afughal, the burial place of Al-Jazuli, in 1513 CE. After al-Qa'im's death in 1517 CE, his realm was divided between his sons Ahmad al-'Araj (d. 1555 CE) and Muhammad al-Shaykh (d. 1557 CE). Ahmad al-'Araj took control of Marrakesh by 1524 or 1525 CE. The Wattasids formally recognized Saadian rule over southern Morocco through the Treaty of Tadla in 1527 CE. Saadian forces defeated the Wattasid army at Wadi al-'Abid in 1536 CE. Muhammad al-Shaykh achieved a stunning victory by capturing the key Portuguese fortress of Agadir in 1541 CE. In response, Portugal evacuated less defensible positions like Azemmour and Safi, and significantly expanded the fortifications of Mazagan. Muhammad al-Shaykh became sole Saadian ruler in 1543 CE after exiling his brother Ahmad al-'Araj. Saadian forces conquered Fez in 1549 CE, ending Wattasid rule and unifying Morocco under Saadian authority. Muhammad al-Shaykh refused to accept Ottoman suzerainty in 1552 CE, asserting independent Moroccan sovereignty. Although 'Ali Abu Hassun, with Ottoman help, briefly retook Fez in January 1554 CE, Muhammad al-Shaykh conquered Fez again in September 1554 CE, definitively ending Wattasid rule. Muhammad al-Shaykh negotiated an agreement with Count Alcaudete (governor of Spanish Oran) for Spanish anti-Ottoman alliance in 1555 CE. Saadian forces occupied Tlemcen in 1556 CE. Muhammad al-Shaykh was assassinated by an Ottoman agent in 1557 CE.

Abdallah al-Ghalib (r. 1557‚Äì1574 CE) succeeded his assassinated father, Muhammad al-Shaykh. He unsuccessfully besieged the Portuguese in Mazagan in 1561 and 1562 CE. In 1564 CE, Spanish troops captured the Ottoman base on Pe√±on de V√©lez. Muhammad II al-Mutawakkil (d. 1578 CE) was a deposed Saadian sultan who sought help from Portugal. King Sebastian I of Portugal (r. 1557‚Äì1578 CE) invaded Morocco in June 1578 CE with a large army to restore al-Mutawakkil. Abd al-Malik (d. 1578 CE), uncle of al-Mutawakkil, overthrew him with Ottoman support and took Fez in 1576 CE. On August 4, 1578 CE, Saadian forces, led by Abd al-Malik and his brother Ahmad, inflicted a heavy defeat on the Portuguese at the **Battle of Ksar el-Kebir (Battle of the Three Kings)**, where King Sebastian, al-Mutawakkil, and Abd al-Malik all died. Ahmad al-Mansur (r. 1578‚Äì1603 CE), brother of Abd al-Malik, became sultan, initiating the golden age of the Saadian dynasty, marked by immense wealth, astute diplomacy, and military strength.

During Ahmad al-Mansur's reign, Portugal united with Spain in 1580 CE, making Portuguese outposts on the Moroccan coast Spanish possessions. Al-Mansur received a Spanish embassy favorably in 1579 CE, leading to strained relations with the Ottomans, whose symbol of suzerainty he reportedly trampled in 1581 CE. However, he was forced to agree to Ottoman "protection" and pay tribute in 1582 CE to stop Algerian corsair attacks. Saadian forces conquered the Touat oases in 1583 CE. Tensions with the Saadians decreased after the death of Ottoman Grand Admiral Ulu√ß (d. 1587 CE) and changes in Ottoman administration in Algiers, leading Al-Mansur to stop regular payments to Murad III. In 1591 CE, Al-Mansur launched a successful invasion of the Songhai Empire, resulting in the Battle of Tondibi and the defeat of Songhai.

After Ahmad al-Mansur's death in 1603 CE, a civil war erupted among his sons for succession, leading to fragmentation of the Saadian realm. Marrakesh changed hands frequently between 1606 and 1609 CE. Al-Ma'mun, a Saadian prince, sought refuge in Spain in March 1608 CE. Moulay Zaydan (r. 1609‚Äì1627 CE), another son of Al-Mansur, recaptured Marrakesh in 1609 CE with Ottoman, English, and Dutch weapons. He later became the sole reunified Saadian ruler. Abdallah al-Ghalib II, son of Al-Ma'mun, also claimed the throne and held Marrakesh. Al-Ma'mun ceded Larache to Spain in 1610 CE in a desperate bid for aid, shattering Saadian credibility. 'Ali Abu Hassun al-Simlali (leader in the Sous valley from 1614 CE) conquered the Dra'a Valley and Sijilmasa.

In 1609 CE, Morisco exiles in Sal√© and Rabat founded the **Republic of Bou Regreg**, which became a major piracy base. This Republic allied with Muhammad al-'Ayyashi, a religious warrior and governor, in 1615 CE. Jan Jansz (Murat Rais), a former Dutch privateer, became a corsair captain in Sal√© after his capture in 1618 CE. He led corsair expeditions into the English Channel in 1622 CE and to Iceland in 1627 CE, and the Sack of Baltimore in Ireland in 1631 CE. Internal fighting returned to the Republic of Sal√© in 1636 CE, with English intervention, and English ships bombarded the Kasbah in 1637 CE. The Republic of Sal√© fell under the control of the Dilaites in 1640 CE. Ahmad al-Abbas (d. 1659 CE), the last Saadian sultan, was assassinated in 1659 CE, ending the Saadian dynasty.

The **Alaouite Dynasty** began to rise in Tafilalt in 1631 CE when Mawlay al-Sharif (d. 1659 CE), the head of the Alaouite family, was declared leader to defend against the Dala'iyya brotherhood. The Alaouite Dynasty was formally established in 1666 CE. Moulay al-Rashid (r. 1666‚Äì1672 CE) founded the Alaouite Sultanate and conquered Marrakesh in 1668 CE. Mawlay Ismail (r. 1672‚Äì1727 CE), brother of al-Rashid, consolidated the Alaouite state, created the *Abid al-Bukhari* professional army, and moved the capital to Meknes. Sidi Mohammed III (r. 1757‚Äì1790 CE) was an Alaouite sultan known for encouraging European trade. Mawlay Sulayman (r. 1792‚Äì1822 CE) imposed restrictions on commercial activities with Europeans, leading to rural revolts in 1819 and 1820 CE, which found support in Fez due to his policies. Riots erupted in Fez in 1820 CE, and Ibrahim, son of Yazid, was proclaimed sultan. Mawlay Sulayman was defeated and captured near Marrakesh in 1822 CE. His nephew, Mawlay Abderrahman (r. 1822-1859 CE), succeeded him and enthusiastically encouraged commerce with Europe. Sidi Mohammed (later Muhammad IV, r. 1859-1873 CE), son of Mawlay Abderrahman, led Moroccan troops against the French at Oued Isly in 1844 CE.

**European influence** intensified in the 19th century. The French occupied Oujda in July 1844 CE and bombarded Tangier and Essaouira in August 1844 CE, leading to a Moroccan defeat at the Battle of Oued Isly. Spanish troops occupied the Chafarinas islands in 1848 CE. The Spanish-Moroccan War began in 1859 CE over a minor border incident near Melilla, with Spanish troops taking Tetuan in February 1860 CE. A rebellion in Fez was sparked in 1873 CE by Madani Bennis buying the right to collect the *maks* gates tax. Mahammad Bayruk built a trading station at Tarfaya in 1878 CE in partnership with Donald Mackenzie, seeking European support. Mawlay Hassan led expeditions to the Sous in 1882 and 1886 CE, forcing Mackenzie out of Tarfaya. The Spanish occupied Villa Cisneros (Dakhla) in 1886 CE. Mawlay Hassan led a Tafilalt expedition in 1893 CE due to French advances in the Sahara. Rebellions such as the Rehamna rebellion in Marrakesh (1895 CE) and the Misfioua rebellion in the Atlas Mountains (1899 CE) also occurred. Ba Ahmad, the effective ruler of Morocco, died in May 1900 CE, and the young Sultan Mawlay Abdul Aziz took control.

Between 1903 and 1907 CE, Morocco experienced famine and insurrections led by El-Rogui (Bou Hmara) and Mulai Ahmed er Raisuni. Muhammad bin Abd al-Kabir al-Kittani (d. 1909 CE) called for jihad in 1905 CE, finding support among artisans and the urban poor. Riots erupted in Casablanca in June 1907 CE after French engineers extended a railway through a graveyard, leading to French bombardment and occupation. French troops also occupied Oujda in retaliation for the murder of a French doctor in Marrakesh. The **Hafidiya Civil War** saw Abd al-Hafid (r. 1908-1912 CE) wrest the throne from his brother Abd al-Aziz (r. 1894-1908 CE). Rebellions erupted in the Middle Atlas in January 1911 CE, and Mawlay Zein, another brother of the sultan, was proclaimed in Mekn√®s. Rebels besieged Fez in May 1911 CE. The **Agadir Incident** in July 1911 CE saw the German cruiser *Panther* sent to Agadir, bringing Europe to the brink of war.

The **French Protectorate** was established by the **Treaty of Fez** on March 30, 1912 CE, which Mawlay Abdelhafid was obliged to sign, transferring executive power to the French. Hubert Lyautey (1854-1934) became the first French Resident General (1912-1925), implementing "indirect rule". Morocco was under French and Spanish colonial rule from 1912 to 1956 CE. Mawlay Yussef (r. 1912-1927 CE) was the sultan chosen by Lyautey after Abdelhafid's abdication. The first Berber Dahir, establishing a separate legal system for Berbers, was issued in September 1914 CE. Between 1880 and 1930 CE, French ethnographers produced the "Moroccan colonial archive," which was a "formidable intellectual achievement" but marked by Orientalism.

**Nationalist sentiment** grew. Modernist Moroccans established the first "Free School" in Fez in 1921 CE to provide Islamic education in Arabic. Abdelkrim el-Khattabi (c. 1882‚Äì1963) led a fierce resistance against Spanish and French forces in the Rif War. El Raisuni disbanded his forces in August 1922 CE. Fighting occurred in Tache de Taza and the Middle Atlas in 1923 CE. A cell of the Zawiya secret society was established in Tetuan in 1924 CE. The Spanish army retreated from Chaouen in October and December 1924 CE, and El Raisuni died in April 1925 CE as Rifi forces occupied Chaouen. Rifi forces attacked the French Protectorate in April 1925 CE, advancing near Fez. Lyautey resigned in October 1925 CE after Marshal Petain took overall command of the French army. A huge amphibious operation landed Spanish troops near Alhucemas in September 1925 CE.

The **Berber Dahir** in 1930 CE, codifying customary law in Berber areas, sparked a massive nationalist protest movement. Nationalist leaders set up the secret society, Zawiya, with branches in many Moroccan cities. The first nationalist newspaper, *L‚ÄôAction du Peuple*, was founded in 1932 CE. Abdelkhalek Torres became the nationalist leader in the Spanish Zone in 1935 CE. The Spanish coup, initiating the Spanish Civil War, began in July 1936 CE with the *Ej√©rcito de √Åfrica* in Spanish occupied Morocco. Forty-seven thousand Moroccans had joined the colonial army by 1940 CE when the French government surrendered to the Germans. The **Istiqlal Party** was founded in 1943 CE, providing much of the leadership for the nationalist movement. In January 1944 CE, the Istiqlal Party released a manifesto demanding full independence. The establishment of the UN in 1945 CE, praising universal equality, provided impetus for anti-colonial movements. Sultan Muhammad V (r. 1927‚Äì1961 CE) supported the French effort in WWII but later backed the nationalist movement. He delivered a momentous speech in Tangier on April 10, 1947 CE, appealing for independence and territorial unity. Nogu√®s was the French Resident General during WWII. Lalla Aisha, daughter of Sultan Muhammad V, gained popularity for her speeches with uncovered hair. El Glaoui, Pasha of Marrakesh and a "colonial collaborator," died in 1956 CE. The Aix-les-Bains conference was organized by the French government in August 1955 CE.

**Morocco gained independence** in November 1955 CE when Mohammed V returned from exile after an agreement with the French for an independent constitutional monarchy. El Glaoui died in January 1956 CE. The French zone of Morocco became independent in February 1956 CE. King Mohammed V declared Morocco's independence on March 2, 1956 CE. Spanish authorities returned the northern and southern zones of their Protectorate to Morocco in April 1956 CE. Si Bekka√Ø, former Pasha of Sefrou, became the first Prime Minister. Mohammed El Mokri (1860-1957), long-serving Grand Vizier, was not involved in the government after independence. Generals Ben Hammou Kettani and Mohammed Ben Mizian, high-ranking Moroccan soldiers in the French and Spanish armies respectively, joined the new Moroccan army.

Under **King Hassan II** (r. 1961‚Äì1999 CE), who succeeded his father Mohammed V in 1961 CE, Morocco experienced significant developments. A rebellion in the Rif in 1958 CE was crushed by Crown Prince Hassan. Ben Barka formed the Union National des Forces Populaires (UNFP), a socialist party, in February 1959 CE. Prince Hassan negotiated the end of American bases in Morocco in late 1959 CE. An earthquake destroyed Agadir in February 1960 CE. King Mohammed V sacked the UNFP government in May 1960 CE and appointed his son as minister of defense and deputy prime minister. Hassan II proclaimed Morocco's first constitution on November 18, 1962 CE, which was overwhelmingly approved in a referendum in December 1962 CE despite opposition boycotts. Major Mohammed Oufkir (d. 1972 CE) was aide-de-camp to Mohammed V and later a general. His family, including daughter Malika Oufkir, was imprisoned after his failed coup attempt. The **Hassan II Prize for Manuscripts and Archival Documents** was established in 1969 CE to collect indigenous records. Abdallah Laroui's *History of the Maghrib: An Interpretive Essay*, challenging conventional historical paradigms, was published in 1970 CE. Hassan II's Moroccanization policies in 1973 CE led to the departure of many Europeans. The **Polisario Front (PF)** was formed in 1973 CE, aiming for an independent Western Sahara. Spain withdrew from Western Sahara in 1975 CE. The **Saharan Arab Democratic Republic** was declared by POLISARIO in February 1976 CE. King Hassan II became chairman of the Committee for the Liberation of Jerusalem in 1979 CE. POLISARIO inflicted damage on Moroccan troops in 1980 CE. Rains failed in Casablanca in the winter of 1980-81 CE, leading to riots in May 1981 CE over price increases. The Moroccan defensive wall (Berm) was built across the desert in 1982 CE. The leadership of the Muslim student movement was arrested in 1984 CE. POLISARIO guerrillas were excluded from the former Spanish territory by new walls in 1985 CE. The collapse of the USSR in September 1990 CE reduced US interest in supporting dictatorial states. Unions called a strike in December 1990 CE, which turned into riots. GDP per capita was $1227 in 1997 CE, with half the population living on less than one dollar a day.

Upon the death of **King Hassan II** in July 1999 CE, **Mohammed VI** (r. 1999‚Äìpresent) became king. Sporadic demonstrations against the Moroccan government occurred in La√¢youne in October 1999 CE. Shaykh Yasin, Islamist leader of Al-Adl wal-Ihsan, was released in May 2000 CE. Former prisoners protested outside their closed prison in October 2000 CE. Islamists were arrested again in February 2001 CE. POLISARIO rejected James Baker's new plan in June 2001 CE for not including an initial referendum. Around a quarter of a million Moroccans were working legally in Spain in July 2001 CE. POLISARIO organized brief armed clashes in November 2001 CE. A new family code (Mudawana) was passed in 2004 CE. The **Moroccan Equity and Reconciliation Commission** was established in 2004 CE to document human rights abuses between 1956 and 1999, and its report was published in 2005 CE. The **Amazigh language** was incorporated into the constitution in 2011 CE. During the uprisings of 2011 CE, King Mohammed VI promised sweeping constitutional reforms for a more robust separation of powers and an independent judiciary. The **Archives du Maroc**, the first national post-colonial archives, opened in 2013 CE, 55 years after independence. The Right to Information Law (31.13) was ratified in February 2018 CE.

In recent years, Moroccan authorities dismantled an IS-affiliated cell in April 2021 CE. Organic Law 04-21, changing parliamentary seat calculation, was proposed and endorsed by most political parties in March 2021 CE. Omar Radi, a journalist, was sentenced to six years in prison for espionage and rape in July 2021 CE. Soulaimane Raissouni, another journalist, was sentenced to five years for sexual assault. Social media commentators Chafik Omerani and Saida El Alami were sentenced to prison for criticizing the government. Parliamentary elections in September 2021 CE saw the victory of the National Rally of Independents (RNI), the Party of Authenticity and Modernity (PAM), and the Istiqlal Party (PI). Aziz Akhannouch, leader of the RNI, became head of government. A defamation campaign against Mohamed Ziane was launched in November 2021 CE. In April 2022 CE, 79 persons were charged or convicted for publicly eating during Ramadan. Morocco‚Äôs Competition Council released a report detailing unfair competition practices in the fuel market in October 2022 CE. Moroccan authorities dismantled a terrorist cell in Nador and Melilla and arrested five suspected terrorists linked to IS in October 2022 CE. Mohamed Ziane (born c. 1941), former minister for human rights, was arrested without a judicial warrant and sentenced to three years in prison in November 2022 CE for criticizing the government. Justice Minister Abdellatif Ouahbi threatened to strip associations of the right to file complaints against elected officials.

A prominent **mythological figure** is **Aicha Kandicha**, a jinn-like female entity widespread in the Doukkala region, depicted as a beautiful woman with hoofed legs who seduces men. Local historians like Ali Risouni and Fatima Bouchmal have provided insights into figures like Sayyida al-Hurra. Fatima Bouchmal, who wrote information for the qasbah museum signs in 2011, attributes the lack of historical records on women leaders to societal attitudes.

In 1552, Morocco was politically fragmented, defined by a tripartite struggle between the declining **Wattasid dynasty**, the rising **Saadian dynasty**, and fortified **Portuguese coastal enclaves**. The Wattasids, ruling from Fez, were militarily weak and had pursued appeasement with Iberian Christian kingdoms, eroding their legitimacy and confining their control mainly to northern territories. In contrast, the Saadians, a Sharifian family from the Sous valley, galvanized a *jihad* (holy war) against the Portuguese occupiers, uniting southern tribes. Their military victories, particularly the capture of Agadir in 1541, established them as national heroes and defenders of Islam. By 1552, the Saadians, under Muhammad al-Shaykh, had made Marrakesh their capital (since 1524\) and conquered the Wattasid capital of Fez in 1549, effectively ending the old dynasty's rule. Although the Wattasids would attempt an Ottoman-backed recapture of Fez in 1554, their power was broken by 1552\. This conflict led to a formal partition, initially recognized by the 1527 Treaty of Tadla, which divided Morocco along the Oum Er-Rbia River, with Wattasids in the north and Saadians in the south. Even after Fez's fall, Saadian authority was still consolidating, leaving Morocco as a patchwork of shifting allegiances with profound economic consequences, preventing a single national economy and creating regional economic spheres.

The third power was the **Kingdom of Portugal**, which maintained a string of heavily fortified coastal enclaves, or *presidios*, functioning as independent city-states. Following the loss of Agadir in 1541, Portugal focused resources on key strongholds like Tangier, Ceuta, and Mazagan (present-day El Jadida). The fortress of Mazagan, in the Doukkala plains, was massively expanded between 1541 and 1548 into a state-of-the-art Renaissance military fortress with star-shaped bastions. These enclaves were also *feitorias* (commercial warehouses) designed to dominate maritime trade, representing a distinct economic system that interacted complexly with the Moroccan hinterland. Thus, a Doukkala resident in 1552 navigated a crossroads of three competing powers, each with its own economic interests, currency, and political agenda.

The political divisions were mirrored in the economy, with the Saadian-Wattasid conflict being an economic war between two systems: the Saadians controlled dynamic, high-value trade routes in the south, while the Wattasids presided over an increasingly isolated northern agrarian economy. The agrarian core of the Moroccan economy, particularly in the fertile Doukkala and Chaou√Øa coastal plains, was based on rain-fed cereal production (wheat and barley) and livestock (cattle, sheep, goats), providing food, wool, and hides. The social structure was predominantly tribal, a mix of indigenous Berber and Arab groups. The **Saadian ascendancy was financed by their control of trans-Saharan trade routes** in the south, linking Morocco to West African empires for gold, ivory, slaves, and crucially, salt. This trade provided immense wealth, enabling the Saadians to purchase modern firearms and artillery from European traders, a decisive military advantage that the Wattasids lacked.

Morocco faced a **monetary crisis** in the mid-16th century, primarily due to currency debasement and foreign coins (Spanish and Portuguese) flooding the market. The traditional monetary system included the gold *mithqal* and silver *uqiyyahs*. This created a chaotic, multi-currency environment and inflation, damaging the less fortunate. Money changers were essential for assessing the value of various coins. A historical misconception is that Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur initiated Morocco's sugar industry; in fact, the foundations were laid earlier. Early Saadian leader Muhammad al-Shaykh actively promoted sugarcane cultivation and sugar export from the Sous valley, making it the region's primary export by the late 1540s and early 1550s. This sugar was traded for European cloth and saltpeter (for gunpowder), funding the Saadian war effort in 1552\.

The **souk (marketplace)** was the vibrant heart of Moroccan economic and social life, functioning as a "frontier market" in Doukkala where conflict and commerce intertwined. Souks were organized by craft or commodity, with specific alleys for artisans like blacksmiths (*souk haddadine*), leatherworkers (*souk cherratine*), and spice merchants (*souk el attarine*). Doukkala markets offered local agricultural bounty: wheat, barley, bread, vegetables, pulses, olives, figs, nuts, live animals (sheep, goats, chickens), meat, dairy, eggs, raw wool, hides, and fresh/salted fish. Artisans sold hand-woven textiles (djellabas, hendiras), Berber rugs, leather goods (babouches, bags), pottery (tagines, serving dishes), and metalware. Goods from afar, crucial for broader trade networks, included Saharan salt, dates from southern oases, and spices (cumin, cinnamon, saffron). More cosmopolitan markets might also feature luxury goods from trans-Saharan routes like elephant ivory, exotic woods, and ostrich feathers.

In 1552, there was no single, unified currency in Morocco; the **Wattasid Sultanate, Saadian Sultanate, and Portuguese enclaves each minted their own coins**. The Wattasids in the north minted square silver *dirhams* (full, half, third, quarter denominations) and copper *falus*. The Saadians in the south issued their own silver *dirhams* and half *dirhams*, stylistically distinct from Wattasid ones, primarily for paying soldiers and funding expansion, as their gold *dinars* came later. The currencies were not officially interchangeable, but de facto circulation occurred in frontier zones like Doukkala, relying on money changers to establish fluctuating exchange rates based on silver content and political fortunes. Foreign coins further complicated the multi-currency environment. **Barter** remained a fundamental part of the economy, especially in rural areas, offering a hedge against inflation. High-demand goods like salt, cloth, or iron bars also functioned as commodity currencies. Portuguese enclaves used their *r√©is* (silver, copper) and *cruzados* (gold, silver).

**Trade with the Portuguese at Mazagan** was contradictory: official *jihad* rhetoric from the Saadians coexisted with local commercial interchange driven by survival and profit. Fortresses like Mazagan depended on the Moroccan hinterland for food, leading the Portuguese to cultivate relationships with local "Moors of Peace" (*Mouros de paz*) who supplied goods in defiance of the Saadian resistance. Moroccans supplied Mazagan with wheat, barley, meat, vegetables, horses, local textiles, hides, and wool. In return, the Portuguese offered European manufactured goods, notably high-quality cloth. Ironically, European weaponry (firearms, cannons, gunpowder) was also clandestinely imported and acquired by Moroccan forces, sometimes from English or French traders, and then used against the Portuguese themselves. Transactions involved a mix of Portuguese *r√©is*, Moroccan *dirhams* (Wattasid or Saadian), and direct barter, especially for certainty in volatile currency markets.

The social perception of Moroccans who traded with the Portuguese was not uniform. For *Mouros de paz* who formally allied with the Portuguese, trading was a treaty obligation and a survival strategy. Independent merchants, including Jewish traders, viewed it as economic opportunism. However, the Saadian state and religious leaders (marabouts) branded any Moroccan aiding the enemy as a **traitor**, as their movement was built on unified Muslim resistance. The economic and political dynamics of 1552 were not a permanent decline but a "crucible" for a new, powerful Moroccan state. Saadian control of trans-Saharan gold, their early sugar industry, and their use of this wealth for a modern army with firearms were foundations for their future success. This period set the stage for the country's unification under Muhammad al-Shaykh in 1554, the victory at Alc√°cer Quibir in 1578, and the "Golden Age" of Sultan Ahmad al-Mansur.

Moroccan history is a rich tapestry woven from threads of faith, ambition, tribal loyalty, and geopolitical struggle, reflecting a dynamic narrative of unification and fragmentation. Situated at the westernmost edge of the Islamic world, the Maghrib al-Aqsa, or "Far West," has served as both a sanctuary and a crossroads, a bastion of independence and a prize for empires. This report provides a chronological and territorial analysis of its ruling houses, from the foundational Idrisid dynasty to the enduring Alaouite monarchy of the present day. To fully comprehend this history, understanding its geography and the shifting borders and spheres of influence under each dynasty, including the unique administrative division between government-controlled lands (*Bled el-Makhzen*) and autonomous tribal territories (*Bled es-Siba*), is crucial. The tumultuous 16th century, a pivotal era of profound social, economic, and political transformations, is framed through the eyes of a fictional character, Zaynab bint Yusuf, an Andalusian refugee, to bring this period to life.

The **Idrisid dynasty** (788‚Äì974) marked the birth of the first autonomous Islamic state in Morocco, emerging from the political vacuum left by the Great Berber Revolt of 740-743 CE, which had successfully ended direct Umayyad and Abbasid rule but resulted in fragmentation. **Idris ibn Abdallah**, a *sharif* (descendant of the Prophet Muhammad) and political refugee, arrived in Volubilis in 788 and was welcomed by the Awraba Berber tribe, who recognized his noble lineage as a source of legitimacy to unite the region. This established a potent political model based on Sharifian descent that would later be successfully revived by the Saadian and Alaouite dynasties. Idris I's reign was cut short in 791 when he was poisoned, but his posthumous son, **Idris II** (r. 791‚Äì828), born to an Awraba Berber mother, continued his father's work. Idris II proved to be a visionary state-builder, founding the city of **Fez** on the banks of the Oued Fes in 808-809, which rapidly grew into the political, economic, and religious heart of the kingdom, fostering the Arabization of the urban population. At its zenith, the Idrisid state stretched from western Algeria to the Sous valley, establishing itself as the preeminent power over rival principalities like Nekor, Barghawata, and Sijilmasa. However, the unity proved ephemeral. After Idris II's death in 828, his son **Muhammad ibn Idris** (r. 828‚Äì836) divided the kingdom among seven of his brothers, creating a federation of eight autonomous statelets, which shattered centralized power and led to decades of internal rivalries. By the early 10th century, the weakened Idrisid statelets became pawns between the powerful Umayyad Caliphate of C√≥rdoba and the ambitious Fatimid Caliphate from Ifriqiya. The Fatimids, with their Miknasa Berber allies, drove the Idrisids from Fez in 921, and the last Idrisid ruler was captured and executed by the Umayyads in 985, bringing the dynasty to an end. The territorial representation of the Idrisid realm shows a unified interior under Idris II, which later fragmented into multiple smaller principalities.

The collapse of the Idrisids ushered in a century of fragmentation, dominated by Zenata Berber tribes, until the dramatic rise of two successive Berber empires: the **Almoravids** and the **Almohads**. The **Almoravid movement** (1040‚Äì1147) originated in the Saharan desert among the Lamtuna and Gudala tribes of the Sanhaja Berber confederation. Their spiritual leader, **Abdallah ibn Yasin** (r. 1040‚Äì1059), established a *ribat* to instill strict Maliki Islamic doctrine and martial discipline in his followers, known as *al-Murabitun* (Almoravids). Under the military leadership of **Yusuf ibn Tashfin** (r. 1072‚Äì1106), this religious fervor was channeled into conquest. They swept north, conquering Morocco's southern oases and plains, and in 1062, Yusuf ibn Tashfin founded **Marrakesh**, which became their new imperial capital. By 1082, they had subjugated all of Morocco and extended their empire eastward into western Algeria. In 1085, the Christian king Alfonso VI of Castile captured Toledo, prompting the *Taifa* kingdoms of al-Andalus to appeal to Yusuf ibn Tashfin for aid. In 1086, Yusuf inflicted a crushing defeat on Castilian forces at the **Battle of Sagrajas (al-Zallaqah)**, halting the Christian *Reconquista* for a generation. By 1090, he deposed the *Taifa* kings and annexed al-Andalus to his African empire, uniting the Maghreb and Islamic Spain under a single political authority for the first time. The Almoravid empire at its greatest extent (c. 1120\) created a vast, unified political space stretching from the northern banks of the Senegal and Niger rivers to most of modern Spain and Portugal, shifting the region's center of gravity from C√≥rdoba and Kairouan to Marrakesh.

The **Almohad movement** (1121‚Äì1269) followed, representing the zenith of Berber power. Founded by **Ibn Tumart** (r. 1121‚Äì1130), a religious scholar from the Masmuda Berber confederation in the High Atlas mountains, who preached a radical doctrine of absolute divine unity (*Tawhid*) and declared himself the *Mahdi*. After Ibn Tumart's death, his successor, **Abd al-Mu'min** (r. 1133‚Äì1163), transformed the movement into an unstoppable military force, capturing the Almoravid capital of Marrakesh in 1147\. Abd al-Mu'min embarked on breathtaking expansion, conquering the entire Maghreb from the Atlantic coast of Morocco to Tripoli in modern Libya by 1159, and by 1172, his successors had brought all of Muslim al-Andalus under their control. The Almohad Caliphate was the largest empire ever ruled from Morocco, a dominant power in the western Mediterranean. The reigns of **Abu Yaqub Yusuf I** (r. 1163‚Äì1184) and **Yaqub al-Mansur** (r. 1184‚Äì1199) marked the apogee of Almohad power and culture, patrons of philosophy and commissioning magnificent architectural masterpieces like the Koutoubia Mosque in Marrakesh and its sister minaret, the Giralda, in Seville. Al-Mansur's victory over the Castilians at the Battle of Alarcos in 1195 seemed to reaffirm Almohad military supremacy. However, in 1212, al-Mansur's successor, **Muhammad al-Nasir** (r. 1199‚Äì1213), suffered a decisive defeat at the **Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa** against a coalition of Christian kingdoms, a catastrophe from which the Almohads never recovered. Their authority in al-Andalus crumbled, and the empire disintegrated in North Africa as peripheral dynasties like the Hafsids, Zayyanids, and Marinids rose. The last Almohad caliph, **Idris II al-Wathiq** (r. 1266‚Äì1269), was killed in Marrakesh in 1269 by the ascendant Marinids. The Almoravids and Almohads facilitated a profound cultural exchange, with Andalusian art, architecture, literature, and administrative practices deeply enriching Moroccan civilization.

As the Almohad empire crumbled, the **Marinids** (1244‚Äì1465), a nomadic tribe of Zenata Berber origin, rose to power. They captured Meknes, Sal√©, and Rabat, seized Fez in 1244, and ultimately conquered Marrakesh in 1269 under **Abu Yusuf Yaqub** (r. 1258‚Äì1286), ending Almohad rule definitively and becoming the first Marinid Sultan. Unlike their predecessors, Marinid legitimacy was primarily military and political, focused on restoring order and continuing the *jihad* in Spain. The Marinid era is remembered as a golden age of art, architecture, and intellectual life in their capital, Fez. Their most distinctive contribution was the establishment of the *madrasa* (theological college), such as the Bou Inania (1351-56) and Al-Attarine (1323-25) in Fez, which are masterpieces of Hispano-Moorish style. These madrasas were a political strategy to create a loyal elite of scholars and bureaucrats dependent on the state, counterbalancing rural marabouts and tribal leaders. Despite their cultural brilliance, the Marinid state was chronically unstable after the death of **Sultan Abu Inan Faris** (r. 1348‚Äì1358). Real power shifted to their viziers, particularly from the related Wattasid tribe. External pressures, including the Portuguese capture of Ceuta in 1415, eroded their prestige. The last Marinid sultan, **Abd al-Haqq II** (r. 1420‚Äì1465), was murdered in a revolt in Fez in 1465, bringing the dynasty to an end. The Marinid Sultanate (c. 1360\) primarily centered on modern Morocco, marking a contraction of centralized power compared to its imperial predecessors.

The 16th century in Morocco was a period of intense conflict and transformation. Zaynab bint Yusuf, an Andalusian refugee in Fez, would witness the decline of the **Wattasid dynasty** in the north and the rise of the **Saadian dynasty** in the south. In Zaynab's youth (1505-1554), the Wattasid sultans in Fez held diminishing power, barely extending beyond the northern plains and widely seen as ineffective against the growing Portuguese threat along the Atlantic coast. Meanwhile, the **Saadians**, a Sharifian family from the Sous and Draa river valleys, emerged as a powerful force. Their legitimacy stemmed from their descent from the Prophet (*shurafa*) and their declaration of a *jihad* against the Portuguese, contrasting with Wattasid appeasement policies. Led by **Muhammad al-Qa'im** and his sons, **Ahmad al-Araj** and **Muhammad al-Shaykh**, the Saadians rapidly gained power, capturing Marrakesh in 1524 and making it their capital. The 1527 Treaty of Tadla formalized the country's division, with Wattasids ruling the north from Fez and Saadians the south from Marrakesh. Saadian prestige soared in 1541 when **Muhammad al-Shaykh** captured the key Portuguese fortress of Agadir, proving them to be the true defenders of Islam. With popular support, the Saadians marched north, capturing Fez in 1549\. The last Wattasid sultan, **Ali Abu Hassun** (r. 1554), made a desperate, Ottoman-backed attempt to regain power in 1554 but was defeated and killed, extinguishing the Wattasid dynasty and uniting Morocco under **Muhammad al-Shaykh**. This period was marked by a fragmented political landscape, also including Portuguese coastal enclaves and the Principality of Debdou.

After the final victory over the Wattasids in 1554, Zaynab and her family moved to the vibrant new capital of Marrakesh, witnessing the consolidation of a unified Saadian state under **Sultan Mohammed ash-Sheikh** and later **Abdallah al-Ghalib** (r. 1557‚Äì1574). However, a larger threat emerged from the powerful Ottoman Empire to the east, which saw Morocco as a rival and launched direct invasions in 1557 and 1558, briefly occupying Fez. This confrontation forced a profound **military transformation** in Morocco, as Saadian sultans modernized their forces by adopting Ottoman-style organization, hiring Turkish and European renegade instructors, and emphasizing firearms. This allowed Morocco to successfully repel the Ottoman threat and maintain its sovereignty, making it the only Arab nation in North Africa to remain independent of Istanbul's rule, and forging a distinct Moroccan national identity. Saadian society was a tapestry of diverse cultures, with the ruling family's Sharifian lineage at its apex, supported by religious families, Sufi masters, merchants, artisans, and a vital Jewish community. Women's lives varied, from urban women in harems to figures like **Sayyida al-Hurra**, the powerful female governor of T√©touan who commanded a pirate fleet and negotiated with European kings. The Saadian economy was robust and multifaceted, based on revitalized trans-Saharan trade for West African gold, salt, ivory, and slaves, and a large-scale sugar industry in southern Morocco, particularly around Taroudant, which relied heavily on slave labor. The wealth from trade and conquest fueled a great architectural revival, continuing the elegant Hispano-Moorish style with grandeur, seen in the expansion and redecoration of the Ben Youssef Madrasa in Marrakesh.

In her old age (1578), Zaynab would witness the events leading to the **Battle of the Three Kings**. After the death of Sultan Abdallah al-Ghalib in 1574, his son, **Abu Abdallah Muhammad al-Mutawakkil** (r. 1574‚Äì1576), was challenged and overthrown by his uncle, **Abd al-Malik** (r. 1576‚Äì1578), who returned from exile with Ottoman military backing. Desperate, al-Mutawakkil appealed to the young Portuguese King **Sebastian I**, who, dreaming of a crusade, agreed to invade Morocco to restore him in exchange for coastal control. In the summer of 1578, a huge Portuguese armada landed at Asilah. The two armies converged near Ksar el-Kebir: King Sebastian's force of nearly 20,000, including Portuguese nobility and European mercenaries, was strong in infantry but hampered by a large baggage train. Sultan Abd al-Malik's army of around 50,000 combined traditional Moroccan cavalry with professional infantry armed with modern arquebuses, many Turkish-trained. On August 4, 1578, the Moroccan army's superior numbers and mobility enveloped the slower-moving Portuguese square, turning the battle into a rout and slaughter. In the chaos, King Sebastian, al-Mutawakkil, and Sultan Abd al-Malik all died, giving the battle its famous name. **Ahmad al-Mansur** (r. 1578‚Äì1603), Abd al-Malik's brother, was proclaimed the new sultan. The victory was monumental for Morocco, ending the threat of Portuguese colonization, securing independence, and flooding the treasury with gold from ransoms, funding a subsequent "Golden Age". For Portugal, it was a national trauma that led to a severe succession crisis and its absorption into a personal union with Spain under the **Iberian Union** for 60 years.

The reign of **Ahmad al-Mansur** (1578‚Äì1603) is remembered as the golden age of the Saadian dynasty, characterized by unprecedented power and splendor, funded by Portuguese ransoms and astute diplomacy. His court in Marrakesh became legendary for its opulence, exemplified by the construction of the magnificent El Badi Palace. Al-Mansur's most audacious venture was the 1591 invasion of the Songhai Empire in West Africa, which, with a small force armed with modern firearms, shattered the Songhai army at the Battle of Tondibi, giving Morocco direct control over the gold and salt mines of the Niger Bend and earning him the epithet *al-Dhahabi* ("the Golden"). However, al-Mansur's state was highly centralized around his own authority, and he failed to establish a clear line of succession. Upon his death from the plague in 1603, his sons plunged the country into a devastating and protracted **civil war (1603-1627)**, splitting the Saadian realm along a north-south axis with rival sultans in Marrakesh and Fez. This period of *fitna* (strife) led to the complete collapse of central authority, with regional warlords, religious brotherhoods like the powerful Dala'iyya, and even pirate republics like that of Sal√©, carving out independent domains. The cession of the Atlantic port of Larache to Spain in 1610 by al-Ma'mun in a desperate bid for aid shattered the dynasty's credibility. This anarchy and fragmentation created the power vacuum that allowed for the rise of Morocco's next‚Äîand final‚Äîdynasty: the Alaouites.

As Saadian power crumbled in the early 17th century, the **Alaouites** (1631‚ÄìPresent), a new Sharifian family, rose to prominence in the pre-Saharan oasis of Tafilalt. In 1631, **Mawlay al-Sharif** (r. 1631‚Äì1635), head of the Alaouite family, was declared leader by the people of Tafilalt to defend against the Dala'iyya brotherhood. His sons transformed this local leadership into a national dynasty, with **Mawlay al-Rashid** (r. 1664‚Äì1672) emerging as the undisputed leader after sibling rivalry. Al-Rashid reunited the country through military campaigns, defeating the Dala'iyya and capturing Fez in 1666 and Marrakesh in 1668, thus establishing the Alaouite Sultanate. The Alaouite state was consolidated during the long reign of his brother, **Mawlay Ismail** (r. 1672‚Äì1727), a ruthless ruler who created a new, powerful professional army known as the *Abid al-Bukhari* (Black Guard) to pacify the country and drive out foreign forces. He moved the capital from Fez to the new imperial city of Meknes, which he transformed with massive construction projects. However, Ismail's death in 1727 plunged Morocco into another period of chaos and civil war (1727-1757) as his many sons contested the succession. Stability was eventually restored under his grandson, **Mohammed III** (r. 1757‚Äì1790), who brought the army under control and rebuilt the central administration. The Alaouite dynasty continued to rule an independent Morocco through the 19th century, facing increasing pressure from European colonial powers. This culminated in the **1912 Treaty of Fes**, which established the French and Spanish Protectorates, ending Moroccan sovereignty for 44 years, though the Alaouite sultans were retained as nominal rulers. **Sultan Mohammed V** (r. 1927‚Äì1961) became a symbol of the national struggle for freedom, and upon Morocco's return to independence in 1956, the monarchy was restored. In 1957, Mohammed V formally changed his title from Sultan to King, marking the beginning of the modern Moroccan monarchy, which continues to this day under his grandson, **King Mohammed VI** (r. 1999‚ÄìPresent). The Alaouite Sultanate during the pre-colonial period was characterized by two distinct zones: *Bled el-Makhzen* (land of direct government control) and *Bled es-Siba* (land of autonomous tribal regions), a system that defined the Moroccan state until the 20th century.

The year 1552 in Morocco was a period of significant political transformation, marked by the decline of the Wattasid dynasty and the consolidation of power by the ascendant Saadian sharifs. This internal dynamic unfolded against the persistent presence of European colonial enclaves, particularly the formidable Portuguese fortress of Mazagan (El Jadida) on the Atlantic coast.

The **Wattasid dynasty**, a Zenata Berber lineage, ruled from Fez from 1472 until their eventual demise in 1554\. Their reign was characterized by internal strife and external pressures, primarily from Portugal and Spain. A defining failure of Wattasid rule was their inability to protect Morocco's coastal territories from Iberian incursions, leading to the loss of strategic areas. By 1552, Wattasid influence was severely diminished, largely confined to remnants of their northern territories, and their legitimacy was all but spent. The last Wattasid sultan, Abu Hassun, briefly reclaimed Fez with Ottoman help in early 1554 but was permanently defeated by Muhammad al-Shaykh Saadi by September of that year.

In stark contrast, the **Saadian dynasty**, an Arab Sharifian lineage from southern Morocco, rose to prominence as fervent champions of *jihad* against Portuguese expansion, garnering substantial support from Sufi orders and local tribes. A pivotal moment in their rise was the capture of Agadir from the Portuguese in 1541, which triggered the immediate Portuguese evacuation of Azemmour and Safi. This success solidified Saadian legitimacy, and by January 1549, Muhammad al-Shaykh Saadi had conquered Fez, becoming the undisputed ruler of a largely unified Morocco. In 1552, the Saadians had largely cemented their control, rejecting an Ottoman diplomatic overture for nominal suzerainty, which underscored their ambition for independent, sovereign rule. Despite their successes, Mazagan remained a heavily fortified Portuguese enclave, posing an enduring strategic and military challenge to the newly unified Saadian state.

**Mazagan**, today known as El Jadida, was a critical Portuguese fortified colony on Morocco's Atlantic coastline, initially settled in 1502 with a citadel built in 1514\. Following the loss of Agadir in 1541, the Portuguese strategically reinforced Mazagan, transforming it into a formidable, star-shaped fortress between 1541 and 1548\. This ambitious project involved Italian, Portuguese, and Spanish engineers, resulting in a pioneering example of Renaissance military architecture in Africa. Its robust fortifications, including bastions, inclined ramparts, and a massive underground cistern, allowed it to withstand numerous sieges, notably a major assault in 1562\. The fortress, housing approximately 4,000 individuals and four churches, served as a vital base for controlling maritime gold and spice trade routes.

The **Doukkala region**, inland from Mazagan, was a vibrant agricultural heartland in the 16th century. It was historically home to a diverse confederation of Amazigh (Berber) tribes, with significant Arab migrations leading to cultural intermingling. The tribal structure was often organized into "khams khmas" ("five fifths") for tax collection and military conscription. The extended family served as the fundamental social and economic unit, though seclusion for women was less strictly applied in rural areas than in urban or elite contexts due to economic necessity. Rural society exhibited a clear social hierarchy, with influential saintly families (marabouts or *shurafa*), wealthy landowners, and local government representatives at the apex, followed by smaller landlords, local chiefs, and minor functionaries, and at the base, propertyless farm laborers (*haratin*) and former Negro slaves. Despite rivalries, a strong sense of community and solidarity, often formalized through tribal contracts overseen by religious centers (Zawaya), prevailed.

Daily life in Doukkala was deeply agrarian, with small, subsistence farms reliant on rainfall, cultivating cereals (wheat, barley), legumes, olives, grapes, figs, and sugar cane. Livestock rearing, particularly sheep and cattle, was also significant. Traditional rural housing included unique dry stone structures called **Tazotas**, found exclusively in the region, and homes typically built around a central courtyard. Nomadic groups used tents. Rural women were indispensable, actively involved in fieldwork, weaving wool, gathering wood, and fetching water, significantly contributing to the household economy. Meals were often communal, eaten by hand from a shared dish. Religious and social celebrations, such as Islamic holidays (Ramadan, Eid Al Adha), marriage customs, and celebrations for a child's first fast or Quran memorization, punctuated the year. Sufi practices, including *dhikr* sessions and prophetic praises, were regularly organized in Zawaya.

Morocco in the 16th century was predominantly Sunni Muslim, adhering to the Maliki rite, with Islam profoundly influencing daily life. **Sufism** was deeply embedded in rural society, providing spiritual framework and social cohesion. Holy men (*murƒÅbi·π≠≈´n* or marabouts) held immense spiritual influence, possessing *baraka* (blessing) and *karƒÅmƒÅt* (supernatural powers), often linked to shrines at venerated tombs. Popular beliefs and superstitions were also prevalent, including omens associated with household items and the perceived power of objects like agate stones. A prominent mythological figure was **Aicha Kandicha**, a female entity akin to a jinn, widespread in Doukkala. She was typically depicted as a beautiful young woman with hoofed animal legs, residing near water sources, and known for seducing men, leading them to madness or death. A compelling theory connects her to a historical figure from El Jadida (Mazagan) who actively resisted the Portuguese by seducing soldiers, who were then ambushed and killed by Moroccan fighters, transforming her into a powerful symbol of local resistance. Kandicha was associated with specific rituals and offerings like amber, honey, pearls, shells, bread, figs, and olives, with henna and incense used in her honor. Moroccan culture was a rich synthesis of Arab, Berber, and Andalusi influences, further enriched by Mediterranean, Hebraic, and African elements. Traditional clothing like the djellaba was widely worn.

The economic landscape of Doukkala was a dynamic interplay of local agricultural production, traditional crafts, and evolving trade networks, significantly influenced by the Portuguese presence and geopolitical shifts. Local markets, or *souqs*, were fundamental, operating on regular schedules and organized by artisan guilds in larger cities. A diverse array of goods was exchanged, including agricultural products (cereals, legumes, fruits, vegetables, sugar cane), livestock, textiles (wool, silk, djellabas, caftans), spices, perfumes, traditional beauty products (argan oil), handicrafts (pottery, jewelry), and imported goods like firearms and alcohol. While **barter** was common, the use of **money** was widespread, and haggling was integral to trade. Craft production, especially rug weaving by Amazigh women, was a major economic and artistic activity contributing significantly to household incomes. Historically, trans-Saharan trade in gold, ivory, and slaves was a major economic driver, but the 16th century witnessed a strategic shift towards Atlantic coastal trade due to deteriorating security inland, leading to the decline of traditional Moroccan inland trade centers. The **Jewish community** played a crucial role in transit trade, controlling gold markets and specializing in silversmithing, money changing (*sarrafa*), and textile production, primarily residing in urban centers.

The **Portuguese influence on the local economy** was complex and often detrimental. They imposed taxes on farmers, plundered crops and livestock, seized agricultural lands, and actively engaged in the slave trade, capturing Moroccans for forced labor. Despite this, some Moroccans cooperated with the Portuguese in trade, facilitating the exchange of goods. The Portuguese introduced new goods like fabrics, mirrors, knives, firearms, and alcohol, primarily seeking gold in return. The Saadian dynasty, in their struggle against the Portuguese, developed sophisticated economic policies, monopolizing sugar exports and imposing taxes on trans-Saharan trade to fund military campaigns. They also actively sought trade relations with European powers hostile to Portugal (e.g., England, Holland) to acquire firearms.
