In 1631, they sent a draft treaty proposing to return to Hornachos and hand over Sal√© under several conditions: they wanted compensation for replaced neighbors, municipal authorities from their own nation to prevent past injustices, and a limited presence of Old Christians (only necessary priests and friars). They also requested that the Inquisition not punish those born in Barbary for twenty years for not being imposed on the Catholic religion, and that their old privileges and estates be respected. They offered to deliver the fortress of Sal√© with its 68 cannons, their privateering ships, and intelligence, including correspondence with the King of England and papers from Amsterdam burgomasters. They also proposed stripping the Jewish quarter and other Dutch and French merchant estates, promising to hand over the loot to the King in exchange for 200 pounds of gold, and requested the return of children taken during the expulsion. This proposal was signed by four Hornachero leaders. However, negotiations failed, partly due to renewed internal fighting in 1636, which saw English intervention through a bombardment of the Kasbah in support of the indigenous Marabutus faction. After 1640, the republic fell under the control of the Dilaites.

The republic entered a period of **decline**. In 1641, the Zawiya Dila'iya, which controlled much of Morocco, imposed religious hegemony over Sal√©. By the early 1660s, the republic was at war with the Zawiya. Ultimately, in June 1668, Sultan Moulay al-Rashid of the Alaouite dynasty vanquished the Dilaites and ended the republic's independence.

**Piracy** was a defining characteristic of the Republic of Sal√©, beginning with the wealthy Moors from Spain who acquired ships. They targeted mainly Spanish vessels, giving 10% of their spoils to the Saadians before their rebellion. The Sal√© Rovers conducted extensive operations, including an expedition into the English Channel in 1622 led by Murad Reis. In 1625, they seized captives from Plymouth, England, and in 1626, five ships off the coast of Wales. In 1627, they reached Iceland, sacking Reykjavik and raiding Grindav√≠k in what became known as the Turkish Abductions, and attempted to raid Bessasta√∞ir. They also captured the island of Lundy in the Bristol Channel, using it as a raiding base for five years. Their fleet captured over 40 fishing vessels in the Newfoundland banks in two years, and Sal√© ships appeared off the coasts of Acadia or Nova Scotia in 1624\. In 1631, Murad Reis led the Sack of Baltimore in Ireland. European agents, including Dutch and French consuls, dealt directly with the Andalusians regarding piracy and commerce.

The **legacy** of piracy in Sal√© persisted even after the republic's demise, although it was in decline. Sidi Mohammed III's attempt (1757-1790) to revive piracy ironically hastened the city's decline, especially with the establishment of Mogador in 1760 as a more modern port. In 1818, Sultan Moulay Slimane officially ended the "holy war" and dissolved the Sherifian Navy, definitively concluding Sal√©'s role as a corsair city. In popular culture, the character Robinson Crusoe, in Daniel Defoe's novel, is depicted as being held captive by Sal√© pirates before sailing to freedom from the Sal√© river mouth. The anarchist writer Peter Lamborn Wilson also features the Republic of Sal√© in his 1995 book *Pirate Utopias: Moorish Corsairs & European Renegadoes*, viewing such pirate enclaves as early forms of autonomous, proto-anarchist societies that operated beyond government control and embraced unrestricted political freedom.

The **Saadi Sultanate**, also known as the Sharifian Sultanate, was a state led by the Saadi dynasty that ruled present-day Morocco and parts of Northwest Africa from 1510 to 1659\. This Arab Sharifian dynasty successfully resisted Ottoman expansion, making Morocco the only part of North Africa to remain outside Ottoman suzerainty, and established a model of political-religious legitimacy that continued under the later 'Alawi dynasty. The Saadian period is considered a significant chapter in Moroccan history, marking the beginning of a modern Moroccan national identity with more clearly defined territorial borders and increased international recognition.

The **Saadi dynasty claimed descent from the Islamic prophet Muhammad** through Ali ibn Abi Talib and Fatima Zahra. They were established in the Draa River valley by the early 14th century and later settled in Tidsi in the Sous valley. Their rise to power began in 1510 amidst weak central rule in Morocco by the Wattasid dynasty and significant Portuguese expansion along the Atlantic coast. **Muhammad al-Qa'im** was recognized as the military leader and political representative by the tribes of the Sous and Sufi groups in 1510 to oppose European presence. He moved his base to Afughal in 1513, linking the early Saadians with the followers of al-Jazuli and opposition to the Wattasids. Upon his death in 1517, his sons Ahmad al-'Araj and Muhammad al-Shaykh took over, becoming the true founders of the dynasty. Ahmad al-'Araj captured **Marrakesh** in 1524 or 1525, which symbolically became a spiritual and political capital for the Saadians. In 1527, the Wattasids recognized Saadian rule over southern Morocco through the **Treaty of Tadla**, roughly partitioning the country along the Oum Er-Rbia River. Muhammad al-Shaykh further solidified Saadian power by capturing Agadir from the Portuguese in 1541, leading to the collapse of Portuguese colonial power in the region and enhancing his reputation. After a conflict with his brother, Muhammad al-Shaykh became the sole Saadian ruler. He then conquered **Fes** in 1549, effectively ending Wattasid rule and making him the sole ruler of Morocco. This set the stage for a confrontation with the **Ottoman Empire**, which had expanded into Algeria. After initial skirmishes and a brief Ottoman installation of a Wattasid ruler, Muhammad al-Shaykh decisively retook Fes in 1554\. He was assassinated in 1557, reportedly on the orders of the Ottoman sultan, after refusing Ottoman suzerainty and seeking an anti-Ottoman alliance with Spain.

The **apogee of Saadian power and wealth** occurred during the 24-year reign of **Ahmad al-Mansur (1578-1603)**. He ascended the throne after the decisive Saadian victory at the **Battle of Alc√°cer Quibir (or "Battle of the Three Kings") in 1578**, where the Portuguese intervention was thoroughly defeated. The significant ransoms from captured Portuguese nobility, along with control of the sugar trade, filled the state's coffers, earning al-Mansur the title "ad-Dhahabi" ("the Golden") and allowing him to mint high-quality gold coins. Al-Mansur modernized his army along Ottoman lines, adopting firearms and artillery, and employed Turkish titles and terms. He insisted on an efficient state administration and was a patron of culture, commissioning the lavish **El Badi Palace** in Marrakesh and sponsoring poets, musicians, and scholars. His relations with the Ottoman Empire were ambivalent; he initially recognized Ottoman suzerainty but later asserted his independence, minted coins in his own name, and even proclaimed himself caliph. He also pursued careful diplomatic relations with European powers, notably developing an **Anglo-Moroccan alliance** with England to counter Spain, focusing on trade in commodities like saltpeter, cloth, and sugar, and even discussing a potential invasion of Spain.

Al-Mansur's major foreign military venture was the **invasion of the Western Sudan**, particularly the Songhai Empire, in 1590-1591. This was likely motivated by a desire to control the gold mines, increase access to slaves for the sugar industry, and elevate his claim as a universal Muslim ruler. The Saadian army, led by Judar Pasha, defeated the Songhai at the Battle of Tondibi, occupying Gao and Timbuktu. Although Moroccan control was tenuous and short-lived after al-Mansur's death, the conquered region annually sent riches, including gold, slaves, and ivory, to Marrakesh.

The **decline of the Saadian Sultanate** began after Ahmad al-Mansur's death from plague in 1603, leading to a **succession war (1603-1627)** among his three sons: Muhammad al-Sheikh al-Ma'mun, Abu al-Ma'ali Zaydan al-Nasir (Moulay Zaydan), and Abdallah al-Wathiq (Abu Faris). The realm fragmented, with Marrakesh and Fes often ruled by rival factions. Moulay Zaydan eventually ruled from Marrakesh, while Al-Ma'mun's sons ruled Fes. Al-Ma'mun's decision to cede the northern port city of Larache to Spain in exchange for military aid severely damaged the Saadian dynasty's credibility. The civil war also saw the rise of local religious leaders and chieftains, such as Abu Mahalli, who rebelled against Moulay Zaydan. The Saadian realm was nominally reunified under **Abu Marwan Abd al-Malik II** in 1627, but central authority continued to decline. Control over the Sudan territories was relinquished, leading to the formation of the Pashalik of Timbuktu, and the important sugar mills in southern Morocco also declined. Independent political entities and opposition groups emerged, including the **Republic of Bou Regreg** formed by Morisco exiles in Sal√© and Rabat, which became a significant piracy base. The **Dala'iyya Sufi brotherhood** in the Middle Atlas grew in power, establishing a new Berber state and even engaging in foreign relations, capturing Fes in 1641\. The last Saadian sultan, Ahmad al-Abbas, was assassinated in 1659, ending the dynasty, with the 'Alawi dynasty eventually conquering Marrakesh in 1668 to establish new rule over Morocco.

The Saadian period brought significant **social and demographic changes** to Morocco. Large waves of emigrants and refugees from the Iberian Peninsula, including **Andalusi Muslims and Sephardic Jews**, settled in the country after 1492 and the subsequent expulsions. These arrivals revitalized northern cities like T√©touan and profoundly impacted the Jewish community, increasing its population and cultural activity while creating ethnic divisions that later blended. The population also included **Christian European captives**, many working on construction projects or in armament production, and thousands of **sub-Saharan Africans** brought as slaves from the Sudan conquest. Additionally, **Turkish or Ottoman mercenaries and soldiers** were present due to regional geopolitics. Many of these new arrivals were tied to state operations and, after the Saadian decline, some played independent roles, like the corsairs of Sal√©.

Under the Saadians, particularly Ahmad al-Mansur, the **central government's authority became more absolute**, marking a new stage in the development of the **Makhzen** (the royal government). The status of the sovereign ruler became increasingly associated with that of a **sharif** (descendant of Muhammad), a concept that became irreversible under the succeeding 'Alawi dynasty. This era is also seen by some scholars as the **beginning of a modern Moroccan national identity**, with more defined territorial borders and international recognition, and the name "Morocco" becoming commonly used abroad to designate the country.

**Culturally, the Saadian dynasty refined the existing Moroccan-Moorish architectural style**, with some scholars considering it the last "renaissance" of this style. Notable architectural examples include the ornate **Saadian Tombs** in Marrakesh, the **Ben Youssef Madrasa**, and the Mouassine and Bab Doukkala Mosques in Marrakesh. These mosques were part of larger civic complexes with various institutions and amenities for local residents. The lavish **El Badi Palace**, famous for its imported Italian marble, was also constructed by Ahmad al-Mansur. The usage of **zellij** (mosaic tilework) became more widespread and complex, with examples from the Saadian Tombs considered among the best. From the Saadian period onwards, Moroccan art and architecture largely became "conservative," reproducing existing styles without major new innovations.

In terms of **manuscripts and calligraphy**, the Saadian sultans compiled large libraries, particularly during Ahmad al-Mansur's reign, acquiring numerous Ottoman and Middle Eastern manuscripts. The **Zaydani Library**, a major collection of royal manuscripts, was captured by the Spanish in 1612 and is now kept in the library of El Escorial. It includes a richly produced royal Qur'an from 1599, commissioned by Ahmad al-Mansur. Another richly decorated royal Qur'an, commissioned by Sultan Abdallah al-Ghalib and dated to 1568, is held by the British Library. While largely preserving Andalusi-Maghrebi artistic traditions, there was experimentation with eastern traditions, such as the systematized use of Maghrebi thuluth script and the incorporation of Ottoman floral motifs like the tulip. The Dala'il al-Khayrat, a collection of prayers for Muhammad by al-Jazuli, was also a popular text transcribed during this period.

The rulers of the Saadi Sultanate included:

* **Rise to power (1510‚Äì1549)**: Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman Abu Abdallah al-Qaim (1510‚Äì17), Ahmad al-Araj (1517‚Äì44), and Muhammad al-Shaykh (1544‚Äì49, after being viceroy in the Sous).  
* **Sultans of Morocco (1549‚Äì1603)**: Muhammad al-Shaykh (1549‚Äì57), Abdallah al-Ghalib (1557‚Äì74), Abu Abdallah Mohammed II al-Mutawakkil (1574‚Äì76), Abu Marwan Abd al-Malik I (1576‚Äì78), and Ahmad al-Mansur (1578‚Äì1603).  
* **Succession War (1603‚Äì1627)**: Key figures ruling from Marrakesh included Abu Faris Abdallah al-Wathiq (1603‚Äì06), Abdallah al-Ghalib II (1606‚Äì09), and Abu al-Ma'ali Zaydan al-Nasir (1609‚Äì27). Rulers from Fes included Abu al-Ma'ali Zaydan al-Nasir (1603‚Äì04), Muhammad al-Sheikh al-Ma'mun (1604‚Äì06, contested until 1613), Abu Faris Abdallah al-Wathiq (1606‚Äì09), Abdallah al-Ghalib II (1609‚Äì23), and Abd al-Malik al-Mu'tasim (1623‚Äì27).  
* **Reunified Rule and Decline (1627‚Äì1659)**: Abu Marwan Abd al-Malik II (1627‚Äì31), Muhammad al-Walid (1631‚Äì36), Muhammad al-Sheikh al-Saghir (1636‚Äì55), and Ahmad al-Abbas (1655‚Äì59).

The history of Morocco is intricately linked with the legacy of its **sultans**, who have governed the nation through centuries, profoundly influencing its identity, culture, art, and architecture. These monarchs have been pivotal in establishing Morocco as a significant player in African and global contexts, with various dynasties leaving unique marks on the country.

### **Historical Background and Origins of the Sultanate**

The origins of the Sultanate of Morocco can be traced back to the **8th century** with the establishment of the **Idrisid dynasty** by Idris I, a descendant of the Prophet Muhammad. Idris I founded the first Moroccan state in Volubilis, unifying local Berber tribes under Islam and establishing governance based on Islamic principles. The Idrisids laid the foundation for future sultanates by promoting Islam, developing a distinct Moroccan identity by blending Arab and Berber cultures, and establishing trade routes. Over centuries, the Sultanate adapted to challenges like foreign invasions and internal strife, maintaining autonomy through negotiations with regional tribes and foreign powers. The establishment of the Sultanate as a hereditary monarchy provided stability but also led to power struggles.

### **Major Dynasties and Their Contributions**

Morocco's history is characterized by several influential ruling dynasties:

* **Idrisids**: The first Moroccan dynasty, they set the precedent for Islamic governance, promoting Islamic scholarship and the Arabic language as the *lingua franca*, and developing trade routes.  
* **Almoravids**: Emerging in the 11th century, they unified the Maghreb and parts of Spain through military prowess and zeal in spreading Islam. Their architectural contributions include the Koutoubia Mosque in Marrakech.  
* **Almohads**: Succeeding the Almoravids in the 12th century, they emphasized reforming Islamic practices, expanded Moroccan territory significantly into modern-day Portugal and southern France, and left architectural legacies such as the Hassan Tower in Rabat and the Giralda in Seville.  
* **Saadians**: Ruling in the 16th and early 17th centuries, this dynasty marked a **golden age** by successfully repelling Portuguese and Spanish incursions, thus reinforcing Morocco's sovereignty. They also fostered a flourishing of arts and culture, especially under Sultan Ahmed al-Mansur, who commissioned the Saadian Tombs in Marrakech.  
* **Alaouites**: The current ruling dynasty since the 17th century, they played a crucial role in **modernizing Morocco** and maintaining its independence during the colonial period. Under Sultan Mohammed V and Hassan II, Morocco underwent significant reforms, modernizing its political and social structures while preserving cultural heritage.

### **The Role of Religion in Sultanate Governance**

Religion has been a cornerstone of governance in Morocco, deeply intertwining with the monarchy's authority. Sultans are regarded as both political and religious leaders, bearing the title "**Amir al-Mu'minin**" (Commander of the Faithful), which entrusts them with protecting Islam and its followers and reinforces their legitimacy. Islamic faith, particularly **sharia law**, has served as a foundation for Moroccan law and customs. Sultans historically used religious legitimacy to consolidate power, often aligning with influential religious leaders. For instance, Almohad sultans utilized religious zeal to justify military campaigns and territorial expansion, known for their strict adherence to Islamic orthodoxy. In contemporary Morocco, the monarchy continues to emphasize its role as protector of Islam, with King Mohammed VI initiating reforms to modernize religious institutions, promote tolerance, combat extremism, and foster dialogue among Islamic schools of thought. This intertwining of religion and governance has created a unique political landscape where the monarchy is seen as a stabilizing force.

### **Prominent Sultans and Their Legacies**

Three significant sultans have profoundly shaped Morocco's history, culture, politics, and identity:

* **Sultan Moulay Ismail (1672-1727)**: Known as "The Warrior King," his reign was marked by robust military campaigns aimed at consolidating power and repelling foreign influences. He successfully reclaimed coastal cities like Tangier from foreign control and established the **Black Guard**, a loyal military unit crucial for suppressing dissent and maintaining his rule. Architecturally, he transformed Meknes into a grand imperial capital with structures like the Bab Mansour gate and the royal palace. He also implemented a **centralized administration** to unify diverse regions, setting a precedent for future Moroccan governance.  
* **Sultan Mohammed V (1927-1961)**: "The Modernizer" was a pivotal figure in Morocco's struggle for independence from French protectorate rule. He became a **symbol of resistance**, promoting Moroccan nationalism and uniting various political factions, notably with his famous call for independence in the 1940s. After Morocco achieved independence in 1956, his leadership solidified his status as a national hero, and he worked to establish a constitutional monarchy. His reign also saw significant **social reforms**, including investments in education, healthcare, and infrastructure, as well as a commitment to religious tolerance and cultural preservation.  
* **Sultan Hassan I (1873-1894)**: Regarded as "The Architect of Modern Morocco," Hassan I focused on **modernizing the country** while preserving its cultural heritage. He implemented military reforms, establishing a modern army with contemporary weaponry and tactics to bolster national defense. He also prioritized **education**, establishing schools that taught both religious and secular subjects to cultivate a literate populace. Diplomatically, Hassan I skillfully navigated complex relationships with European powers, asserting Moroccan sovereignty and positioning Morocco as a respected player on the global stage. His commitment to cultural heritage is evident in his architectural commissions of mosques, schools, and public buildings.

The legacies of these sultans emphasize national unity, cultural heritage, social reform, strong leadership, resilience, and adaptability, continuing to influence modern Moroccan society.

### **Cultural Impact of the Sultans on Moroccan Society**

The sultans have significantly shaped Morocco's cultural fabric, leaving a rich and diverse legacy in art, architecture, literature, and identity formation.

* **Architectural Contributions and Landmarks**: The sultans commissioned grand palaces, mosques, and fortifications, also investing in urban planning and infrastructure. Notable examples include the **Hassan II Mosque** in Casablanca, a modern architectural marvel completed in 1993, symbolizing national pride. The **Royal Palace of Marrakech** exemplifies Moroccan grandeur with its intricate *zellij* tile work and lush gardens. Defensive structures like the **Kasbah of Ait Benhaddou**, built during Almoravid and Almohad reigns, are UNESCO World Heritage Sites showcasing architectural ingenuity.  
* **Influence on Moroccan Art and Literature**: Under royal patronage, Moroccan art flourished, blending traditional techniques with new ideas from trade. **Calligraphy** reached new heights, adorning religious texts and public spaces, with the Almoravid dynasty emphasizing its role in religious devotion. In literature, the 14th century saw the emergence of historical chronicles and poetry, with figures like Ibn al-Khatib and Al-Maqqari intertwining history with poetic expression. Literary salons at royal courts fostered intellectual exchange and creativity.  
* **Role in Moroccan Identity Formation**: The Sultanate has been crucial in shaping a unified Moroccan identity, acting as a symbol of authority and continuity and embodying the nation's values. Sultans historically mediated between diverse ethnic and cultural groups (Arab, Berber, etc.). During the colonial period, sultans like **Sultan Mohammed V** became symbols of resistance, rallying the population around sovereignty and independence. In contemporary Morocco, King Mohammed VI continues to balance modernization with historical significance, promoting unity and pride in Moroccan heritage.

The profound and multifaceted cultural impact of the sultans, through their architectural contributions, artistic patronage, and role in identity formation, continues to resonate in Moroccan society.

The **Maghrawa (ÿ®ŸÜŸà ŸÖÿ∫ÿ±ÿßŸàÿ©)** were a prominent Zenata Berber tribe, recognized as the "big bosses" of Zenata politics, surpassing the Miknasa and Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn in strength. They successfully ruled Fez and a significant portion of Morocco for approximately a century. Throughout their dominance, they maintained strong loyalty to the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba, effectively acting as their "Moroccan viceroys" until their eventual subjugation by the Almoravids. Their territories encompassed Taza, Fez, Sijilmasa, Tadla, and the western Maghreb. Initially adhering to Kharijism, they later converted to Sunni Maliki Islam under the sponsorship of the Umayyads. Their period of influence spanned from the late 8th century until the 1060s, when they were absorbed into the Almoravid empire.

The chronological lineage of Maghrawa leaders illustrates their rise and eventual decline. In their early phase, pre-Umayyad dominance, figures like **al-Khazar ibn Haf·π£ al-Maghrawƒ´** (late 8th century) established a Maghrawa presence in Tlemcen and the central Maghreb, sometimes allying with the Idrissids. **Maym≈´n ibn ZiyƒÅd al-Maghrawƒ´** (early 9th century) was linked with other Zenata tribes, the Rustamids, and early Kharijite networks. The Maghrawa's ascent under the Umayyads began with **Mu·∏•ammad ibn Khazar al-Maghrawƒ´** (early 10th century), who extended control over Taza and approaches to Fez, eventually leaning towards the Umayyads despite earlier alliances with Idrissids. **Mu·∏•ammad ibn al-Khayr al-Maghrawƒ´** (mid-10th century) was an important warlord who wavered between Umayyad and Fatimid influence.

By around 950 CE, **Mu·∏•ammad ibn al-Khayr‚Äôs successors** pushed the Idrissids out of Fez, marking the Maghrawa's direct control over the city. The "superstar" of the dynasty was **Zƒ´rƒ´ ibn  øA·π≠iyya al-Maghrawƒ´** (r. 978‚Äì1001 CE). He expanded Maghrawa rule from Fez to Sijilmasa, initially allied with the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba. Although he briefly leaned towards the Fatimids, he ultimately broke away, ruling almost independently. His conflict with Almanzor, the Umayyad vizier, weakened him, but he left behind a strong, though unstable, kingdom upon his death in 1001\. His son, **Khazr≈´n ibn Zƒ´rƒ´ al-Maghrawƒ´** (early 11th century), maintained Fez and parts of Morocco under Umayyad suzerainty, with his descendants later shifting eastward. **·∏§ammƒÅma ibn Zƒ´rƒ´ al-Maghrawƒ´** (mid-11th century) was the last powerful Maghrawa emir in Morocco, ruling Fez and Sijilmasa, but he was ultimately weakened by rising Sanhaja tribes allied with the Almoravids.

The decline of the Maghrawa occurred around 1050‚Äì1069 CE, as their rule fragmented into local rulers in Fez, Tadla, and Taza. They were eventually defeated by the Almoravids under Y≈´suf ibn TƒÅshfƒ´n around 1069 and were absorbed into the burgeoning Almoravid empire. In summary, the Maghrawa, especially under Zƒ´rƒ´ ibn  øA·π≠iyya, came close to establishing a "Zenata kingdom of Morocco". Their legitimacy was primarily derived from their alliance with the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba. They were constantly engaged in rivalries, fighting against the Idrissids, Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn, and Miknasa. Unlike the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn who were often rebels and the Miknasa who acted as mercenaries, the Maghrawa functioned as de facto kings of Fez on behalf of the Umayyads until their ultimate defeat by the Almoravids in the late 11th century.

The **Miknasa (ÿ®ŸÜŸà ŸÖŸÉŸÜÿßÿ≥ÿ©)** were a prominent Zenata Berber tribe, often characterized as "politically slippery" due to their strategic alliances. They are historically associated with the city of Mekn√®s, which derives its name from the tribe. The Miknasa served as crucial intermediaries between the Idrissids, Fatimids, and Umayyads throughout their period of influence.

Their **origins** trace back to the Zenata Berbers, and their territories extended through Taza, Mekn√®s, parts of northern Morocco, and at times, into Tlemcen. Initially, they adhered to **Kharijite Sufrƒ´ Islam**, but their allegiances shifted towards Sunni Islam depending on their patrons. The Miknasa's active timeline ranged from the late 8th century until the mid-11th century, when they were ultimately absorbed into the expanding Almoravid empire.

The **chronology of Miknasa leaders** reflects their fluctuating political landscape. In their early formation,  øIsƒÅ ibn Yazƒ´d al-MiknƒÅsƒ´, one of their earliest chiefs, briefly allied with the Idrissids while also showing loyalty to the Umayyads. Another early figure,  øAbd al-RahmƒÅn ibn Rustam al-MiknƒÅsƒ´, was linked to Kharijite networks. The Miknasa's prominence increased during the weakening of the Idrissid dynasty. **MasƒÅla ibn ·∏§abb≈´s al-MiknƒÅsƒ´** (d. 924 CE) was a pivotal leader who initially supported the Idrissids in Fez before switching allegiance to the Fatimids around 917‚Äì922 CE. He became the Fatimids' main general in Morocco, successfully overthrew the Idrissid ruler in Fez, and established Fatimid dominance in central Morocco. His son, ·∏§abb≈´s ibn MasƒÅla al-MiknƒÅsƒ´, continued his father's Fatimid allegiance, although the Miknasa began to lose ground as the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba launched counterattacks.

During the mid-10th century, Miknasa leaders like **·∏§umayd ibn Yasal al-MiknƒÅsƒ´** frequently shifted between Fatimid and Umayyad patrons, controlling the vital Taza‚ÄìMekn√®s corridor, which was a key route between Fez and Tlemcen. **Khazr≈´n ibn Fulful al-MiknƒÅsƒ´** (d. 1000 CE) was another significant Miknasa leader who also recognized both Umayyad and Fatimid suzerainty at different times. His descendants, known as the Ban≈´ Khazr≈´n, later expanded eastward to rule Tripoli in Libya.

The **decline** of the Miknasa began in the early 11th century, marked by an eastward shift and fragmentation. Al- øAskar ibn Khazr≈´n al-MiknƒÅsƒ´ controlled parts of Tripoli after 1000 CE. By the time the Almoravids began their rise in the 1050s‚Äì1060s, the Miknasa in Morocco had fractured into numerous local chieftains in areas like Mekn√®s and Taza. They were gradually absorbed by the Almoravid conquest.

In summary, the Miknasa were characterized as "political mercenaries" or brokers, often acting as the decisive factor between the Fatimids and Umayyads. While MasƒÅla ibn ·∏§abb≈´s represented their peak, breaking Idrissid power in Fez, they never managed to establish a lasting independent empire. Their decline, like that of the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn and Maghrawa, occurred because they could not resist the unifying force of the Almoravids.

The **Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn (ÿ®ŸÜŸà ŸäŸÅÿ±ŸÜ)** were a prominent Zenata Berber tribe, often characterized as "rebellious" in the medieval Maghrib. They were known for their shifting loyalties, sometimes aligning with the Umayyads, sometimes with the Fatimids, and at other times operating independently through raids.

Their **origins** are rooted in the Zenata Berbers. Initially, they adhered to **Kharijite (Sufrite) Islam**, though many later adopted Sunni Islam. The Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn established emirates in various locations, including **Tlemcen** (Algeria, 8th‚Äì11th century) and the **Sal√© (Shallat) & Tadla region** (Morocco, 10th‚Äì11th century). Their period of influence spanned from approximately **740 CE**, marked by the Berber Revolt, until their defeat by the Almoravids in **1066 CE**.

Several leaders shaped the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn's history:

* **Early Kharijite Leaders**: **Abu Qurra al-IfrƒÅnƒ´**, who ruled from around 740‚Äì760 CE, led the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn in the Berber Revolt against the Umayyads and established a base in Tlemcen. Another significant Kharijite rebel, **Abu Yazid al-NukkƒÅrƒ´**, known as "the Donkey Rider," was an ally supported by the IfrƒÅn, active from 928‚Äì947 CE.  
* **Tlemcen Emirate**: **Yazƒ´d ibn  øUmar ibn IfrƒÅn**, a successor to Abu Qurra, expanded their rule around Tlemcen. **Abu ·∏§amza ibn Yazƒ´d** continued IfrƒÅnid rule in Tlemcen, sometimes acknowledging the Idrissids. **Abu ZakariyyƒÅ æ Yafran ibn Abƒ´‚Äôl- øƒÄfiya** (died around 955 CE) was a rival of the Fatimids who briefly captured Fez but was later expelled.  
* **Moroccan Emirate (Sal√© & Tadla)**: **Ya ølƒÅ ibn Muhammad al-IfrƒÅnƒ´** ruled Sal√© in the mid-10th century, notably shifting his allegiance between the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba and the Fatimids of Ifriqiya. His successor in Sal√© was **Abu‚Äôl-KamƒÅl Tamƒ´m ibn Ya ølƒÅ** (died 1039), who was known for resisting both the Maghrawa and the Fatimids. The last prominent IfrƒÅnid emir in Morocco was **Abu‚Äôl-Hasan  øAlƒ´ ibn Tamƒ´m**, who ruled from 1039 to 1066 and defended Sal√© until his defeat by the Almoravids in 1066\.

Key characteristics of the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn included their **Kharijite (IbƒÅ·∏çƒ´/Sufrƒ´) roots**, which led to them being viewed as "heretics" by Sunni and Fatimid powers. They were adept at **guerrilla desert warfare**, leveraging their nomadic strength. Their strategy often involved **allegiance-shifting**, playing the Fatimids against the Umayyads to ensure their survival. Ultimately, the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn were subdued and absorbed by the **Almoravids**, who unified Morocco, bringing an end to their independent emirates. They remained perpetual rebels, never fully submitting to either Umayyad or Fatimid control, until the Almoravid conquest.

The **Emirate of SijilmƒÅsa** was a significant historical entity, renowned as the **"legendary gate to the Sahara"** and the northern terminus of the **gold trade route from Ghana**.

The emirate was **founded in 757 CE (140 AH)** by ** øƒ™sƒÅ ibn MazyƒÅd al-MidrƒÅr**, a Berber from the Miknasa tribe. His dynasty became known as the **Ban≈´ MidrƒÅr**. This powerful emirate endured for nearly three centuries, until its **fall in 1055 CE** to the rising **Almoravids**.

Throughout its existence, the Ban≈´ MidrƒÅr rulers of SijilmƒÅsa were characterized by their strategic political maneuvering, constantly **shifting allegiance between C√≥rdoba (representing the Umayyads) and Ifriqiya (representing the Fatimids)**. The chronological list of rulers, based on historical accounts, details a succession of leaders. ** øƒ™sƒÅ ibn MazyƒÅd al-MidrƒÅr** was the founder (757‚Äì772 CE), and his lineage continued through numerous successors, including **Abu‚Äôl-YaqzƒÅn Muhammad ibn  øAbdallƒÅh** (840‚Äì883 CE) and ** øAlƒ´ ibn Muhammad** (883‚Äì909 CE), during whose era SijilmƒÅsa frequently oscillated between Umayyad and Fatimid alliances. The dynasty concluded with **al-Mutawakkil ibn al-Mans≈´r**, who ruled from 1053 to 1055 CE. He was the last emir, captured and executed by the Almoravids under Abu Bakr ibn  øUmar.

By the 11th century, the SijilmƒÅsa Emirate was weakened by **internal strife**, which made it vulnerable and "easy prey" for the burgeoning Almoravid movement. Its eventual conquest by the Almoravids in 1055 CE marked the end of the Ban≈´ MidrƒÅr dynasty's rule.

This timeline tells the story of Morocco across more than a thousand years, from antiquity and early Amazigh societies through the arrival of Islam, to the rise and fall of dynasties that shaped the country's political, religious, and cultural foundations. It concludes with the Saadians, whose rule closed a major chapter in Moroccan history. The Alaoui dynasty, the current ruling family, is not included here as it deserves its own dedicated timeline.

### **Pre-Islamic Period (1000 BCE \- 578 CE)**

Morocco's pre-Islamic past, though often overlooked in written tradition, saw the development of mighty Amazigh communities with distinct political structures. The **Phoenicians** established an early coastal presence around 1000 BCE, notably near the Strait of Gibraltar, Lixus, and Mogador, founding harbors and trading posts that integrated Morocco into Mediterranean trade networks. As Tyre fell, **Carthage** took over Phoenician influence around the 4th century BCE, establishing strongholds along the coast.

Later, ancient sources began referencing **Mauretania**, an Amazigh kingdom formed through a federation of tribes in Northern Morocco and western Algeria, with Volubilis as one of its capitals. This period marked the beginning of entanglement with Rome, as King Bocchus I allied with Rome against Jugurtha between 118-105 BCE. After Bocchus I's death, his two sons divided the kingdom and aligned with rival Roman factions. Juba I's son, Juba II, raised in Rome, was placed on the throne of Mauretania by Emperor Augustus in 25 BCE, bringing Roman influences.

In 40 CE, after Emperor Caligula assassinated Juba II's son Ptolemy, Rome annexed Mauretania, creating the province of **Mauretania Tingitana** with Tingis (Tangier) as its capital. This marked the end of native Amazigh royal rule and the start of direct Roman administration. By the 3rd century CE (around 285 CE), Roman control weakened, and Emperor Diocletian pulled the frontier back to the coast, abandoning Volubilis and creating the "Limes Tingitanus." Local Amazigh rule resurfaced, with Roman presence reduced to military outposts and coastal hubs.

Between 429-534 CE, the Germanic **Vandals** invaded the Maghreb from Iberia but bypassed Morocco, where local Amazigh communities like the Baquates near Volubilis thrived independently. In 533-534 CE, the **Byzantine Empire** attempted to restore former imperial territories, defeating the Vandals in North Africa. However, local Amazigh groups continued to resist Byzantine control through sporadic guerrilla warfare. Byzantine influence in Morocco reached no further than Ceuta, meeting fierce resistance from local Amazigh tribes, notably led by the Riffian leader Garmul between 570-578 CE. Ultimately, all attempts to restore Roman control in the region failed. This poorly documented era, locally governed by Amazigh communities with lingering Byzantine influences, is known as "the dark centuries," setting the stage for the arrival of Arab forces.

### **Islamisation (647 CE \- 750 CE)**

The Muslim conquest of North Africa began under Caliph Othman ben Affan in 647 CE, with forces capturing Tripoli and Sbeitla. By 670 CE, Kairouan was established as a major religious and military center, anchoring the spread of Islam. Between 681-683 CE, Uqba ben Nafi al Fihri, a general of the Umayyad Caliphate, led a major campaign across the Maghreb, reaching Tangier and possibly Ceuta. His control was short-lived, but he helped lay the groundwork for Islam‚Äôs spread among local Amazigh populations.

In 698 CE, the Byzantines were defeated, and Carthage fell. The resistance of the Amazigh warrior queen Dihya ("al Kahina") ended with her death. Under the Umayyad Caliphate, Musa ibn Nusayr consolidated control over North Africa and pushed westward toward the Atlantic plains of Morocco. Arab forces formally began the Islamization of Morocco between 704-715 CE, gradually taking control of Tangier, Volubilis, the Atlas Mountains, Tafilalet, and the Draa Valley. Many Amazigh tribes adopted Islam relatively quickly but continued to resist Arab political domination through frequent revolts. By 708 CE, Morocco was formally integrated into the Umayyad Caliphate based in Damascus.

Between 710-715 CE, under Musa ibn Nusayr's command, the Amazigh general Tariq ben Ziyad (governor of Tangier) crossed the Strait of Gibraltar and launched the Muslim conquest of the Iberian Peninsula. Backed by local Amazigh leaders and many Amazigh warriors, he defeated the Visigothic forces, opening the door to Islamic rule in Spain. In the early 8th century, Kharijite Muslims, who promoted an egalitarian interpretation of Islam, settled in Sijilmassa. Their ideas resonated with Amazigh communities frustrated by Arab domination, leading to uprisings and the formation of independent Amazigh-led Muslim states, most notably Sijilmassa.

The Umayyad dynasty fell, overthrown by the Abbasids in 750 CE. This triggered further political instability in the Maghreb, which fragmented into autonomous regions. In Morocco, independent Kharijite kingdoms emerged, most notably Sijilmassa, and the region became politically detached from the authority of the central Islamic caliphate.

### **Idrisids (788-974)**

In the wake of the Abbasid takeover, the westernmost fringes of the Muslim world remained beyond their effective control. In this vacuum, an Alid fugitive, **Idris ben Abdallah**, fleeing Abbasid persecution, found refuge among the Amazigh tribe of the Awraba in northern Morocco in 788 CE. Welcomed near Volubilis, he was proclaimed Imam, founding the first Moroccan Muslim dynasty, the Idrisids.

Idris I expanded his control across northern Morocco between 789-791 CE, reaching Taza in the East, the Atlantic plains, and Tadla in the south. This marked the foundation of Morocco's first independent Islamic state, benefiting from alliances with tribes like the Meknassa, Ghomara, and Nefrata. In 791 CE, Idris I was assassinated by poison, likely on the orders of Abbasid Caliph Harun al Rachid, who saw him as a threat due to his growing influence and prophetic lineage.

After Idris I's death, his Amazigh wife Kenza, daughter of the Awraba chief, gave birth to their son, **Idris II**. He took power at age 11 in 803 CE, after a stable transition secured by Arab regents. Idris II shifted political favor from his father‚Äôs pro-Amazigh stance to privileging Arab elites, establishing a *makhzen* (state administration) and an Arab military guard. This created growing tensions with Amazigh groups, culminating in the execution of the Awraba leader. Idris II left Volubilis and founded the city of **Fez** in 808 CE. He built his residence (Dar al-Qaytun), Al-Qarawiyyin Mosque, and the commercial district (al-Qasriyya), attracting migrants from C√≥rdoba and al-Andalus by 814 CE. Idris II died in 829 CE under mysterious circumstances, likely poisoned. During his reign, he conquered Nafis, Aghmat, and Tlemcen.

After Idris II's death, decentralized rule began between 829-848 CE, as his ten sons governed their provinces independently. His eldest son, Sidi Mohammed, held only symbolic authority, leading to the fragmentation and unraveling of Idrisid unity. Yahya II, son of Sidi Mohammed, neglected central authority, further weakening the dynasty and sparking internal conflicts, leading to his deposition and a civil war among Idrisid princes. The Kharijite Mediouna, an Amazigh tribe, temporarily occupied Fez in the late 9th century.

In 909 CE, the Fatimid dynasty was founded, and backed by the Amazigh Koutama tribe, they eventually captured Fez. The last Idrisid princes, holding out in the Tangier region, were defeated in 947 CE by forces sent from Muslim Spain‚Äîthe Umayyads of C√≥rdoba, who were rivals of the Fatimids. The Umayyads installed a garrison in Sebta (Ceuta), bringing the Idrisid dynasty to an official end. This period also marked the beginning of Zenata (Amazigh tribe) dominance in Morocco, which would last until the rise of the Almoravids. The Idrisid legacy was more symbolic than imperial; later dynasties often looked to them for spiritual legitimacy, turning the idea of descent from the Prophet into a political asset.

### **Almoravids (1040-1147)**

In the 11th century, as North Africa remained politically fragmented after the fall of the Idrisids, new religious movements emerged. In 1039 CE, Yahya ben Ibrahim, chief of the Amazigh Juddala tribe, returned from Mecca transformed and recruited **Abdallah ben Yassine**, a Maliki jurist, to purify Islam in the Sahara. Together, they founded the Almoravid movement. Abdallah ben Yassine preached a strict, ascetic form of Islam, and after being rejected by the Juddala, he retreated to the Adrar region. There, he established a *ribat* to train warrior-monks who became known as *al Murabitun* (Almoravids), blending faith, discipline, and militancy.

Joined by Yahya ben Omar of the Lamtuna tribe, the Almoravids began unifying southern Amazigh tribes through conquest and religion between 1042-1046 CE. Their first major campaign in Morocco occurred when Sanhaja allies in Sijilmassa requested help against their Zenata rivals, leading to the defeat of the Maghrawa emir and opening the path for northern expansion. Yahya ben Omar, the military commander, died in battle in 1054 CE. His brother, Abu Bakr ben Omar, took command alongside Abdallah ben Yassine, who remained the spiritual leader. Youssef ben Tachfine, Abu Bakr's cousin, emerged as a rising military figure. Abdallah ben Yassine was killed in 1056 CE, but the movement was by then too powerful to stop.

The Almoravids steadily advanced northward, conquering the Souss, Massa, and Taroudant regions. They seized Aghmat, pushed through the Middle Atlas, and finally captured Fez in 1069, marking a major step in unifying Morocco under Almoravid rule. In 1070-1071 CE, Abu Bakr ben Omar left central Morocco to resolve tribal disputes in the Sahara. Before departing, he divorced his wife, Zaynab al-Nafzawiyya, and arranged her marriage to Youssef ben Tachfine. In Abu Bakr‚Äôs absence, Youssef rose in prominence and founded **Marrakech**, establishing it as the new Almoravid capital.

Between 1072-1077 CE, Youssef ben Tachfine accused the *taifa* emirs of decadence and gradually deposed them, unifying Muslim Spain under Almoravid rule. He reorganized the administration into four provinces, ruling a vast empire stretching from the gold-rich lands of the Sudan to the Tagus River in central Iberia. The two cousins, Abu Bakr and Youssef, reconciled and continued the unification of Morocco. Abu Bakr conquered the Kingdom of Ghana, spreading Islam into sub-Saharan West Africa, while Youssef consolidated political and religious unity in Morocco under Sunni Islam and the Maliki school of thought.

In 1086 CE, at the urging of Muslim *taifa* rulers from al-Andalus, Youssef ben Tachfine intervened against Alfonso VI of Castile's advance in Iberia. Reinforced by Andalusian troops, he defeated the Christian forces at the Battle of Sagrajas (Zallaqa), temporarily saving Muslim Spain. The Almoravid conquest of Morocco intensified between 1077-1083 CE as they expanded eastward and northward, capturing Tangier, Tlemcen, Oujda, T√©n√®s, Algiers, and Ceuta, securing a key launch point across the Strait of Gibraltar. Youssef ben Tachfine brought nearly all of Muslim Iberia under Almoravid control, uniting a vast empire from Senegal to Algiers and across the Strait into al-Andalus.

After the death of his heir, Youssef ben Tachfine delegated significant authority to the *fuqaha* (Maliki jurists). These jurists came to dominate state affairs and financial administration, gradually overshadowing provincial governors and the military elite, which contributed to administrative stagnation and decline.

A new challenger, **Ibn Tumart**, the spiritual founder of the future Almohad movement, began denouncing Almoravid rule from his base in Tinmal around 1120 CE. He accused the Almoravids of religious corruption, decadence, and moral laxity, causing the dynasty to lose credibility as the Almohads steadily gained followers. In 1129 CE, Almoravid ruler Ali ben Youssef (Youssef ben Tachfine's son) raised taxes to fund ongoing war efforts, sparking widespread discontent. He died in 1143 CE, leaving behind a weakened state.

Clashes broke out between the Almoravids and the rising Almohads, led by Ibn Tumart‚Äôs disciple, Abd al-Moumen. The Almohads continued their advance, pursuing and killing Tachfine ben Ali (Ali ben Youssef's son) in 1145 CE. They seized Oujda, Mekn√®s, and Fez. Ceuta and Sal√© also surrendered that same year, marking the collapse of Almoravid control in northern Morocco. In al-Andalus, Muslim leaders increasingly shifted their allegiance to Abd al-Moumen in 1146 CE as Almoravid authority crumbled. The entire south of Iberia fell under Almohad control, and the Almoravids lost all remaining claim to legitimacy. Led by Abd al-Moumen, the Almohads defeated the Almoravids and captured Marrakech in March 1147 CE, executing all remaining Almoravid princes and establishing Marrakech as their new capital. This marked the official beginning of the Almohad Caliphate.

### **Almohads (1147-1269)**

The Almohads began as a religious movement in the High Atlas during the early 12th century, challenging both the spiritual authority and political power of the Almoravids. Rooted in the Masmuda tribal world and guided by a powerful religious mission, they grew steadily from their base in Tinmel. Ben Tumart, the movement‚Äôs founder and self-declared "Mahdi" (religious guide), laid the religious and political foundations of the new order between 1124-1132 CE but died shortly after the Battle of Al Buhayra. After two years of consolidation, his chosen successor, **Abd Al Moumen**, was officially proclaimed caliph.

Abd Al Moumen captured Marrakech in 1147 CE, ending Almoravid rule for good, and assumed the dual role of Imam and Caliph, uniting religious and political authority. In 1148 CE, a major rebellion broke out in the Massa region, led by Ben Houd Al Massi, which was brutally suppressed. Launching a major campaign, Abd Al Moumen captured Tunis, Sfax, and Tripoli in 1159 CE. For the first time, the entire Maghreb (as it is known today) was unified under a single Amazigh Islamic authority. Abd Al Moumen crossed the Strait of Gibraltar in 1160 CE, fortified key points, and defeated Castilian forces near Badajoz, marking a new phase of confrontation with the Christian kingdoms of the Iberian Peninsula. He died in Sal√© in 1163 CE while preparing a major campaign into Iberia, leaving behind a vast empire stretching from the Atlantic Ocean to Libya, the most extensive Amazigh Islamic empire in North African history.

Abd Al Moumen's son, **Abou Yacoub Youssef**, succeeded him in 1163 CE. Initially unpopular, the new caliph gradually earned respect through his piety, devotion to books, and unwavering commitment to Almohad doctrine. Seville became the Almohad capital of al-Andalus between 1170-1172 CE, treated as a political and cultural jewel. Abou Yacoub Youssef spent much of his time there, importing Andalusian craftsmen and commissioning major construction projects in Morocco, including mosques, libraries, and palaces. In 1184 CE, Abou Yacoub Youssef launched a campaign to capture Santar√©m from the Portuguese but was wounded by a crossbow bolt and died from his injuries in battle.

His successor, **Yacoub Al Mansour** ("the Victorious"), took power in 1184 CE and quickly consolidated control by reforming tax policies and boosting agricultural production. Unlike his predecessor, he invested more in Marrakech than Iberia. In one of the greatest Muslim victories in Iberian history, the Almohads crushed King Alfonso VIII of Castile at the **Battle of Alarcos** on July 19, 1195 CE. This triumph marked the peak of their political and military power and established absolute dominance over C√≥rdoba, Seville, Murcia, Granada, and much of the Guadalquivir Valley. Despite his physical prime, Yacoub Al Mansour died suddenly near Marrakech in 1199 CE. At the time of his death, he was actively investing in monumental construction projects, including the Giralda minaret in Seville, the Kasbah of Marrakech, and the Hassan Tower in Rabat. His unexpected passing marked the beginning of political instability within the Almohad Caliphate.

**Mohammed Al Nasir** ascended the throne in 1199 CE at just 17 years old but lacked the military skill and charisma of his predecessors. He faced a devastating defeat on July 16, 1212 CE, when a massive Christian coalition crushed the Almohad army at the **Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa**. This marked a turning point, as the loss of prestige and territory reopened the path for the Christian reconquest of al-Andalus. Al Nasir narrowly escaped but, in his final years, withdrew into his palace in Marrakech, abandoning governance. He died in 1213 CE, either from brain inflammation or possibly poisoned by his ministers. With his son, Youssef al-Mountassir, only 10 years old, the empire quickly descended into internal clan rivalries and political fragmentation.

A series of short-lived and increasingly weak rulers followed between 1213-1248 CE, often relying on Christian allies to wage internal conflicts. What began as an internal power struggle after Abdallah Al Adil's death in 1224 CE escalated into a full-blown civil war, deeply affecting the population. As the Almohad state weakened, other powers seized the opportunity; remnants of the Almoravids, led by Yahia ben Ghaniya, briefly resurfaced. In North Africa, the Hafsid dynasty in Tunisia declared full independence in 1228 CE, while the Zayanids rose in rebellion in Tlemcen, Algeria. Across the region, local governors ruled like kings, ignoring Marrakech, which became a weakened political center. By 1228 CE, Almohad power in al-Andalus collapsed entirely, ending their presence in Iberia, and Seville fell back into Christian hands in 1248 CE.

**Idris Al Watiq**, the last Almohad sultan, died in Marrakech in 1269 CE as the city fell to a rising Amazigh dynasty of Zenata descent: the Marinids. Since 1245 CE, the Marinids had been strategically isolating Marrakech through gradual territorial strangulation. With their victory, the Almohad era ended, and a new Amazigh chapter began.

### **Marinids (1269-1472)**

The Marinids were a Zenata Amazigh tribe from eastern Morocco, nomadic and warrior-based, who had long moved on the margins of power. They fought alongside the Almohads at the Battle of Alarcos in 1196 CE, but at this stage, they remained subordinate allies. Following the catastrophic Almohad defeat at Las Navas de Tolosa in 1212 CE, the central authority of the caliphate was severely weakened, and tribes like the Banu Marin began asserting increasing autonomy.

Between 1216-1240 CE, the Marinids advanced into the Rif and Sais plains, asserting their independence more openly. They began extracting tribute from cities like F√®s and Taza, signaling the rise of a parallel power center in Morocco focused primarily on economic control and securing tax revenues. Abu Yahya ben Abd Al Haqq captured Mekn√®s in 1245 CE, then led a strategic assault through the Taza Gap to take F√®s in 1248 CE, a crucial step toward securing northern Morocco. This marked the Marinids‚Äô true entry into major Moroccan politics. They went on to seize Sal√© and assert control over the Atlantic plains up to the Oum Rbiaa River. Between 1250 and 1255 CE, the Marinids expanded into Taza, Sijilmassa, and the Draa Valley. After the death of Abou Yahya, his successor, **Abou Youssef Yacoub**, rose to power, emerging as the true architect of Marinid political supremacy.

Despite Hafsid dynasty support, the Marinids failed in their first attempt to capture Marrakech from the Almohads in 1261 CE. Shifting their focus eastward, they defeated the Zayyanids at the Battle of Wadi Tafna in 1268 CE and captured Tlemcen, expanding their influence into western Algeria. Learning from their earlier failure, the Marinids adopted a new strategy: they backed a dissident Almohad prince, Abou Dabbous, who promised them a share of power if he could overthrow his rival, Idris Al Watiq. With Marinid support, Abou Dabbous captured Marrakech, but once in power, he refused to honor the deal. The Marinids swiftly turned against him, defeating his forces in September 1269 CE, and entered Marrakech in triumph, led by Abou Youssef Yacoub, establishing themselves as its new sovereign rulers. The Marinids conducted a purge of the capital, desecrated the tombs of Almohad rulers, and decapitated the bodies of Abd Al Moumen and Yacoub Al Mansour. Abou Youssef Yacoub established a new district in F√®s, F√®s al-Jadid ("New F√®s"), making it the royal and administrative center, distinct from the older quarter, F√®s al-Bali.

The Marinid golden age began between 1275-1285 CE. Their forces launched five military expeditions into Spain to support Muslim Granada and assert Marinid power across the Strait. Abou Youssef Yacoub died in 1286 CE and was succeeded by his son, **Abou Yacoub Youssef**. Unlike his father, he retreated from direct ambitions in al-Andalus after losing Cadiz to the Nasrids in 1291 CE. Abou Yacoub Youssef was assassinated in 1307 CE at the siege camp of Mansoura, outside Tlemcen in Algeria.

In 1310 CE, his son **Abou Sa√Ød Othmane II** began a long and mostly internal-focused reign, marked by consolidation rather than expansion. He constructed the Bou Inania Madrasa in F√®s, establishing it as a major center of Islamic learning and Maliki scholarship. In 1340 CE, Castilian and Portuguese forces defeated the Marinids at the Battle of R√≠o Salado, ending their ambitions in al-Andalus. From that point on, no Marinid sultan attempted major campaigns in the Iberian Peninsula, though they maintained a limited military presence in outposts.

Sultan **Abou Inan Faris** was assassinated in 1358 CE, triggering court conspiracies, growing instability, and a series of succession wars, with 25 sultans ruling over just 42 years. In 1374 CE, Nasrid Granada captured Gibraltar and Ceuta, definitively ending Marinid military presence in al-Andalus. The reign of **Abou Sa√Ød III** began in 1398 CE amid a period of weakening central authority. Tetouan was captured by Castilian King Henry III a year later, and in 1415 CE, the Portuguese seized Sebta (Ceuta), marking the start of European colonial penetration in Morocco.

After the death of the Marinid sultan, powerful Wattasid vizier Abou Zakariya Yahya installed the young Marinid prince Abd Al Haqq II as a puppet ruler in 1420 CE, marking the beginning of a **Wattasid regency**, rendering the Marinid dynasty largely nominal. Portuguese ambitions escalated; in 1437 CE, their expedition to capture Tangier failed, but their intent remained unchanged. A year later, King Alfonso V launched a more aggressive expansion policy along the Moroccan coast, and the Portuguese captured El Qsar es-Saghir, securing another strategic coastal position. The Revolt of F√®s in 1465 CE, led by the Idrisid line (shorfa), saw the people rise up against the corruption and perceived religious weakness of Abd Al Haqq II. He was captured and assassinated, bringing the Marinid dynasty to an end.

### **Wattasids (1472-1554)**

The Wattasids were a Zenata Amazigh clan, closely linked to the Marinids, who originally served as viziers at their court in F√®s. In the Revolt of F√®s in 1458 CE, Marinid sultan Abd al-Haqq II was executed, but before his death, he ordered the massacre of the main Wattasid leaders. Only the Wattasi Mohammed ash-Cheikh escaped, swearing revenge.

In 1472 CE, **Mohammed ash-Cheikh** seized F√®s, becoming the first sovereign of the Banu Wattas and formally establishing the Wattasid dynasty. Although the city lay in ruins and the kingdom was fragmented, he secured the support of the ulama by promising a jihad against the Iberians. That same year, the Portuguese seized Arzila and Tangier, further tightening their grip on the Moroccan coast. Mohammed ash-Cheikh ruled over a land devastated by famine and epidemics, with his authority limited to the north. From F√®s, he introduced moderate reforms, arabized his army, and replaced local leaders with loyal scholars. Despite his efforts, he failed to unify the country, which remained fragmented and under constant threat from the Portuguese and rival factions. The Fall of Granada in 1492 CE led to thousands of Andalusian Muslims and Jews fleeing to Morocco, but they were poorly received by both the Wattasid administration and the local population. Attempts at centralization failed, as regions like Chefchaouen, Marrakech, and Debdou remained effectively autonomous.

The reign of **Mohammed al-Burtughali** began in 1505 CE. Raised in Portugal as a hostage, he launched jihad campaigns but failed to alter the balance of power in favor of the Wattasids. Between 1508-1513 CE, the Portuguese seized Safi, followed by Azemmour, tightening their grip on the Atlantic coast and increasing European pressure on the crumbling Wattasid state. A new threat emerged as the **Saadians**, backed by the Jazouli Sufi brotherhoods, rose in the south between 1509-1511 CE. The *chorfas* of the Draa positioned themselves as champions of jihad against both the Portuguese presence and Wattasid inaction. Wattasid attempts to retake Tangier and Asilah between 1511-1515 CE ended in failure. Al-Burtughali signed a truce with the Portuguese, a move widely perceived as weakness and met with disapproval from both the population and the elites.

In 1524 CE, the Saadians captured Marrakech, solidifying their power in the south. That same year, Mohammed al-Burtughali died, and **Ahmed al-Wattasi** ascended to the throne, inheriting a fractured and contested kingdom. Ahmed al-Wattasi suffered defeats against the Saadians at Marrakech (1527 CE) and Animay (1529 CE). He was forced to acknowledge Saadian control over southern Morocco, firmly dividing the kingdom into two rival realms. The Saadians seized Agadir from the Portuguese in 1541 CE, a major symbolic victory that exposed Wattasid weakness and marked a turning point in the struggle for Moroccan leadership. The Wattasids were defeated at Oued el Abid in 1537 CE, losing control of Tadla and Tafilalet, further shrinking their domain. The kingdom was formally divided along the Tadla / Oum er-Rbiaa line, creating a clear boundary between Wattasid and Saadian control.

In 1545 CE, Sultan Ahmed al-Wattasi considered an alliance with the Portuguese but was captured by the Saadians and taken to Marrakech. He was later released in exchange for ceding the regions of Habt, Gharb, and Mekn√®s. Saadian leader Mohammed ash-Cheikh entered F√®s in 1549 CE, capturing the Wattasid sultan for a second time. He then departed with his court for Marrakech. In F√®s, this event was seen as a national tragedy. Ahmed al-Wattasi's brother, Bou Hassoun, fled to Badis, seeking support from both the Turks of Algiers and the Spanish in a final attempt to reclaim his throne. Under pressure from Saadian forces, the Portuguese evacuated El-Ksar es-Seghir and Asilah in 1550 CE, marking the decline of the Iberian presence along the Moroccan coast, with only Tangier, Mazagan, and Ceuta remaining under their control.

With support from the Turks of Algiers, Bou Hassoun retook Fez in January 1554 CE. However, his restored rule collapsed quickly when Turkish admiral and corsair Salah Rais attempted to place the city under Ottoman control, sparking resistance. On September 13, 1554 CE, Bou Hassoun was killed in an ambush set by the Saadian sharif Mohammed ech-Cheikh at Ras al-Ma‚Äô in the Tadla region. This marked the definitive end of the Wattasid dynasty.

### **Saadians (1510-1660)**

In the early 16th century, Morocco was fractured and threatened by foreign occupations. From the southern region of the Souss, a Sharifian family emerged, claiming descent from the Prophet (pbuh): the Saadians. United by religious legitimacy and their call for jihad against the Portuguese, they rose rapidly, establishing control over the country by facing the Wattasids, and founded a dynasty that would reshape Morocco‚Äôs power, culture, and place in the world.

The Saadian family, *chorfas* from the Draa, supported by the influential Sufi order of al-Jazouli, began rallying tribes for a jihad against the Portuguese occupying Atlantic coastal cities. **Mohammed al-Qaym bi-Amr Al lah**, their first recognized leader, received the *bay‚Äôa* (oath of loyalty) in Tidsi (Sous) between 1509-1511 CE. Saadian forces successfully launched attacks on Portuguese-controlled outposts like Agadir, Tafedna, and Agouz in 1513 CE, asserting their military strength and religious mission, which enhanced their appeal as a charismatic, nationalist, and spiritual alternative to the fragmented Wattasid authority. Mohammed al-Qaym died in battle during this period.

**Ahmad al-Araj**, a key Saadian leader, seized Marrakech between 1517-1524 CE, establishing it as a base of operations and expanding his control through central and southern Morocco. Meanwhile, his brother **Mohammed ach-Cheikh** governed the Sous, consolidating Saadian influence regionally. The provinces of Tadla and Anmay marked the de facto frontier between the Saadian-controlled south and the Wattassid-controlled north. Mohammed ash-Cheikh scored a major symbolic and strategic victory by recapturing Agadir from the Portuguese in 1541 CE. This blow to Portuguese ambitions strengthened the Saadian image as defenders of Islam and liberators of Morocco, while Ahmad al-Araj began to be sidelined. Tensions rose between the two brothers, culminating in Mohammed ash-Cheikh defeating Ahmad al-Araj in 1544 CE, emerging as the sole ruler. Marrakech became the undisputed capital of the rising Saadian state.

With military superiority and religious legitimacy, the Saadians entered Fez in 1549 CE, effectively collapsing the Wattasid dynasty. Mohammed ash-Cheikh became Sultan of a reunified Morocco. Fortresses like Kasbah Bu Jlud, Kasbah Tamdert, Borj Nord, and Borj Sud solidified Saadian control in the north. Wattassid puppet ruler Bou Hassoun, backed by Ottoman Algeria, momentarily retook Fez in 1554 CE. However, Mohammed ach-Cheikh swiftly returned and defeated him, consolidating Saadian rule and definitively ending Wattassid power.

In 1557 CE, **Mohammed ash-Cheikh** was assassinated by Ottoman agents, in retaliation for his independence from the Ottomans, marking the beginning of a turbulent transition. His son, **Abdal lah al-Ghalib**, succeeded him, repelled Ottoman designs on Morocco, and initiated diplomatic maneuvers with Spain. Under Abdal lah al-Ghalib, the cities of Chefchaouen, Tetouan, and Debdou were brought under Saadian control between 1567-1574 CE. Fortresses were built or refurbished, and military and administrative networks were established across the Gharb and Habt regions.

After Abdal lah al-Ghalib's death in 1574 CE, the kingdom plunged into civil conflict between his son, Al Moutawakkil, and his nephews. These rival parties became the three protagonists of the **Battle of the Three Kings**. In 1578 CE, a Christian-Muslim battle near Ksar el-Kebir (Oued al-Makhazin) altered Morocco‚Äôs fate. King Sebastian of Portugal, Saadian Sultan Al Moutawakkil, and his uncle Abd al Malik were all killed. **Ahmad al-Mansour** ("the Golden one") ascended to the throne, inheriting an emboldened and wealthy kingdom following the Portuguese defeat. To better govern, Ahmad al-Mansour divided Morocco into eight viceroyalties in 1585 CE, ensuring control over key regions like the Rif, Tafilalet, and Draa Valley, which were crucial for trans-Saharan trade and military defense.

Ahmad Al Mansour exploited Anglo-Spanish rivalry to secure military and diplomatic gains. He negotiated with Queen Elizabeth I while maintaining ties with Catholic Spain and successfully captured Arzila. He ruled with opulence from Marrakech, building the magnificent El Badi Palace with materials imported from Italy and India. Trade flourished through the control of trans-Saharan routes, and gold from the conquest of the Songhai Empire filled state coffers. Led by Judar Pasha, Moroccan troops crossed the Sahara and defeated the Songhai Empire at Tondibi between 1590-1591 CE. This conquest brought wealth, salt, slaves, and gold, expanding Moroccan sovereignty over Timbuktu, Gao, and other major centers of Saharan trade, though this control proved tenuous and unsustainable in the long term.

Ahmad Al Mansour died in 1603 CE. His sons‚Äîal-Mamoun in Fez, Abu Faris in Sous, and Zidan in Marrakech‚Äîinherited fragments of the kingdom, leading to a civil war. Abu Faris died of illness in 1610 CE, but the conflict continued, with each prince carving out a domain, supported by European or Turkish allies, further alienating the population. The once-unified *Makhzen* fragmented into rival military camps, disrupting trade routes, and ports like Sal√© and Rabat became semi-autonomous under corsair control. The Spanish took Larache in 1610 CE after a deal with al-Mamoun. Zidan managed to hold Marrakesh between 1613-1628 CE but failed to fully restore Saadian authority, facing challenges from regional marabouts and foreign interference. He retained an important library, the "Zaydani Collection," now preserved in El Escorial. Al-Mamoun's betrayal of religious principles led to his downfall and assassination, and Fez rejected his rule. The *Makhzen* collapsed in the north as tribal rebellions and *zawiya* influence spread.

A series of short-lived and often weak rulers‚Äîal-Walid, Mohammed es-Saghir, and Ahmad al-Abbas‚Äîmarked the final stage of Saadian decline between 1631-1654 CE. Local warlords and religious brotherhoods increasingly asserted autonomy, particularly in the Atlas and Draa Valley. The assassination of the last Saadian sultan by his own guards in 1659 CE marked the definitive collapse of the dynasty. Morocco entered a period of anarchy, dominated by maraboutic power and local warlords, until the rise of the Alaoui clan shortly after.

The *Histoire du Maroc* (History of Morocco), edited by Mohamed Kably, is a thoughtfully crafted work of considerable dimensions, featuring beautifully produced maps and illustrations. It was published by √âdition de l‚ÄôInstitut royal pour la recherche sur l‚Äôhistoire du Maroc in 2011, with a revised edition in 2012, and has been translated into English as *History of Morocco: A Work of Synthesis and Update*. Significant extracts from the English translation are available on the Royal Institute for Research in the History of Morocco's website. This volume is seen as a landmark work of interest to historians across various fields and does not render obsolete earlier scholarly works like Jean Brignon's *Histoire du Maroc* (1967), to which Kably and his collaborators often refer.

Two years after the main volume, a complementary work titled *Chronologie de l‚Äôhistoire du Maroc. Des temps pr√©historiques √† la fin du XXe si√®cle* was published, also edited by Mohamed Kably. Kably's introduction to this chronology reflects on the notion of "event" and its polysemic nature, while Abderrahmane El Moudden offers insights into chronological criteria, the duality of Hegiran and Christian calendars, and source peculiarities. This reasoned chronology also serves as a comprehensive index with its own commentary and annexes, confirming the vision of an overall project.

The *Histoire du Maroc* is a collective effort involving over fifty authors, mostly Moroccan, reflecting a process of **"Moroccanization"** within the discipline of history. While the specific roles of individual authors are not always identified, the contributions from various specialists in their respective fields of specialization are evident, leading to differences in style and expression across chapters. The work attempts to combine the unity of a research project with the remarkable diversity of its inputs, guided by shared references and questions.

The primary guiding principle of the *Histoire du Maroc* is **chronological**, spanning from prehistoric times up to "Independent Morocco," with chapters organized along a unified temporal axis. The book avoids a ritualistic "land and its people" presentation, instead emphasizing the succession of phases. It directly confronts questions of **periodization**, acknowledging that definitions like the late antique Maghreb (3rd-7th century CE) have been influenced by 19th-century French historians. The authors, while sometimes expressing reservations, largely adhere to classic European historical divisions like the "Middle Ages," influenced by French educational traditions during the Protectorate. The Moroccan 15th century, for example, is presented as a "transitory period" between the Middle Ages and the "modern" age, starting with the Portuguese occupation of Ceuta in 1415 and ending with the foundation of the Saadian dynasty in 1510\. Similarly, the "long nineteenth century" (1790-1912) is evoked, with 1790 chosen as a starting point due to its announcement of "irreversible" European influence on Morocco.

The work begins with a unique first chapter by geographer Mohamed Naciri, titled "Natural Dynamics and their Influence on Territorial Structuring in Morocco". This prologue rejects geographical determinism and highlights the interplay of internal (territorial configuration) and external (geopolitical position) constraints, alongside ethnic and "maraboutic-Sharifian" logics. It interweaves geographical, historical, ecological, religious, cultural, and state-related threads, organizing Morocco into four complex groupings: the Sahara, the Atlas mountains, the Atlantic, and the Mediterranean. This chapter establishes the "historicity" of space as a dynamic introduction to the subsequent historical narratives.

At its core, the *Histoire du Maroc* is a **state-centered history**, primarily concerned with the *makhzen* (state) as the origin and locus of central power. It describes and analyzes developments in relation to the *makhzen*, whether dominant or weakened, and its role in power struggles and against territorial fragmentation. This emphasis is seen in discussions of the Almoravid and Almohad periods, the sultanates of Moulay Ismail and Moulay Hassan, the Merinid sultans' patronage of arts, and the construction of the Alawite *makhzen*. While the book addresses aspects like Saadian art and architecture, these are sometimes presented in a framed layout, implying a potential difficulty in integrating them fully into the power-focused narrative.

The book extends to the **"very longue dur√©e,"** incorporating two long phases on Morocco's pre-Islamic history (up to the 8th century BCE) and its relationship with the Mediterranean world before Islam. It addresses the challenges of reconstructing this past due to limited sources and touches upon significant figures like Juba II, presented as a complex figure who influenced early Romanization and urban development. The authors' approach to Juba II is detailed and conscientious, avoiding hyperbole.

The *Histoire du Maroc* consistently defends the idea of Morocco as **multiple and pluralistic**. This plurality is evident in its discussions of local histories, such as the quasi-independent city-states of Ceuta, Safi, Tetouan, Rabat, and Sal√©, the latter thriving on maritime corsairing. The work also highlights **social and demographic diversity**, including the Arab, Berber, and Jewish components of the population. It discusses the history of Jews in Morocco, emphasizing periods of convergence and symbiosis alongside tensions, with figures like Ha√Øm Zafrani underscoring a dual identity rooted in universal Judaism and local culture. The book also examines the presence of **sub-Saharan African populations** in Morocco, particularly in the context of the *'Abid al-Bukhari* army under Moulay Ismail. It clarifies that this army was primarily recruited within Morocco itself, challenging notions of massive slave convoys from Sudan solely for sugar plantations. The complex and heterogeneous nature of these military forces, defying simple racial or legal distinctions, is underscored.

Furthermore, the book explores Morocco's **external relations and the internationalization of knowledge**. It describes exchanges with the outside world through gateways like coastal cities and Gibraltar, and the introduction of goods like tea and porcelain through European trade and diplomatic missions. Accounts from European captives, such as Germain Mo√ºette, provide an "Other's gaze" into Moroccan society and cultural interactions, including theological debates and the circulation of knowledge. The book also touches upon the contribution of foreign scholars and explorers to the study of Morocco, acknowledging how political contexts and ideologies may have influenced their work, yet recognizing their role as eye-witnesses who shaped disciplines.

In conclusion, the *Histoire du Maroc* is presented as an undeniable achievement, characterized by its erudition, unity, and accessible, pedagogical approach. It is a scholarly work grounded in up-to-date, diverse historical knowledge, acknowledging both recent research and valuable "classic" studies. The book's strength lies in its ability to adapt to a variety of subjects within a unified framework, demonstrating an "experimental and pragmatic" approach to Moroccan historiography.

The article "Warfare and Firearms in Fifteenth Century Morocco, 1400-1492" by Weston F. Cook Jr. examines the role of firepower warfare in Morocco during a period of significant political, social, and commercial change in the broader Afro-Asian and Iberian world. While Europe experienced an "Early Modern Military Revolution" rooted in the "Gunpowder Revolution" of the fifteenth century, Morocco was on the verge of political extinction. The article's ambition is modest, focusing on Morocco and offering tentative conclusions rather than declarative ones.

Morocco's primary foreign antagonist, Portugal, utilized a powerful combination of amphibious assault, naval suppressive fire, and a divide-and-conquer strategy, all heavily reliant on artillery warfare and the arquebuse. Lisbon established fortified coastal enclaves and supported them with a self-sufficient seaborne logistical network, using a "carrot-and-stick" approach of commercial incentives for compliant Moroccan notables and violent military excursions into the countryside. These incursions, along with Castilian aggression, accelerated the fragmentation of the Banu Marin sultanate (1262-1465) and led to the rise of the Banu Wattas (1465-1554). However, the new Wattasid Sultanate, which gained stability in 1472 after 15 years of civil turmoil, was more disjointed than its predecessor due to foreign pressures and a "populist gunpowder revolution aborning within society itself".

The analysis of the "Gunpowder Revolution" in Morocco is complicated by sparse and poorly reconstructed indigenous records, with many scholars showing distaste for the era. Consequently, much of the information relies on the often-triumphalist writings of Portuguese chroniclers, some Castilians, and a Lowlands cavalier, Jurge Von Ehingen. These sources, despite their biases, repeatedly testify to the vital role of artillery and firearms in Portuguese hegemony.

Portugal's assault began in 1415 with the capture of Sabta (Ceuta), where the installation of *bombardas*, *colebratas*, and *falconetes* proved crucial for its defense against Moroccan counter-sieges. Although hand-held guns (*tiros de fogo*) and riflemen (*espingarieros*) appeared in Morocco with King Duarte's 1437 expedition against Tangiers, they did not prevent a humiliating Portuguese defeat. Despite this, Lisbon continued to raid coastal villages and induce harbor towns like Tangiers, Sla, Safi, Azammur, Anfa, and Massa to abandon their allegiance to the Banu Marin dynasty. In 1458, King Affonso seized Qsar-s-Saghir, where Lisbon's ship-to-shore fire effectively repelled Muslim defenders, and artillery was decisive in its capture and defense. The first confirmed gunpowder artillery weapon in Moroccan hands was a bombard found as loot in Ceuta in 1415\.

Moroccans soon acquired and used artillery. In 1419, Sultan Abu Sa'id led an international Muslim expedition against Ceuta, which included two bombards crewed by Granadan advisors. Although the assault was ultimately unsuccessful, it demonstrated Moroccan access to European military technology through Granadan Muslims. However, questions remain about the organization, support, and origin of these early Moroccan gun batteries. The 1437 contest for Tangiers saw Portuguese infantry deploying formations of "handgunners and crossbowmen," but personal firearms had yet to appear in Moroccan ranks. In this conflict, the Portuguese lost several weapons, including guns, which were subsequently used in *makhzan* service.

In 1456-57, Moroccan forces demonstrated increased proficiency, coordinating artillery fire with ground attacks during a massive assault on Ceuta and employing harassing fire to prevent sorties. When Affonso seized Qsar-s-Saghir the following year, the Moroccan Wazir led a counter-siege with at least 32 guns, and Moroccan soldiers armed with hand-guns were noted for the first time. During this unsuccessful eight-week siege, Moroccan batteries used pairs of bombards, often firing them in alternating sequence or volley, and employed dummy guns and multiple positions. The presence of Granadan, Moroccan, and "elchees" (European mercenaries, prisoners, and converts) as crewmen confirmed that the Banu Marin *makhzan* now possessed a distinct, full-time artillery corps. Zurara, a Portuguese chronicler, reported that Banu Marin batteries fired over 30 rounds a day, a respectable volume of fire, though he claimed they inflicted little damage despite firing 1,595 cannonballs. The failure of these sieges was attributed to the defenders' natural advantages, superior Portuguese artillery, continuous resupply, and increasingly, internal conflict within the Moroccan state.
