For the next decade, Portuguese raids and cannonades against the coast became almost annual events, leading to the destruction of cities like Anfa (modern Casablanca) in 1463\. Conversely, Moroccan artillery almost vanishes from records during the 1460s, a period marked by Marinid-Wattasid wars and a lack of documentation. By 1471, Portugal's King Affonso launched an unprecedented armada against Arzila, overwhelming the defenders with 400 ships, over 30,000 troops, and a landing force armed with arquebuses, crossbows, and cannons. This led to the surrender of Arzila and Tangiers, and Muhammad ash-Shaykh al-Wattas, facing superior Portuguese firepower, signed a humiliating ten-year truce.

Over 15 years, the balance of firepower drastically shifted in Portugal's favor, attributed to Affonso's decision to "modernize" his arsenal with German and Flemish artisans to cast bronze cannons and forge matchlock arquebuses. Lisbon also established trading posts in quasi-autonomous port towns, which were then fortified with cannons and garrisons, and enforced a strict arms embargo to prevent the sale of gunpowder weapons to Muslims. This vigilance likely accelerated the depletion of Moroccan gunpowder arsenals. European observers nearing the sixteenth century often remarked on the absence of firearms among Moroccans and their apparent ignorance and terror of gunnery. Moroccan chroniclers also alluded to their own lack of firepower, either by omission or by commenting on the "irresistibility" of cannons.

Despite this period of decline, glimpses of Moroccan gunpowder weapons in action reappeared in the 1490s. Leo Africanus mentioned that the Wattasid Sultan installed 100 *makhzan* arquebusiers at Larache after the abortive "Fort Graciosa" campaign, and credited the skillful deployment of arquebuse soldiers for the success of *makhzan* tactics against cavalry militias at Dubdu in 1496\. More significantly, Africanus noted the proliferation of firearms, including the arquebuse and *espingard*, among tribes and polities in the Dra'a valley in Morocco's deep south, who resisted both Portuguese and Wattasid control. This incipient revival of firearms capabilities suggests that gunpowder weaponry would soon revolutionize communal defiance and resistance in Morocco, marking the beginning of the country's "military revolution" in the sixteenth century.

A key factor in the fluctuation of Moroccan gunnery was the complex relationship with Granada, the last Islamic state in Iberia. Until the late 1450s, Granada was a primary source for weapons, expertise, and crews for Morocco. However, there is no evidence that the Marinids or Muhammad ash-Shaykh al-Wattas attempted to establish domestic production of firearms, with Morocco's first foundry not appearing until the 1530s. After 1458, these ties unraveled due to internal political strife in both states and intensified military isolation caused by Portuguese surveillance. During the Granadan War (1481-1492), Muhammad ash-Shaykh al-Wattas maintained an "embarrassed neutrality," selling grain to Christians and observing the Castilian blockade. When Granada fell in 1492, a significant number of Granadan war veterans, experienced in gunpowder combat or crafts, migrated to Morocco, finding service with the *makhzan*, as privateers, bodyguards, mercenaries, and even with domestic challengers to the Wattasids. The fifteenth century, therefore, laid the dim and debatable roots for Morocco's prolonged military revolution in the sixteenth century, highlighting the necessity of studying military history as social and international comparative history.

The mid-16th century in Morocco was a pivotal period marked by the intense conflict between the declining **Wattasid dynasty** and the ascendant **Saadian sharifs**. This struggle was not merely for territory but represented a clash of legitimacy, governance models, and the very identity of the emergent Moroccan state. The conflict unfolded on a complex geopolitical stage, with the formidable powers of the Iberian Peninsula (Portugal and Spain) and the Ottoman Empire casting long shadows over the Maghreb, influencing alliances and shaping Morocco's identity.

### **The Decline of Wattasid Power**

By the early 16th century, the Wattasid dynasty, of Zenata Berber origin, was in terminal decline. Formally established in Fez in 1472 as successors to the Marinids, their authority was tenuous and largely confined to northern Morocco. The south had fragmented into principalities, and vital Atlantic ports were increasingly falling under Portuguese control. The Wattasids' legitimacy, initially secured through alliances with the urban elite of Fez and Idrisid sharifian families, became fragile due to a crisis of confidence among the populace. They were widely perceived as having failed in their sacred duty to defend the *dar al-Islam* (abode of Islam) against Christian encroachments, adopting a policy of conciliation towards the aggressively expanding Portuguese and Spanish. This strategic failure had devastating economic consequences, as Portuguese occupation of key commercial hubs like Safi, Mazagan, Agadir, and Mogador severed Morocco's traditional trans-Saharan and maritime trade routes, leading to economic stagnation and social unrest. This military impotence and economic decay created a power vacuum, particularly in the south, fostering an environment ripe for new leadership.

### **The Rise of the Saadian Sharifs**

Emerging from the Sous Valley in the south, the Saadian family posed a potent challenge to the Wattasids, building their claim on military success and superior religious legitimacy. They asserted *sharifian* lineage, claiming direct descent from the Prophet Muhammad through his daughter Fatima and grandson Hasan ibn Ali, which was a powerful ideological tool resonating deeply in a society seeking spiritual and political renewal. This marked a significant shift from centuries of Berber-led dynasties, establishing a model of political legitimacy vested in the Prophet's house that would endure in Morocco.

The Saadians translated this religious charisma into political power through a relentless and successful *jihad* against the Portuguese. Their rise began with the unification of tribes in the Sous Valley, culminating in the watershed moment of their capture of the Portuguese stronghold of Agadir in 1541\. This immense military and propaganda triumph cemented their status as heroes and true defenders of the faith, galvanizing popular support and allowing them to systematically roll back the Portuguese presence, taking control of other key ports like Azemmour and Safi. The conflict escalated, with open rivalry declared in 1528, following the 1527 Treaty of Tadla that partitioned Morocco. The Saadians used their southern base to capture Marrakesh in 1524, decisively defeat the Wattasid army at the Battle of Wadi al-'Abid in 1536, and finally conquer the Wattasid capital of Fez in 1549\. The last Wattasid ruler, Ali Abu Hassun, was defeated and killed at the Battle of Tadla in September 1554, making the Saadian sultan, Muhammad al-Shaykh, the undisputed master of a reunified Morocco.

### **The Ottoman-Portuguese-Moroccan Geopolitical Triangle**

The Wattasid-Saadian conflict was deeply entwined with the wider strategic competition between the Ottoman Empire and Habsburg Spain (allied with Portugal). Morocco's strategic location made it a prize, and its internal struggle provided an entry point for imperial ambitions. In a desperate move, Wattasid ruler Abu Hassun declared himself an Ottoman vassal in 1545 and sought aid. The Ottomans, aiming to extend influence across North Africa, provided limited support, enabling Abu Hassun to briefly retake Fez in January 1554 with Algerian troops under Salah Reis. However, the Saadians reconquered the city months later, ending Wattasid rule permanently.

The Saadian victory recast external pressures, as a strong, unified, and expansionist Morocco now positioned itself as a significant third force, a buffer state between the two superpowers. Saadian Sultan Muhammad al-Shaykh refused vassalage, directly challenging the Ottoman Sultan's claim to Muslim leadership by proclaiming himself Caliph and Mahdi. This assertion of supreme authority was anathema to the Ottomans, leading to the assassination of Muhammad al-Shaykh in 1557 by an Ottoman agent. The Saadians, in turn, pursued complex diplomacy, cultivating commercial and political contacts with European powers like England and France to counterbalance Spanish and Ottoman influence, thereby forging a distinct Moroccan state identity adept at navigating early modern global politics.

### **The Armies of the Sultanates: A Comparative Analysis**

The outcome of the Wattasid-Saadian conflict was largely determined by the significant technological and organizational differences between their armies. The Saadian military was an early modern war machine built on gunpowder and professional discipline, while the Wattasid host represented a late-medieval army unable to adapt to 16th-century warfare realities.

#### **The Wattasid Host: The Old Guard**

The Wattasid army reflected military traditions of the preceding Marinid era, relying on feudal levies and a small professional core. Its structure comprised:

* **Tribal levies:** Primarily cavalry from allied Zenata Berber tribes, whose loyalty was conditional, dependent on pay and plunder, and often seasonal. Leo Africanus estimated the Wattasid sultan of Fez maintained a permanent force of 6,000 cavalry, including 500 mounted crossbowmen and 500 mounted arquebusiers, likely referring to the elite corps.  
* **Makhzaniyyah:** An elite, salaried standing cavalry corps, continuing the Marinid system, serving as the sultan's personal guard and frontier defenders.  
* **Foreign specialists:** Granadan advisors and European renegades ('*elchees*') were employed to crew artillery pieces.

Wattasid arms, armor, and attire lacked standardization. Visual evidence from the Pastrana Tapestries (depicting the Portuguese conquest of Asilah in 1471\) shows a variety of head protection including turbans, European-style kettle helmets, sallets, and Islamic-style helmets similar to Mamluk designs. Shields were generally round, with the traditional leather *adarga* also common. Weaponry included lances and sabers for cavalry, with elite units using crossbows and early firearms. They possessed siege artillery (large bombards) but were technologically outmatched and logistically inferior to Portuguese forces. Regular battle dress included flowing robes and tunics, with no standardized uniform; rank was indicated by the quality of fabric and ornamentation. Elite troops like the *Makhzaniyyah* received clothing as part of their pay.

#### **The Saadian Force: Architects of a New Way of War**

In contrast, the Saadians were military innovators, consciously creating a new type of army that embraced gunpowder technology and professional organization. Their army underwent rapid evolution, starting as a feudal force but quickly establishing a professional, standing army funded by a new, centralized taxation system (*naiba*). This professional core grew from an initial 500 cavalry to 3,000 by al-Qaim's death, and more than tenfold by 1540\.

Saadian forces featured a diverse recruitment base to ensure loyalty to the state over specific tribes, including allied Berber and Arab tribes, **Andalusi Muslims** (refugees eager for revenge against Iberians), **Turkish officers** (bringing Ottoman tactical expertise), and **European renegades and mercenaries**. Later, Saadian sultans like Ahmad al-Mansur would deliberately entrust high military commands to European and Andalusi officers to limit Turkish influence, also employing them as personal guards.

The decisive factor in Saadian success was their complete adoption of firearms, building their entire military doctrine around them.

* **Arquebusiers:** Formed the core, deployed as infantry skirmishers and mounted units. Mounted arquebusiers were drilled in advanced tactics like the **caracole**, creating continuous volleys of fire.  
* **Artillery:** They assembled a formidable artillery train. The capture of Agadir in 1541 provided access to oceanic trade routes for European arms imports and wealth from the sugar trade to fund a domestic arms industry. A cannon foundry was established in Marrakesh under a skilled Morisco artisan. At Agadir, Muhammad al-Shaykh deployed over 50 cannons, including massive bronze siege guns like *Ma√Ømuna* (firing 420mm stone munitions) which were hauled by camels to high ground for plunging shots, demonstrating sophisticated modern siege craft.

Ottoman influence was significant in Saadian military transformation. Sultan Abd al-Malik (1576-1578) explicitly reorganized his army along Ottoman lines and adopted Ottoman-style clothing for his court and troops as a statement of modernity and power. Saadian elite and professional soldiers likely adopted attire similar to Janissaries, including long coats or kaftans, often made from dark blue English wool ("Brown Blues") for high-ranking officials, which became a signature color of the Saadian elite.

### **Command, Rank, and Distinction**

In both armies, hierarchy blended formal titles with displays of wealth, but the Saadians moved towards a more centralized command.

* **Wattasids:** The Sultan was supreme commander, but field armies could be led by a vizier. The *Makhzaniyyah* were commanded by a *qƒÅ'id*. Rank was distinguished by equipment quality, fabric richness, weapon ornamentation, and horse quality.  
* **Saadians:** The Sultan was the absolute commander-in-chief, often appointing his sons to command formations to ensure loyalty. The adoption of Ottoman-style organization suggests formalized officer ranks (e.g., *Agha*, *Bey*). Distinction of rank was primarily visual but more codified, with Ottoman-influenced kaftans creating clearer uniforms for the elite. Luxurious dark blue kaftans of imported English wool identified high-ranking commanders. Sultan Ahmad al-Mansour later formalized this with an elite guard of 2,500 warriors with distinctive dress and equipment.

### **Comparative Military Profile (c. 1554\)**

The Battle of Tadla in 1554 showcased the military asymmetries.

* **Army Composition:** Wattasids relied on Zenata Berber tribal levies and a small *Makhzaniyyah*. Saadians had a growing professional standing army of diverse recruits (loyal tribes, Andalusis, Turks, Europeans) funded by centralized taxes, with tribal levies as auxiliaries.  
* **Primary Armament (Cavalry):** Wattasids used lance and saber, with small elite mounted crossbowmen/arquebusiers. Saadians used lance and saber, widely supplemented by matchlock arquebus, with mounted arquebusiers as a key offensive element.  
* **Primary Armament (Infantry):** Wattasid infantry had limited roles, using spears, swords, shields, and some crossbows. Saadian infantry was a core component, dominated by disciplined arquebusiers deployed as skirmishers.  
* **Artillery:** Wattasids possessed siege artillery but were outmatched in number, quality, and tactical deployment. Saadians had a modern and potent artillery train, many produced domestically, used skillfully in mobile field roles and complex sieges.  
* **Tactical Doctrine:** Wattasids relied on traditional massed cavalry charges and static sieges. Saadians employed innovative combined-arms tactics, integrating infantry firearms, mobile artillery, and cavalry, using advanced maneuvers like the caracole.  
* **Funding Model:** Wattasids relied on a failing feudal model of tribute and dwindling state treasury due to lost coastal trade. Saadians were funded by a modern, centralized tax system (*naiba*) and substantial revenues from conquered ports and the sugar trade. The Saadian success was a self-reinforcing cycle: military victories secured trade routes, which provided resources for advanced gunpowder weapons, leading to further victories and state consolidation.

### **Banners of Power: Flags and Dynastic Identity**

Flags and banners were potent symbols of legitimacy, authority, and military identity.

* **Wattasid Standard:** The precise design is uncertain. A specific design on Vexilla Mundi (white field, thick red border, central black-and-white checkered square with red square) lacks corroboration. A more widely circulated depiction attributes a plain red field with a central gold eight-pointed star (from two overlapping squares) to Wattasids, Marinids, and Saadians, suggesting it was a general state symbol for the Sultanate of Fez. The Marinids' primary color was white, sometimes with religious inscriptions, suggesting the Wattasids might have continued using a white royal standard, while the red flag served as a broader state symbol. Different colored flags were also used for military units.  
* **Saadian Banner:** Evidence points to both red and white banners. The red flag with the gold star is often attributed to them, linking them to Moroccan state tradition and the *shurafa*. However, specific textual evidence confirms the primary personal and royal banner of early Saadian sultans was **white**, known as *al-'alam al-mans√ªr* ("the Victorious Banner"). This was a sophisticated political act, claiming legitimacy as heirs to the Marinids while revitalizing the tradition under new *sharifian* leadership. Descriptions portray it as a "dramatic statement in white," often silk, embroidered with Qur'anic verses in gold thread. The choice of white, eschewing green, signaled the Saadian movement's unique, divinely favored nature. Historians confirmed its use in royal parades, suggesting a hierarchy where the white *al-'alam al-mans√ªr* was the Sultan's personal standard, and a red flag a more general state or war banner.  
* **Language of Color on the Battlefield:** In the 16th century, colored banners were essential for command, control, and communication, identifying units, signaling maneuvers, and providing rallying points. Almohad armies used various colored flags, and the Ottoman army, emulated by Saadians, had a developed system of unit flags. Saadians likely adopted similar systems for their professional army, resulting in a colorful spectacle led by the Sultan's white banner, with other colored flags marking formations.

### **The Fabric of Daily Life: Civilian Attire in a Time of Transition**

Civilian attire in mid-16th century Morocco reflected social status, wealth, occupation, and regional identity, with new garments among the elite and traditional dress for common people.

#### **The Urban & Elite Wardrobe**

The Saadian era saw significant developments in elite fashion due to the dynasty's wealth and cosmopolitan connections.

* **Kaftan:** A long, one-piece robe, open at the front, fastened with braided-silk buttons (*aakad*) and trimmed with decorative braid (*sfifa*), likely introduced from Persia or the Ottoman Empire. Initially for men, it was adopted enthusiastically by women during the Saadian period, spurring innovation in luxurious fabrics. Elite men favored kaftans made from dark blue English wool ("Brown Blues"), which signified wealth.  
* **Takchita:** Popularized by Sultan Ahmad al-Mansour, this was an elaborate two-piece women's ensemble with a foundational kaftan (*tahtiya*) worn under an ornate over-tunic (*fouqia* or *mansouria*).  
* **Jabador:** An ensemble of a loose-fitting tunic and baggy trousers (*sarwal*). Originally everyday wear, it became a symbol of elite status for urban notables and high-ranking artisans in the 16th century.  
* **Qamƒ´·π£:** A loose-fitting tunic or shirt, a foundational garment for most urban dwellers.  
* **Sarwal:** Loose, baggy trousers, worn as part of the Jabador or standalone.  
* **Djellaba:** A distinctive, ankle-length hooded robe, the most common outer garment for both men and women. Its material and color could indicate status, with fine white wool associated with aristocracy.

#### **Clothing of the Common Folk (Doukkala Region)**

The Doukkala region, an Atlantic coastal area with a mix of Arab and Berber tribes near Portuguese-controlled Mazagan and Safi, was a fertile agricultural plain and textile center.

* **Haik:** The single most important garment for commoners, a large, versatile piece of woven fabric (approx. 5m x 1.5m) draped to envelop the body from head to toe. Worn by both men and women over underclothes, it provided protection and modesty. Doukkala haiks were famous for quality and a major export, traded widely via trans-Saharan routes. For commoners, it was likely woven from local wool, in plain, undyed, or earth-toned colors.  
* **Basic Underclothing:** Peasants wore a simple *qamƒ´·π£* (tunic) and *sarwal* (trousers) made from durable, locally produced fabrics like rough wool or linen. Dyes from local vegetables resulted in common colors like brown, red, and grey.  
* **Footwear:** Simple and practical, consisting of leather slippers known as **babouches** or sturdy leather clogs for rougher work.

#### **Portuguese Presence and Cultural Exchange**

The Portuguese presence in coastal enclaves was primarily military and commercial, forming alliances with some local tribes. Their influence on local Moroccan clothing was more economic than stylistic, as Moroccans did not adopt 16th-century Portuguese attire due to cultural and climatic differences. Instead, Portuguese demand for local products, especially textiles like the Doukkala haik, stimulated the regional economy and reinforced traditional goods production. While new European cloth may have entered coastal markets, the fundamental forms of Moroccan dress remained unchanged, reflecting a largely one-way exchange where Moroccan crafts were valued exports.

### **Conclusion**

The mid-16th century was a watershed in Moroccan history, transforming the state, its military, and its identity. The Wattasid-Saadian conflict showcased a clear narrative of military evolution: the Wattasids represented a medieval system, unable to keep pace with gunpowder technology and reliant on unreliable tribal loyalties. Their eclectic armor and decentralized command reflected a fragmented state. The Saadians, conversely, built a professional, disciplined army by combining *sharifian* ideology with a robust financial model from centralized taxation and trade. Their embrace of modern firearms, domestic arms industry, and sophisticated tactics gave them a decisive advantage, making their military machine the engine of their political success.

This transformation was mirrored in their material and symbolic culture. The Wattasids represented continuity with Morocco's Berber-led medieval past, likely inheriting banners from Marinid predecessors. The Saadians were conscious architects of a new identity; their adoption of the white, embroidered *al-'alam al-mans√ªr* was a brilliant act of political communication, claiming ancient legitimacy while signaling a radical and victorious renewal. Their embrace of Ottoman-influenced fashion for elite troops symbolized their ambition and alignment with the most powerful Islamic empire of the day, forging a unique Moroccan synthesis of power. Even the widespread production and export of the traditional *haik* in the Doukkala region reflected a vibrant local economy and deeply rooted culture that persisted amidst conflict. The Wattasid-Saadian conflict was foundational for the modern Moroccan state, establishing the principle of *sharifian* rule, a professional national army, and Morocco's unique position as an independent power, distinct from its European and Ottoman neighbors.

The provided resource details the history and rivalries of three prominent Zenata Berber tribes: the Ban≈´ Maghrawa, Ban≈´ Miknasa, and Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn. These tribes played significant roles in the political landscape of the Maghreb, particularly in Morocco, from the late 8th century until their eventual subjugation by the Almoravids in the 11th century.

### **Ban≈´ Maghrawa**

The **Ban≈´ Maghrawa** were considered the most powerful branch of the Zenata confederation, based primarily in central Morocco, with their influence extending from Taza to Fez, Tadla, and Sijilmasa. Their key leaders included al-Khazar ibn Haf·π£ in the 8th century, Mu·∏•ammad ibn Khazar who seized Taza in the 10th century, and **Zƒ´rƒ´ ibn  øA·π≠iyya** (978‚Äì1001), who ruled Fez and much of Morocco. They were predominantly loyal to the **Umayyads of C√≥rdoba**, although they occasionally "flirted" with the Fatimids before returning to their pro-Umayyad stance. The Maghrawa controlled Fez for approximately a century, from around 950 to 1069 CE. Their rule ended when they were crushed by the rising **Almoravids** and absorbed into their empire.

### **Ban≈´ Miknasa**

The **Ban≈´ Miknasa** were associated with the Middle Atlas region, including Taza and Sijilmasa, and were known for their shifting allegiances. Initially leaning towards **Kharijite Rustamid** ideologies, they later became strong allies of the **Fatimids**, especially in SijilmƒÅsa and Tripoli. Notable leaders included Ab≈´ Qurra (late 8th century), an ally of the Idrissids, and **MasƒÅla ibn ·∏§abb≈´s** (10th century), who served as a Fatimid commander. The Midrar ibn Yƒ´s≈´ of the 8th-9th century was the founder of the SijilmƒÅsa dynasty, which was held under the Fatimid orbit by the Ban≈´ WƒÅs≈´l and later the Ban≈´ Khazr≈´n, who expanded eastward to rule Tripoli in the 11th century. The Miknasa ultimately lost control of Morocco to the Maghrawa due to Fatimid decline and were absorbed by the Almoravids in the mid-11th century.

### **Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn**

The **Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn** were based in Middle Morocco, specifically from Taza to Sal√©, and were characterized as **militant Kharijites**. Their leaders included Ab≈´ Qurra Yafranƒ´ (8th century), an early Kharijite emir, and Yadd≈´ ibn Ya ølƒÅ (9th century), who fought the Idrissids. A significant figure was **Ab≈´ Yazƒ´d ‚Äúal-·∏§imar‚Äù** (d. 947), who led a major Kharijite revolt in Ifriqiya with IfrƒÅn support. In the 11th century, Ya ølƒÅ ibn Abƒ´ Bakr was an anti-Umayyad rebel based in Sal√©. The Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn were strongly Kharijite (IbƒÅ·∏çƒ´/·π¢ufrƒ´) and often opposed both the Fatimids and Umayyads. Centered on Sal√©, they frequently fought against the Maghrawa and Umayyads. Their power was eventually crushed by the **Almoravids** between 1050 and 1060 CE.

### **Conclusion**

In summary, the Maghrawa functioned as the de facto "kings" of Morocco, ruling Fez as allies of the Umayyads; the Miknasa acted as "bureaucrats/mercenaries," particularly with the SijilmƒÅsa bankers as clients of the Fatimids; and the Ban≈´ IfrƒÅn were "rebels," known for their hardcore Kharijite beliefs and perpetual insurgency. All three Zenata tribes ultimately fell to the **Almoravids in the 11th century**, who unified Morocco under Maliki Sunni Islam, bringing an end to their independent rule.

Morocco's linguistic landscape is characterized by the complex interaction of four primary languages: **Classical Arabic, Moroccan Arabic, Berber, and French**. This interplay is profoundly shaped by religion, ethnicity, and issues of identity, education, and development. The historical evolution of this situation over fourteen centuries is crucial for understanding current debates about language policy. Issues of language and identity frequently arise when multiple languages compete for cultural, political, educational, or economic space. The adoption of one language as official can confer legitimacy and prestige, potentially leading to feelings of exclusion and marginalization among speakers of other languages tied to their identity. The situation in the Maghreb (Algeria, Morocco, Tunisia) is distinct from the Middle East and Gulf regions due to different ethnic and linguistic make-up, and particularly its history as a French colony, which significantly altered the linguistic balance.

In contemporary Morocco, each of the four main languages occupies a distinct, sometimes contentious, space:

* **Classical Arabic** is a written language, primarily used in formal education, media, administration, and religion. It is the official language and dominant in written literary forms. It is not anyone's native language but is learned through formal instruction. Islam confers a privileged position to Classical Arabic as the language of the Qur'an and prayers.  
* **Moroccan Arabic** is the native language for the majority of the population. It is the language of popular culture, including works of literature, TV, cinema, and newspapers. While sharing properties with Classical Arabic, there are significant differences at lexical, phonological, morphological, and syntactic levels.  
* **Berber** is the language of the original people of Morocco and the native language of an estimated 40% of the population. Despite being the language of a vibrant culture, it is not recognized as an official language.  
* **French** is not an official language but holds dominance in higher education, particularly in the sciences, certain media sectors, and industries like banking. It also has a presence in cultural activities through Francophone literature.

The historical roots of this linguistic situation trace back to the **Arab conquest in the second half of the 7th century**. Before this, Berber, an Afroasiatic language, was prevalent across a wide swathe of North Africa. The Arabs introduced Islam and Arabic, and the conversion of Berbers was swift, even leading to a Berber-led military force invading Spain and establishing a Muslim state where Arabic became the language of scholarship for Arab, Berber, and Jewish scholars. Early Moroccan dynasties like the Almoravids, Almohads, and Marinids were Berber, while subsequent dynasties identified as Arab. The Arabs brought two varieties of Arabic: Classical Arabic, the religious language of Islam, and colloquial Arabic. **Diglossia**, the coexistence of two varieties of the same language family with separate roles and prestige, has characterized Arabic-speaking societies from early Islamic centuries. The Arabization of the Maghreb intensified with waves of immigration from the East, particularly the Banu Hilal and Banu Maaqil in the 12th century, and more significantly, an influx of Arabic-speaking Muslims from southern Spain (Andalusia) after the end of Arab-Muslim rule there in the late 15th century due to the Spanish Inquisition. While major cities gained sizable Arab populations, much of the countryside, especially mountainous and southern areas, remained predominantly Berber until the French colonial period. Berber's lack of a standard writing system and its absence as a language of scholarship or administration, particularly in religious institutions like Al-Qarawiyyin mosque, contributed to the relatively rapid Arabization of the Maghreb.

The **French occupation of Morocco in 1912** further complicated the linguistic map. The traditional Islamic education system, which largely neglected sciences and emphasized memorization of grammar and jurisprudence, made the ground "ripe" for French to establish itself. The French colonial administration introduced a new educational system with five main components: a European system, a Jewish system, a predominantly French Muslim system, a limited traditional Islamic system, and private "free schools" that were nationalistic in focus. The objective of French schools was to produce limited professionals who would not challenge the occupation, while elite Moroccan children were given French education to prevent exposure to pan-Arabic and pan-Islamic ideas from the Middle East. The French pursued a **"divide and rule" policy concerning the Berbers**, aiming to shield them from Arabic and Islamic culture by establishing Berber schools where Arabic and Islamic manifestations were "resolutely avoided". This policy, which culminated in the infamous **Berber Decree of 1914 (enacted in 1930\)**, aimed to create separate judicial systems for Arabs and Berbers. This decree sparked widespread protests from both Berbers and Arabs. Berbers revolted because they saw it as an attempt to weaken Islam among their community and divide the country, while Arabs viewed it as an attempt to deny Morocco's Muslim and Arab identity. These reactions demonstrated that for Berbers, Classical Arabic was crucial to their identity as Muslims, and for Arabs, it was an anchor to the East and a symbol of unity with other Arab countries. Nationalist leaders like Allal Al-Fassi envisioned a post-independence Morocco as an Arab country with Arabic replacing French in all spheres.

In the **post-colonial period (after 1956\)**, Morocco inherited educational and administrative systems dominated by French. Despite Classical Arabic being recognized as the official language in the constitution, most subjects, apart from religious studies, were taught in French. This situation was unacceptable to independence movement leaders, who advocated for the Arabization of education and administration, viewing the continuing presence of French as a symbol of incomplete independence. Graduates educated in the Middle East saw Morocco as an Arab country tied to the Arab East, arguing that French dominance in administration ignored the interests of the majority of citizens who did not speak it and infringed upon Islam and the national language. Arab nationalists also embraced Arabization, not primarily for religious reasons, but to firmly bind Morocco to other Arab countries, viewing French as an obstacle to pan-Arab unity. This pan-Arab nationalism, which had roots in the Middle East as a reaction to Ottoman rule, sought to establish Arabic as the central language for national and pan-Arab identity, advocating for comprehensive Arabization that would eliminate French, and importantly, local Berber and Arabic dialects from schools, radio, and television.

Conversely, Moroccan elites educated in the French system were less eager for comprehensive Arabization, seeing the continued presence of French as a connection to the West and finding no conflict with Arabic and French coexisting with separate functions. They also did not support a prominent role for Berber. Berber leaders, however, sought official recognition for Berber beyond folkloric representation, feeling threatened by Arab nationalism's emphasis on Arabic. They argued that Arabic could fulfill its religious role while allowing other languages to perform other functions, drawing parallels to countries like Pakistan and Iran where national languages are distinct from the language of religion. They also advocated for French to remain important, hoping it would prevent the Maghreb from being exclusively anchored to the East, which they felt threatened Berber identity. They argued for Arabization and Arabic as the language of Islam and national unity, but also for the creation of a Berber institute to preserve the language and for necessary bilingualism (Arabic/French) to maintain cultural relations with Europe and Africa. These arguments have become more forceful with eased restrictions on media and organizations, with some critics suggesting that Arabization is based solely on ideological considerations rather than linguistic reality. While the French historically tried to use Berber as a wedge during the colonial period, current Berber advocacy for French stems from a concern for their language and identity against the political pan-Arabic overtones of total Arabization.

Currently, elementary and secondary education in Morocco are Arabized, but university education, especially in the sciences, remains predominantly in French. This split creates problems for students with inadequate French backgrounds entering university and incentivizes enrollment in non-science subjects. It also exacerbates a socioeconomic gap, with strong competition for private French schools that only the wealthy can afford. While there is increasing acknowledgement of Berber as part of Moroccan identity, reflected in television news, papers, and magazines, this has not yet translated into concrete efforts like teaching Berber in schools or recognizing it as an official language. In the diaspora, particularly in Europe, there is an active Berber movement advocating for its teaching and development of a writing system, framing it as a human rights issue and asserting a pan-Maghrebi Amazigh national identity. French remains economically important, though English is gaining ground in higher education, with some proposing to replace French with English due to its international dominance and lack of colonial connotations. The complex interaction of these languages is determined by ethnicity, religion, social class, and educational background, making attempts to shift linguistic dominance challenging. Classical Arabic is expected to be maintained due to its religious role, while the maintenance of spoken languages like Berber and Moroccan Arabic will largely depend on ethnic identity.

The **Idrisid dynasty** (788‚Äì974 CE, 171‚Äì363 AH) was an Arab Muslim lineage that ruled most of present-day Morocco and parts of western Algeria. Named after its founder, Idris I, this dynasty established the first independent Islamic state in Morocco.

### **Origins and Foundation (Idris I)**

The Idrisids were a Hashemite family of Hijazi origin, tracing their lineage back to the Prophet Muhammad through Ali ibn Abi Talib and Fatima Zahra. Their ancestor, **Idris ibn Abdallah al-Kamil**, fled Abbasid persecution after the Battle of Fakh in Mecca in 786 CE and sought refuge in Volubilis in Morocco. There, local Amazigh (Berber) tribes, including the Awraba, pledged allegiance to him as their leader, prince, and imam in 788 CE (171 AH). Idris I began the construction of Fez and expanded his control across northern Morocco, reaching Taza in the East, the Atlantic plains, and Tadla in the South by 791 CE. However, in 793 CE, the Abbasid Caliph Harun al-Rashid orchestrated his assassination by poison.

### **Consolidation and Organization (Idris II)**

After Idris I's death, his son **Idris II** was born to his Amazigh wife, Kenza. Idris II assumed the imamate from 804 CE (177 AH) and reigned until 828 CE (213 AH). He played a crucial role in strengthening the nascent state by introducing new structures and institutions. He brought many craftsmen from Al-Andalus and Kairouan, founded Fez, and made it the state capital in 808 CE. He also adopted Islamic administrative systems such as the vizierate, secretariat, judiciary, and imamate. To reduce tribal influence, Fez attracted significant populations from Kairouan and Al-Andalus, fostering a diverse population that facilitated the spread of the Arabic language as the language of religion and culture. Under Idris II, the kingdom expanded significantly, becoming the most important political entity in the Maghreb al-Aqsa and extending its direct influence throughout the country.

### **Period of Division**

Upon Idris II's death, he left behind several sons. His eldest son, **Muhammad ibn Idris II**, succeeded him (828‚Äì836 CE). In 836 CE, Muhammad chose to divide the kingdom among his eight or more brothers, viewing it as a family inheritance. This division, while having some benefits, primarily led to the weakening of central authority and the emergence of regional emirates striving for maximum autonomy. The kingdom was divided into at least nine provinces, according to historical accounts:

* Muhammad ibn Idris retained Fez and its surroundings.  
* Al-Qasim ibn Idris received Tangier, Ceuta, Qalat Hajar al-Nisr, T√©touan, and Masmuda lands.  
* Dawud ibn Idris was given Hawwara, Tessoul, Meknes, the Ghayatha Mountains, and Taza.  
* Isa ibn Idris ruled Chellah, Sal√©, Azemmour, and Tamesna.  
* Yahya ibn Idris controlled Basra and Asilah, Larache, and the lands of Wargha.  
* Omar ibn Idris governed Lixus, Tergha, and the lands of Sanhaja and Ghomara.  
* Ahmad ibn Idris was granted Mekn√®s, Fazaz, and Tadla.  
* Abdallah ibn Idris controlled Aghmat, Nafis, Masamida, and Sous.  
* Hamza ibn Idris ruled Tlemcen and its territories.

Despite Muhammad ibn Idris's intention to maintain central oversight, conflicts soon erupted. Isa ibn Idris rebelled against Muhammad. Muhammad tasked his brother Al-Qasim (governor of Tangier) to suppress the rebellion, but Al-Qasim refused. Muhammad then commissioned Omar, who was governor of Ghomara, to act. Omar deposed Isa and confronted Al-Qasim, who had retreated to Asilah. Historical inquiries suggest the rebellion stemmed from Muhammad's decision to revoke the governors' right to mint coins, which significantly impacted Isa, who governed the silver-rich region of Wazqur. This led Isa to cut off the silver supply to the mint in Fez. Although conflicts occurred, they did not immediately lead to widespread fragmentation, and a truce was likely reached. Political stability within the Idrisid emirates was generally maintained, possibly due to the veneration the family enjoyed among the local Moroccan population, who viewed them as deeply integrated through marriage and local customs rather than as foreign rulers.

### **Later Idrisid Rulers in Fez and Decline**

Fez remained the central capital, ruled by several princes:

* **Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Idris** (836‚Äì848 CE) had a reign characterized by peace and prosperity.  
* **Yahya ibn Muhammad** (848‚Äì864 CE) oversaw extensive construction in Fez, including the expansion of the city and the building of the renowned Al-Andalus and Al-Qarawiyyin Mosques, attracting migrants from across the Islamic West.  
* **Yahya II ibn Yahya** (864‚Äì874 CE) faced a crisis due to his misconduct, leading to a revolt.  
* **Ali ibn Omar** (874‚Äì883 CE) stabilized the city but was later defeated by a Kharijite rebel and forced to retreat.  
* **Yahya ibn Al-Qasim ibn Idris** (died 904 CE) successfully defended the Idrisid state in Fez against Kharijite rebels.  
* **Yahya ibn Idris ibn Omar** (904‚Äì917 CE) was highly praised by historians like Ibn Khaldun as the "highest in kingship among the Banu Idris" and described as a scholar, Hadith preserver, eloquent speaker, and brave leader.

However, the late 9th and early 10th centuries saw the Idrisids come under severe external threat. The **Fatimids**, newly established in Ifriqiya, sought to extend their control over the entire Maghreb. Masala ibn Habbus al-Miknasi, the Fatimid governor of the Central Maghreb, led an army against the Idrisids, defeating Yahya ibn Idris near Meknes. Yahya was besieged in Fez and forced to surrender, becoming a vassal of the Fatimid Caliph. Masala, however, simultaneously appointed Musa ibn Abi al-Afiyah, his cousin, as head of the Miknasa tribe in Morocco. Musa then worked to undermine the Idrisids, eventually inciting Masala to imprison and exile Yahya, who died in tragic circumstances in 934 CE (332 AH).

A bitter struggle ensued between Musa ibn Abi al-Afiyah and the Idrisids. **Al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn al-Qasim**, known as Al-Hajjam, attempted to restore Idrisid authority, capturing Fez and initially defeating Musa. However, Musa retaliated, pursuing Al-Hasan back to Fez, where he was betrayed and died. Musa ibn Abi al-Afiyah then controlled Idrisid lands in northern Morocco, forcing the remaining Idrisids to seek refuge in the formidable fortress of Hajar al-Nisr in the Rif Mountains. The Idrisids' precarious situation was exacerbated by the larger conflict between the Fatimid Caliphate in Ifriqiya and the Umayyad Caliphate in Al-Andalus, with Morocco as their battleground. Despite their attempts at neutrality, the Idrisids were often compelled to submit to either side due to military pressure. Their ties to the Umayyads were perhaps stronger due to geographical proximity and the Umayyads' respectful treatment of Idrisid leaders.

### **End of the Idrisid Dynasty in Morocco**

Despite continuous persecution, the Idrisids maintained a presence through local leadership. Key branches included:

* **Banu Omar**: Based in Sadina (southern Rif), from whom the Hammudids (who briefly ruled Cordoba and later became Taifa kings in Al-Andalus) descended.  
* **Banu Dawud**: Influential in the Sebou Valley.  
* **Banu Al-Qasim**: A significant branch with centers in Basra and Asilah.  
* **Banu Isa**: Located in Wazqur in the Middle Atlas, controlling a silver mine.  
* **Banu Ubaydallah**: Settled in southern Morocco, founding the cities of Tamdoult and Igli, and holding significant spiritual influence.  
* **Banu Sulayman**: Descendants of Idris I's brother, Sulayman, who ruled Tlemcen.

By the mid-10th century, the Idrisids agreed to appoint **Al-Qasim ibn Muhammad ibn Al-Qasim** as their leader, who ruled until his death in 948 CE (337 AH). He was succeeded by his son, **Abu al-Aish Ahmad**, a scholar and jurist loyal to the Umayyads. Abu al-Aish chose to end his life fighting in Al-Andalus, where he was martyred in 959 CE (343 AH). His brother, **Al-Hasan ibn Kannun** (959‚Äì974 CE), took over. Facing renewed Fatimid attacks, Al-Hasan temporarily shifted his allegiance to them but reverted to the Umayyads once the Fatimid armies withdrew. Another Fatimid army, led by Buluggin ibn Ziri, forced Al-Hasan to again switch allegiance, this time actively participating against Umayyad supporters, which angered the Umayyad Caliph Al-Hakam II.

Al-Hakam II dispatched a large army to Morocco. After fierce battles, Al-Hasan was forced to retreat to Hajar al-Nisr before surrendering and being taken captive to Cordoba in 974 CE (363 AH). After two years, a disagreement with Al-Hakam II led to Al-Hasan and his family's exile to Fatimid Egypt, where they were well received. In 985 CE (373 AH), the Fatimid Caliph provided Al-Hasan with an army to reclaim his emirate in Morocco. However, Al-Mansur ibn Abi Amir sent a powerful army to intercept him. Al-Hasan sought clemency but was assassinated on his way back to Cordoba in 985 CE (375 AH). With the death of Al-Hasan ibn Kannun, the last of their kings, the Idrisid era in Morocco definitively ended.

### **Legacy of the Idrisids**

Despite their eventual decline, the Idrisids played a fundamental role in Moroccan history:

1. They effectively transformed Morocco into an Islamic land, actively spreading Islam throughout various regions.  
2. They initiated the first real attempt to move beyond tribalism by establishing an Islamic-style state with Fez as its capital, marking the true beginning of the Moroccan state in history.  
3. Their urban development efforts included founding new cities, revitalizing old ones, boosting commercial activity, and establishing numerous mints across Morocco.

### **Modern Idrisids and Chronology**

Today, Idrisids are found in the Maghreb countries and the Middle East, with notable historical figures such as Prince Abdelkader al-Jazairi (Algeria), King Mohammed Idris al-Sanusi (Libya), and Sharif Mohammed ibn Ali al-Idrisi (Jizan, Saudi Arabia).

The Idrisid rule spanned approximately 130 years and progressed through three main phases:

* **Foundation Phase**: 41 years, from Idris I to the death of Idris II in 829 CE.  
* **Strength Phase**: 30 years, under Muhammad ibn Idris, Ali ibn Muhammad, and Yahya ibn Muhammad.  
* **Weakness Phase**: 59 years, under Ali ibn Omar, Yahya III, and Yahya IV.

During the foundation and strength phases, the Idrisids successfully unified the country and subjugated various tribes, extending their state from far south Sous to the Rif in the north and beyond Tlemcen in the east.

The **Almoravid State** was a Berber Muslim dynasty centered in present-day Morocco. It established an empire that extended across regions of the Maghreb al-Aqsa (Morocco) and Al-Andalus (Islamic Spain), beginning in the 1050s and lasting until its fall to the Almohad State in 1147 CE. The Almoravids were a hereditary monarchy. The new state emerged from a reformist Islamic religious movement.

### **Name and Origins**

The term "Almoravids" (ÿßŸÑŸÖÿ±ÿßÿ®ÿ∑ŸàŸÜ) derives from the Arabic word "*al-Murabit*" (ÿßŸÑŸÖÿ±ÿßÿ®ÿ∑), meaning "the person who binds" or, metaphorically, "the person ready for battle in the fortress". The name is linked to a Maliki school of Islamic law called "Dar al-Murabitin" (ÿØÿßÿ± ÿßŸÑŸÖÿ±ÿßÿ®ÿ∑ŸäŸÜ), founded by the scholar Wajaj ibn Zallu in Souss al-Aqsa (southern Morocco). Wajaj sent his student Abdallah ibn Yasin to preach Maliki Islam among the Sanhaja Berbers. The name "Almoravids" thus refers to the followers of Dar al-Murabitin, "the house of those who were bound together in the path of God". While the exact timing and reasons for adopting this name are debated, it might be related to Wajaj ibn Zallu al-Lamtuni's *ribat* (fortified monastery) in Aklu (near modern Tiznit), where Abdallah ibn Yasin received his initial training.

The Almoravid movement initially relied on the Sanhaja Berber tribes. This tribal alliance transformed into popular support, which then became military backing, leading to the rise of a regional economic power due to their control over trade routes. This was combined with a reformist Islamic spirit based on the Maliki school of thought, leading them to call themselves "the state of *Ribat* and Reform". At the time of their emergence, Morocco was governed by several small states or tribal confederations, including Barghawata, Ghomara, Maghrawa in the center and north, and various Shi'ite and pagan groups in the south. These areas were characterized by continuous conflicts and widespread chaos. The Almoravids aimed to spread Islam and confront those who opposed it.

### **Foundation and Early Expansion**

The Almoravid movement began to take shape when Yahya ibn Ibrahim, a leader of the Juddala tribe, returned from Hajj in 427 AH/1035 CE. In Kairouan, he met Imam Abu Imran al-Fasi, a Maliki jurist from Fez. Yahya explained his people's ignorance of religious principles and asked al-Fasi to send a student to teach them. Abu Imran al-Fasi, who had previously failed to challenge the ruling Maghrawa in Fez, saw this as an opportunity for a reformist jihadist project. He instructed Yahya to seek assistance from his student Wajaj ibn Zallu al-Lamtuni, who ran a *ribat* (religious-military center) in Aklu. Wajaj chose **Abdallah ibn Yasin**, who accompanied Yahya ibn Ibrahim to the Sahara.

Ibn Yasin established a *ribat* near the Senegal River, intentionally close to the Ghana Empire, to train his followers in a jihadist environment and to remain in a constant state of readiness. He emphasized the implementation of Islamic law and gathered scholars around him. After initial efforts to spread the message peacefully, Ibn Yasin declared jihad against the tribes that refused to comply. The first military actions were against the Juddala, then the Lamtuna, and Musufa tribes, all of whom were defeated and submitted.

After Yahya ibn Ibrahim's death in a battle, Ibn Yasin appointed **Yahya ibn Umar al-Lamtuni** as the military leader, giving him the title "Amir al-Haqq" (Prince of Truth). This strategic choice of a Lamtuni leader strengthened the movement by attracting thousands from the Lamtuna tribe.

The Almoravids then focused on **unifying Morocco**. Their first major military campaign was to retake Awdaghost from the Ghana Empire in 447 AH/1056 CE, after which they expanded southward into West Africa, spreading Islam. In 445 AH/1053 CE, a request from the scholars of Sijilmasa and Draa for aid against the Maghrawa Zenata rulers led the Almoravids to march into the Draa region. They defeated the Maghrawa, killed their emir Mas'ud, and captured Sijilmasa, where Ibn Yasin implemented strict Islamic laws, destroying musical instruments, burning places selling alcohol, and abolishing illegal taxes. Sijilmasa later revolted due to the Almoravids' strictness, briefly returning to Maghrawa control.

After the death of Yahya ibn Umar in battle around 448 AH/1056 CE, Abdallah ibn Yasin appointed his brother **Abu Bakr ibn Umar** as the new leader. Abu Bakr quickly moved to conquer the Sous region, where **Yusuf ibn Tashfin** emerged as a key military commander, demonstrating administrative skill after capturing Sijilmasa. The Almoravids conquered Massa and Taroudant, replacing the existing Shi'ite presence with Maliki Sunni Islam. They then besieged and captured Aghmat in 449 AH/1057 CE, where Abu Bakr married Zaynab al-Nafzawiya, the former wife of the Maghrawa emir.

Abdallah ibn Yasin was wounded in successive battles against the Barghawata and died shortly thereafter. Abu Bakr then took on both religious and political leadership, further defeating the Barghawata. In 452 AH/1060 CE, Abu Bakr continued his conquests, opening all the lands of Zenata, Miknasa, and Luwata.

### **Founding of Marrakesh and Yusuf ibn Tashfin's Rise**

Due to overcrowding in Aghmat, Abu Bakr decided to build a new capital. He selected a location between two rival tribes, a wide, uninhabited land traversed by the Nafis River, near Mount Dern. The foundation of **Marrakesh** was laid in 462 AH/1069 CE. Before the city's completion, Abu Bakr left for the Sahara to settle tribal disputes, delegating authority over Morocco to his cousin **Yusuf ibn Tashfin** in 463 AH/1070 CE. Yusuf completed the construction and fortification of Marrakesh, establishing it as the new Almoravid capital.

Abu Bakr later formally relinquished his rule over Morocco to Yusuf ibn Tashfin, citing Yusuf's piety, courage, and justice. Abu Bakr then continued his jihad in the Sahara against pagan tribes. He divorced Zaynab al-Nafzawiya, advising her to marry Yusuf, which she did after her waiting period, further solidifying Yusuf's position.

Yusuf ibn Tashfin continued the unification of Morocco. He skillfully exploited divisions among the Zenata leaders and entered Fez without a fight in 455 AH/1067 CE. After suppressing a rebellion and recapturing Fez, he broke the power of Zenata tribes, including Maghrawa and Banu Ifran, and organized the city's mosques, hotels, and markets. By 467 AH/1074 CE, Yusuf had extended his control over almost all of northern Morocco, excluding Tangier and Ceuta. He then opened cities in the Central Maghreb, including Oujda, Tlemcen, and Oran, reaching Algiers, where he built the Great Mosque. By 475 AH/1081 CE, after thirty years of fighting, Morocco was unified under Almoravid rule.

### **The Conquest of Al-Andalus**

With Morocco unified, Yusuf ibn Tashfin turned his attention to **Al-Andalus**. The Muslim Taifa kingdoms were fragmented and warring, allowing Alfonso VI of Castile to expand his control, including taking Toledo. The people of Al-Andalus appealed to the Almoravids for help, including delegations of judges from Seville, Badajoz, and Granada. Yusuf agreed to cross into Al-Andalus, first securing Algeciras from the Taifa king al-Mu'tamid ibn Abbad to establish a secure crossing point.

Yusuf ibn Tashfin declared a general mobilization for jihad, gathering forces from across Morocco. The first units crossed to Algeciras in mid-Rabi' al-Awwal 479 AH/1086 CE, followed by Yusuf and his senior commanders. He then marched to Badajoz, resting in Seville for three days before calling upon the Taifa kings to join the jihad. The combined Almoravid and Andalusian forces met Alfonso VI's Castilian army at the **Battle of Zallaqa** in 479 AH/1086 CE, securing a major victory for the Muslims. After the victory, Yusuf ibn Tashfin was granted the title "**Amir al-Muslimin**" (Commander of the Muslims).

However, the Taifa kings quickly resumed their internal conflicts and even paid tribute to Alfonso VI. In response, Yusuf ibn Tashfin returned to Al-Andalus in 481 AH/1088 CE, intending to depose the Taifa kings and unify Al-Andalus with Morocco. He crossed for the third time in 483 AH/1090 CE, capturing Granada and exiling its ruler Abdallah ibn Buluggin. His commanders then proceeded to depose other Taifa rulers, capturing Cordoba in 484 AH/1091 CE, followed by Seville, Almeria, and Murcia. Yusuf left the Banu Hud in Zaragoza to defend against Christian attacks. The Almoravids faced significant resistance in Valencia from El Cid (Rodrigo D√≠az de Vivar), but eventually conquered the city in Rajab 495 AH/1102 CE.

Yusuf ibn Tashfin made his fourth and final crossing in 490 AH/1096 CE. He led an army against Castile, defeating Alfonso VI near Consuegra in 491 AH/1097 CE. He returned to Marrakesh, leaving his son Ali in charge of Al-Andalus with instructions to form a standing army to defend the fortresses and frontiers. Yusuf ibn Tashfin died in Marrakesh on 1 Muharram 500 AH/2 September 1106 CE.

### **Reign of Ali ibn Yusuf and Decline**

**Ali ibn Yusuf** succeeded his father in 1106 CE. He made administrative changes and appointed his older brother Abu Tahir Tamim ibn Yusuf as supreme commander of all Almoravid armies in Al-Andalus. Under Ali, Almoravid influence in Al-Andalus reached its peak in the first ten years of his reign. Zaragoza, the last of the Taifa kingdoms, submitted to Almoravid rule in 503 AH/1110 CE. The Almoravid forces achieved victories, including recapturing Lisbon and other cities in western Al-Andalus.

However, the Almoravid state entered a period of **decline**. Alfonso I of Aragon launched a major offensive against Zaragoza, capturing it in 3 Ramadan 512 AH/18 December 1118 CE after a severe siege, despite Almoravid efforts to relieve the city. This loss marked a significant blow to Almoravid prestige. In 514 AH/1120 CE, the Almoravids suffered a major defeat at the Battle of Cutanda against Alfonso I, further damaging their reputation. In 519 AH/1125 CE, Alfonso I conducted a deep raid into Al-Andalus, prompting a fatwa from Qadi Ibn Rushd al-Jadd, leading to the forced displacement of Christian Mozarabs from Granada to Meknes and Sal√© in Morocco due to their perceived collaboration with Alfonso. In 528 AH/1134 CE, the Almoravids decisively defeated Alfonso I at the Battle of Fraga, where Alfonso was mortally wounded.

Despite these military engagements, the Almoravid state began facing internal and external pressures. An **economic crisis** hit the state, marked by severe floods in Tangier (532 AH), locust infestations in Al-Andalus (526-531 AH) leading to famine and plague, and large fires in the markets of Cordoba and Fez. This, combined with insecurity and rising living costs due to the ongoing Almohad advance, caused a massive migration of Berbers from Morocco to Al-Andalus around 535 AH/1140 CE.

The **Almohad movement** emerged as a major threat during Ali ibn Yusuf's later reign. Founded by Ibn Tumart, who denounced Almoravid religious corruption and laxity, the Almohads gained followers in the Atlas Mountains. Ali ibn Yusuf tried to suppress the Almohads, but they grew in strength, engaging in numerous battles and besieging cities like Fez and Meknes.

After Ali ibn Yusuf's death in 1143 CE, his son **Tashfin ibn Ali** became heir. Tashfin focused on confronting the Almohads in Morocco, but his efforts failed. The Almohads, under Abd al-Mu'min ibn Ali, captured several cities and received pledges of allegiance from Almoravid commanders, including the head of the Almoravid fleet in Cadiz. Tashfin ibn Ali was killed in 1145 CE.

**Ibrahim ibn Tashfin**, aged 14, briefly succeeded him, but his uncle Ishaq ibn Ali refused his rule, leading to internal conflict within Marrakesh as the Almohads besieged the city. In 18 Shawwal 541 AH/March 1147 CE, the Almohads entered Marrakesh, ending the Almoravid dynasty with a brutal massacre of its inhabitants and the Lamtuna tribe, including the young Ibrahim. Some Almoravid remnants, known as the Banu Ghaniya, continued to rule in the Balearic Islands and parts of Ifriqiya, maintaining the Abbasid allegiance and the Maliki school of thought against the Almohads.

### **Almoravid Policy and Administration**

The Almoravid administrative system was an **emirate of Muslims**, based on the principle of *shura* (consultation) in selecting the emir. The emir of Muslims was the supreme commander. Cities were governed by governors (*wulat*) chosen from the Sanhaja, particularly the Lamtuna tribe, based on their piety, justice, and administrative and military competence. These governors were closely monitored by the emir and his deputies, who could transfer or dismiss them for negligence or incompetence. Deputies often held significant political and military authority, especially in Al-Andalus, where they were tasked with confronting rebellions and leading wars. Prominent deputies included Sir ibn Abi Bakr, Ali ibn Yusuf, Tamim ibn Yusuf, and Muhammad ibn Aisha.

Initially, Almoravid emirs relied on scribes, but later adopted a ministerial system. Ministers, often from the Lamtuna tribe, managed financial and administrative affairs and served as trusted advisors. Military ministers, like Sir ibn Abi Bakr, and scribal ministers, who were jurists, also existed. The Almoravid administration also established a **Chancery Department** (Diwan al-Insha'), led by renowned Andalusian literary figures.

#### **Title of Emirate**

After Abu Bakr ibn Umar's death in 480 AH/1087 CE, Yusuf ibn Tashfin was proposed the title "Amir al-Mu'minin" (Commander of the Faithful) by jurists. However, Yusuf considered this title exclusive to the Abbasid Caliphs, who descended from the Prophet's family and ruled Mecca and Medina. Recognizing the Abbasid Caliph as the sole legitimate representative of the Islamic Caliphate, Yusuf adopted the titles "**Amir al-Muslimin**" (Commander of the Muslims) and "Nasir al-Din" (Defender of the Faith). This implicitly recognized Abbasid legitimacy while asserting his political authority over the Maghreb and Al-Andalus. Almoravid coinage, like the Sijilmasi dinars, initially bore ambiguous recognition of the Abbasid Caliph, but later, especially during Abu Bakr ibn Umar's rule (440-480 AH/1058-1087 CE), the phrase "Abdallah Amir al-Mu'minin" appeared, raising questions about whether it referred to the Almoravid founder or the Abbasid Caliph. Maliki jurists explained that Yusuf's title required Abbasid approval, leading him to send a delegation to Baghdad, headed by Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi and Qadi Ibn al-Qasim. Caliph al-Mustazhir Billah approved Yusuf's request, confirming the continuation of the caliphal tradition and acknowledging the Sanhaja family of Banu Wartanatiq (from which Yusuf descended) as the political authority in the Islamic West, with the duty to protect its frontiers and uphold the Maliki *madhhab* (school of thought).

#### **Judiciary**

The Almoravids granted extensive powers and independence to judges, providing them with financial support and executive authority. The judicial system was hierarchical, with a general head of the judicial institution, usually in Marrakesh, and regional heads known as "*Qadi al-Jama'a*" (Chief Judge) in Morocco and Al-Andalus. Prominent judges included Ibn Hamdin, Ibn Rushd al-Jadd, and Ibn al-Hajj al-Qurtubi. Almoravid emirs encouraged impartiality, instructing judges to treat the strong and weak equally. Judges also had advisory councils of scholars and supervised state expenditures, particularly for endowments and public interests, acting as both "disbursing officers" and "state inspectors". Almoravid emirs preferred Moroccan judges for these roles. Jewish communities were allowed to maintain their own courts for internal disputes, without Almoravid interference. However, in cases involving both Muslims and Jews, disputes were brought before Muslim courts.

### **External Relations**

#### **Abbasid Caliphate**

The first official contact between the Almoravids and the Abbasids occurred after the Battle of Zallaqa and Yusuf ibn Tashfin's conquest of Al-Andalus. However, informal connections predated this, as Abu Imran al-Fasi and his Maliki scholars were adherents of the Abbasid Caliphate. Abbasid caliphs' names were inscribed on Almoravid coins, initially alongside Abu Bakr ibn Umar, and later with Yusuf ibn Tashfin.

#### **Hammadid State**

Relations with the Hammadid state in Algeria were complex. Initially, the Hammadids (also Sanhaja Berbers) supported the Almoravids against common Zenata enemies. However, after the Almoravids seized Tlemcen, relations deteriorated. Despite Almoravid efforts to maintain good ties, the Hammadids seized opportunities to attack Almoravid territories, particularly during Yusuf ibn Tashfin's campaigns in Al-Andalus. Yusuf avoided prolonged war with them, seeing them as a buffer against the more dangerous Banu Hilal Arabs. He sought reconciliation, emphasizing the need for Muslim unity against Christians. Relations improved later, especially with the emergence of the Almohad threat.

#### **Mediterranean Sea**

The Almoravids engaged in naval activities to protect their Atlantic coasts and northern strongholds like Ceuta and Tangier. After their conquest of Al-Andalus, the Almoravid fleet grew to about 100 ships, stationed in major ports like Ceuta, Cadiz, and Almeria. Their shipbuilding industry, particularly in Almeria, gained international renown. The fleet participated in the siege of Ceuta in 476 AH/1083 CE and played a crucial role in transporting Almoravid forces to Al-Andalus. Under Ali ibn Yusuf, the navy flourished, reaching ten fleets by 1144 CE. The Almoravid fleet also launched pre-emptive attacks against Norman ships in the Mediterranean, reaching the coasts of Sicily, Galicia, and Catalonia to counter early Crusader activities.

### **Military Structure**

The Almoravid army was a new, organized, and professionally trained force dedicated to military life. Its core consisted of the *Mulaththamin* (veiled Sanhaja tribesmen), known for their strength and effectiveness in battle. As the state expanded, the army integrated new elements, including **Andalusian Arabs** (some of whom had settled in Morocco during the Idrisid era), and a fighting contingent of **Banu Hilal Arabs**. Yusuf ibn Tashfin also formed a personal **Special Guard** of 2,000 courageous soldiers recruited from various regions, including a Black Guard and Andalusian Christian youths who were often converted to Islam. These guards were chosen for their good physique, bravery, and skill.

#### **Navy**

To protect its extensive coastlines and strategic ports, particularly after incorporating Al-Andalus, the Almoravids developed a significant **naval fleet** . This fleet, which grew to about a hundred ships, was stationed in key ports such as Ceuta, Cadiz, and Almeria . Shipbuilding, especially in Almeria, became internationally renowned . The navy comprised transport and combat vessels, assisting ground forces and playing a role in the conquest of the Balearic Islands and other territories . Its strength peaked under Ali ibn Yusuf .

#### **Flags and Banners**

Flags and banners played a prominent role in the Almoravid military system, symbolizing their unique combat traditions and high morale. Abu Ubayd al-Bakri described the Almoravids' fighting spirit and tactical discipline, noting that they would stand when the banner was upright and sit when it leaned, fighting to the death rather than retreating. Ibn al-Khatib stated that the Almoravid banner was **black**, like that of the Abbasids, rectangular, multi-colored, and adorned with gold. Made of pure silk, these banners often bore Qur'anic verses in Kufic script, such as "There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah" and "Victory comes only from Allah, the All-Mighty, the All-Wise". The use of different colored banners also identified various tribal units within the Almoravid army, especially after it diversified with Berber, Andalusian, and Arab elements.

### **Culture**

The Almoravids, although originating from a desert environment, quickly embraced and fostered the rich civilization of the regions they conquered, particularly Al-Andalus.

#### **Literature and Language**

The Almoravids adopted **Arabic as the official language** of administration and the state across their territories. They encouraged scholars and literary figures from Al-Andalus to settle in Morocco, which significantly contributed to the Arabization of the Maghreb. Arabic had not been an official language in Morocco before the Almoravids. The era saw the production of numerous legal works in the Maliki *madhhab*.

Many notable literary figures emerged, including Qadi Iyad al-Sabti, Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi, and Ibn Bassam, who documented Andalusian literary life. Ibn al-Sayrafi, one of the Almoravid poets, authored "Taqsi al-Anba' wa Siyasat al-Ru'asa'" and "al-Anwar al-Jaliyya fi Akhbar al-Dawla al-Murabitiya". Poetry, particularly the Andalusian *zajal* and *muwashshah*, flourished during this period. Poets like Ibn Quzman, the leader of Andalusian *zajal*, gained prominence. The Almoravids appreciated this popular form of poetry, which blended Arabic and Iberian cultures. Despite the flourishing of popular poetry, the era also witnessed a revival of Arab-Islamic thought through philosophers like Ibn Rushd and Ibn Tufail. Women played a significant role in literary and social life, holding literary gatherings, and some, like Hawa bint Ibrahim ibn Tafliwit and Tamima bint Yusuf ibn Tashfin, were praised by poets for their charitable acts.

#### **Education**

The Almoravids prioritized education from the early days of their movement, spreading it throughout Morocco, even to remote villages. Upon entering Al-Andalus, they discovered new horizons in learning. They focused on educating both boys and girls from a young age. The traditional system of Quranic schools (*mah·∏çara* or *masjid*) continued, often located in mosques or teachers' homes. Students, including adults who left work, would often fund their own education. Teachers were typically pious elders, and a high standard of conduct and instruction was expected, though not always adhered to. Early education focused on the Quran, Arabic language rules, calligraphy, proper recitation, and basic arithmetic. Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi notably advocated for prioritizing Arabic and poetry over other sciences. Libraries, both large and small, proliferated, and travel for advanced studies between Morocco and Al-Andalus was common. Many Andalusian scholars, jurists, and poets also traveled to Marrakesh, offering their talents and services to the Almoravid rulers .

#### **Architecture**

Almoravid architecture blended with the distinctive Andalusian style, creating a unique hybrid. Morocco became a "visual extension of Al-Andalus". Yusuf ibn Tashfin brought artisans from Cordoba to Fez, who built hotels, baths, and water fountains. His son Ali commissioned Andalusian engineers to build the Tansift Bridge at the entrance to Marrakesh. Almoravid monuments showcased Moroccan-Andalusian art, notably in religious buildings like the Qarawiyyin Mosque in Fez and the Great Mosque of Tlemcen. The Qarawiyyin Mosque was expanded under Ali ibn Yusuf, acquiring adjacent land from Jewish owners through purchase, not seizure. The Almoravids also built numerous forts, walls, and castles, especially in Al-Andalus, employing decorative inlay techniques. The famous minbar (pulpit) of the Kutubiyya Mosque in Marrakesh was crafted in Cordoba between 1137 and 1147 CE, made of cedar and pine wood, designed to be movable on wheels. The construction of city walls was sometimes delayed; Marrakesh, their capital, remained without walls for seventy years until the Almohad threat emerged.

#### **Sciences**

Despite originating from a desert environment, the Almoravid state attracted scholars and put their expertise to its service, becoming a center of civilization and organization. While some historians, particularly Orientalists, characterized the Almoravid era as one of stagnation and suppression of philosophical sciences, it saw a flourishing of medical, astronomical, linguistic, and historical sciences. Prominent scholars included the geographer Al-Sharif al-Idrisi, known for his "Nuzhat al-Mushtaq fi Ikhtiraq al-Afaq" (The Excursion of One Eager to Traverse the Horizons), and Abu Ubayd al-Bakri, author of "Kitab al-Masalik wa al-Mamalik" (Book of Roads and Kingdoms). Mathematical, astronomical, and philosophical sciences also thrived, with figures like Ibn Bajja (Avempace), who served as a minister to the Almoravids. Ibn Bajja authored works on logic, geometry, mathematics, and philosophy, including "Tadbir al-Mutawahhid" (The Governance of the Solitary). Although accused of heresy by some, he remained a minister for twenty years before being poisoned in Fez. Other notable scientists included the physician Ibn Zuhr, whose "Al-Taisir fi al-Mudawat wa al-Tadbir" (Book of Simplification Concerning Therapeutics and Diet) significantly influenced European medicine.

#### **Economy**

At the outset of their movement, Almoravid revenues primarily came from *zakat*, *kharaj*, *jizya*, spoils of war (*ghanima*), and *ushur* (tithes). Upon establishing Marrakesh as their capital, they implemented an Islamic financial system, abolishing unlawful taxes imposed by the Zenata, such as *magharim* and *mukus*, and established a public treasury that accumulated unprecedented wealth.

The Almoravids' gold dinars were widely respected due to their high purity (up to 96% gold), sourced from the gold mines and trade routes of Ghana, which they controlled. This made the Almoravid dinar a standard currency in international trade, even circulating in Christian Europe until the 19th century under the name *Maraved√≠*.

Economically, the Almoravids developed agriculture, especially barley and date palms, in oases like Sijilmasa, and cultivated crops like melons, squash, cotton, and sugarcane. They also engaged in animal husbandry, raising camels for transport and resources, and mules and donkeys for local transport. Taroudant became known for sugarcane, wool, cotton, and camel hair textiles. Aghmat exported copper, textiles, wool, glass, stones, spices, and iron products to West Africa. Salt, extracted in slabs by slaves and transported by camels to Sudan and Ghana, was a crucial commodity and was even used as a form of currency. Jewish traders, with their extensive networks, played a pivotal role in facilitating trade between the Islamic West, the East, and Europe, especially after the unification of Al-Andalus and Morocco. The large Moroccan fleet also secured trade routes, fostering commercial activity with Western European countries.

### **Society**

The Almoravid society was predominantly Berber (Amazigh) in the Maghreb, but it also included Arab populations (especially Banu Hilal and their allies) and a significant presence of sub-Saharan Africans. Africans from south of the Sahara were recruited into the Almoravid armies, and women were employed in household services or as concubines, noted for their culinary skills.

**Women** held a respected position, participating in tribal councils and exercising influence over men. Almoravid leaders sometimes took their mothers' names (e.g., Ibn Aisha, Ibn Ghaniya). Zaynab al-Nafzawiya, Yusuf ibn Tashfin's wife, was particularly influential, described by Ibn Khaldun as "one of the world's most famous women in beauty and leadership". Even Christian women in the royal family influenced state policy.

#### **Christians and Mozarabs**

Christians in Al-Andalus lived mainly in cities like Granada, Seville, Valencia, and Cordoba, and some rural areas, likely in large numbers. Mozarabs (indigenous Spanish Christians who adopted Arab culture or converted to Islam) were particularly active in economic life, excelling in various professions, trade, and agriculture. Many Christians achieved high social status and influence, especially under Ali ibn Yusuf, who showed them considerable patronage. They were allowed to share public facilities like wells with Muslims and participate in prayers for rain. The Almoravid state protected their social rights and provided special cemeteries for them. However, some jurists called for strict measures against Christian women entering churches for reasons other than religious holidays due to perceived immoral behavior. Christians, while typically wearing their own distinctive clothing, also adopted Muslim attire, a practice some jurists tried to curb.

Ali ibn Yusuf, born to a Christian mother, was the first to widely employ Europeans in Morocco, not only in the army but also in administrative roles, such as tax collection in mountainous regions. Prominent Christian figures, like the Catalan noble Reverter I, held high positions and fought alongside Tashfin ibn Ali against the Almohads. Some, like Ali ibn Reverter (Reverter's son), converted to Islam and became Almoravid military commanders. Slavs (*Saqaliba*) also served as Almoravid guards. Christians were granted autonomy in managing their civil and religious affairs through a system where local leaders, known as *qawƒÅmis*, were responsible for their communities to the Muslim authorities. They had their own independent courts led by a "Qadi al-Nasara" (Judge of Christians). Despite this, some faced harassment and forced displacement, especially those who assisted Alfonso I of Aragon during his campaigns in southern Al-Andalus. A significant expulsion of Mozarabs occurred in 519 AH/1125 CE, with many resettling in Marrakesh, Sal√©, and Meknes. War captives, particularly from the Battle of Zallaqa, also increased the Christian population in Morocco.

#### **Jews**

Jews in Al-Andalus flourished after adopting Arab-Islamic civilization, developing their own schools and producing significant intellectual and literary works. Upon the Almoravids' arrival, Jews held considerable influence in Al-Andalus, with large numbers settling in cities like Granada (which was sometimes called "the city of Jews") and Lucena. While the Almoravid state largely respected Jewish legal autonomy, some sources indicate a period of hardship. Many Jewish scholars emigrated from the Iberian Peninsula, fleeing pressure to convert to Islam or face death. Notable rabbis, philosophers, and scholars emerged, such as Samuel al-Fasi (Samuel Marochitanus), who converted to Christianity and held high Almoravid positions. Ibn Ezra, a polymath, and Musa ibn Maymun (Maimonides), who grew up under Almoravid rule and wrote most of his works in Arabic, were also prominent. Jewish families like the Banu Tibbon were renowned for their translation work. Jews also played roles in administration and held ministerial positions, such as Abu Ya'qub Sulayman ibn al-Mu'allim and Sulayman ibn Farusal, who was assassinated during a diplomatic mission.

Towards the end of the Almoravid rule, especially under Ali ibn Yusuf, some precautionary measures were taken against Jews in Marrakesh, prohibiting them from staying in the capital overnight due to internal conflicts and the threat of Ibn Tumart. Some fatwas (religious edicts) supported punishing Jews and Christians who imitated Muslims in dress and prohibited them from supervising Muslims in their homes or selling alcohol and pork in Muslim markets. Ibn Abdun, an Andalusian jurist, advised against selling scientific books to Jews, claiming they would appropriate Muslim scholarship.

### **Rulers**

The Almoravid rulers and their approximate years of rule were:

1. Yahya ibn Ibrahim (1042-1043 CE)  
2. Abdallah ibn Yasin (1043-1055 CE)  
3. Abu Bakr ibn Umar (1055-1071 CE)  
4. Yusuf ibn Tashfin (1071-1106 CE)  
5. Ali ibn Yusuf (1106-1143 CE)  
6. Tashfin ibn Ali (1143-1145 CE)  
7. Ibrahim ibn Tashfin (1145-1145 CE)  
8. Ishaq ibn Ali (1145-1147 CE)
