Morocco's rich history is characterized by a succession of influential dynasties that have shaped its identity, culture, and architectural heritage, making it a internationally recognized multicultural nation with diverse UNESCO World Heritage sites. The nation is a preferred destination for tourists interested in nature, history, art of living, and Moroccan hospitality.

### **Idrisid Dynasty (789-974, 1465-1472)**

The **Idrisid dynasty** was the first royal dynasty to govern Morocco, founded by Idris I, who initially established Volubilis as its capital. From 789 to 978, the Idrisids exerted control over a significant portion of the Maghreb, encompassing present-day North Africa. During their rule, they were instrumental in building new cities, with Fez becoming the capital after Volubilis under Idris II and his successors. Numerous monuments attest to their architectural and religious contributions, notably the **Al-Qarawiyyin Mosque**, considered one of Morocco's most important mosques and a true architectural masterpiece. After several years of governance, the Idrisid dynasty fell, paving the way for the Almoravids.

### **Almoravid Dynasty (1060-1147)**

Following the Idrisids, the **Almoravids** emerged, expanding their rule across parts of the Maghreb region. They established **Marrakesh in 1062**, which became their capital and a crucial trade hub connecting sub-Saharan Africa with the Maghreb. The Almoravids constructed numerous religious sites, including mosques like the **Koutoubia Mosque**, whose construction began during their era, as well as Quranic schools, city walls, and palaces. They also developed an irrigation system to supply water to the entire region. The Almoravid dynasty's rule eventually ended when they were overthrown by the Almohads.

### **Almohad Dynasty (1145-1248)**

The **Almohads** conquered Morocco, deposing the Almoravid dynasty. Under the command of Ibn Tumart, they declared war on the Almoravids in the name of religion, advocating for strict monotheism, which is reflected in their name. The architectural and cultural legacy of this dynasty remains deeply embedded in iconic landmarks of Marrakesh, their capital. A notable achievement was the restoration of the **Koutoubia Mosque**, whose architecture was designed in the style of the Giralda in Seville. The Almohad dynasty ruled for approximately half a century but weakened following a defeat against the Christians in 1212\.

### **Marinid Dynasty (1244-1465)**

Founded by the Berber leader Abu Yahya, the **Marinid dynasty** established Fez as its capital after overthrowing the Almohads and expelling them from the Maghreb. Their reign, however, was not long-lived, as they were defeated by the Portuguese who launched attacks on the coast via Ceuta and the Strait of Gibraltar. Driven by religious values, the Marinids built numerous Zawiyas, mosques, and Quranic schools, such as those found in Sal√©. This dynasty was distinguished by various achievements and architectural principles, including **wood and plaster decorations, patterned tiled roofs, and diverse ceramics**. The Chellah Necropolis in Rabat contains remnants that testify to the rich culture, history, architecture, and religious institutions of this dynasty.

### **Saadian Dynasty (1554-1659)**

The **Saadian dynasty** ousted the Marinids from power, initially making Fez their capital before relocating it to Marrakesh. They declared war on the Portuguese and successfully reclaimed several cities, most notably **Agadir**. The Saadians formed an alliance with Spain to safeguard themselves from Turkish threats. Under the Saadians, Morocco experienced a **golden age**, flourishing in culture, knowledge, and wealth. They acquired gold from Sudan following their defeat of the African Songhai Empire. The dynasty also commissioned many artistic works, including the construction of a tower in Fez and proposed alterations to the Al-Qarawiyyin Mosque. In Marrakesh, they built the **Saadian Tombs, Ben Youssef Madrasa, and the El Badi Palace**. The Saadians were eventually dethroned by the Alaouite dynasty.

### **Alaouite Dynasty (1666-Present)**

The **Alaouites**, whose origins trace back to Tafilalt, removed the Saadians to assume royal power. They initially established Fez as their capital, which was later moved to Meknes under Sultan Moulay Ismail, and ultimately to **Rabat, the current capital of Morocco since 1912**. This dynasty brought about significant transformation in Fez, improving the city's landmarks and constructing walls, including the renowned **Bab Mansour gate**. The Alaouite dynasty continues to rule Morocco to this day, with **Mohammed VI** serving as the twenty-third king, dedicated to ensuring the kingdom's unity and upholding its religious values.

The history of Morocco is extensive, dating back to ancient times and marked by a succession of civilizations and dynasties that have shaped its unique identity, culture, and political landscape.

### **Prehistoric Eras (Early Stone Age to Bronze Age)**

Morocco's prehistoric past reveals a long history of human habitation and cultural development. Archaeological findings indicate human presence in the region for at least 400,000 years, with evidence of early human species and, notably, the oldest Homo sapiens remains discovered globally.

* **Early Discoveries:** Fossilized bones of a 400,000-year-old human were found in Sal√© in 1971\. In 1991, remains found in Jebel Irhoud were later dated in 2017 to at least 300,000 years old, making them the earliest known Homo sapiens fossils. Perforated shell beads, considered the oldest personal ornaments, were discovered in Taforalt Cave (82,000 years old) in 2007 and in Bizmoune Cave (over 142,000 years old) in 2021\.  
* **Stone Age Civilizations:** The **Acheulean Civilization** dates back approximately 700,000 years, with evidence from Thomas Quarries in Casablanca showing remains of *Homo erectus* and stone tools. The **Mousterian Civilization** emerged at least 300,000 years ago, with its earliest traces in Jebel Irhoud and its last appearance 80,000 years ago. This period saw advancements in human tools and adapted to initial humid, then arid, climatic changes. The **Aterian Civilization**, considered native to Morocco, originated over 145,000 years ago. Aterian people specialized in hunting gazelles using stemmed tools like arrows and spears, and also hunted large animals, demonstrating adaptability to new climates. The **Iberomaurusian Civilization** marked the Upper Stone Age, beginning around 22,000 years ago. This period saw significant population growth, artistic, cultural, and organizational innovations, with specialized tools for processing meat, hides, and bones. Key findings include numerous human remains and artifacts in Taforalt Cave.  
* **Neolithic and Bronze Age:** The **Capsian Civilization** flourished in Morocco and North Africa between 8000 BCE and 2700 BCE. Discoveries at Under Ghar Cave indicate the presence of **Cardial** (Neolithic) and **Bell Beaker** (Bronze Age) civilizations, evidenced by pottery, ceramics, tools, and weapons. The geography of Morocco during the Middle Stone Age (20,000-5000 years ago) was more like a savanna, supporting hunter-gatherer cultures before the region became drier after 5000 BCE, leading to the development of early agriculture and cattle domestication.

### **Classical Era (8th Century BCE \- 7th Century CE)**

This era saw significant external influences, laying the groundwork for later political entities.

* **Phoenician and Carthaginian Influence:** Recorded history for Morocco begins with Phoenician colonization of the coast between the 8th and 6th centuries BCE, establishing trading posts and integrating Morocco into Mediterranean trade networks. Lixus and Thymiaterion are notable early cities. Carthage expanded its dominance over these coastal areas from the 5th to the late 3rd century BCE, and Carthaginian explorer Hanno established several trading centers along the Atlantic coast. These societies adopted Punic culture, developing livestock farming and pottery production.  
* **Kingdom of Mauretania:** An independent Amazigh kingdom, Mauretania, emerged around 300 BCE, extending from the Moulouya Valley to the Atlantic and from northern Morocco to the southern Atlas Mountains. Under kings like Bocchus I, its political reach extended into central Algeria. Rome's interest in the region grew, leading to Juba II being installed as king under Roman protection in 25 BCE.  
* **Roman Period:** Following the assassination of Ptolemy (Juba II's son) in 40 CE, Rome annexed Mauretania, dividing it into Mauretania Tingitana (northern Morocco, with Tingis/Volubilis as capital) and Mauretania Caesariensis (northern Algeria). Roman control, primarily through alliances rather than military occupation, focused on economically beneficial coastal plains. Christianity was introduced in the 2nd century CE, spreading among urban populations, slaves, and Amazigh peasants, with some Christian movements serving as political protests. After the 3rd century CE, Roman authority weakened, and local Amazigh rule re-emerged, enduring through Vandal invasions and Byzantine attempts to restore imperial control, which largely failed to extend beyond Ceuta.

### **Early Islamic Era (8th Century \- 11th Century)**

The arrival of Islam fundamentally transformed Morocco's political and cultural landscape, leading to the establishment of the first independent Islamic states.

* **Islamic Conquest and Berber Revolt:** The Islamic conquest of Morocco began in the mid-7th century and was completed by the early 8th century, spreading Arabic language and Islam. Indigenous Berbers embraced Islam but retained their customary laws and paid taxes to the new Islamic administration. However, in 740 CE, Amazigh tribes revolted against the ruling Umayyad Caliphate, instigated by Kharijite teachings. This Berber Revolt led to Morocco's secession from Umayyad and Abbasid control, fragmenting it into several small, independent Amazigh states.  
* **Independent Amazigh Emirates:**  
  * **Burghwata State:** The first independent Islamic kingdom to emerge from the Caliphate's rule, the Burghwata governed the Tamesna region (from Sal√© to Safi) for over three centuries (744-1058 CE).  
  * **Nekor Emirate:** Established in 744 CE in the Rif region by Salih ibn Mansour, it was the first independent Islamic emirate in the Far Maghreb and adhered to Sunni Islam, unlike other contemporary Kharijite emirates.  
  * **Banu Midrar (Sijilmasa):** This emirate, mentioned as one of the independent states, was a significant entity at the "gate to the Sahara".  
  * **Idrisid Dynasty (788-974 CE):** Founded by Idris ibn Abdallah al-Mahd, a descendant of the Prophet Muhammad, who fled Abbasid persecution and was welcomed by the Awraba Amazigh tribe in Volubilis in 788 CE. Idris I expanded control across northern Morocco before his assassination. His son, Idris II, established Fez as the capital in 808 CE and expanded the state's influence. The Al-Qarawiyyin Mosque was founded during their rule. Despite fragmentation and eventual subjugation by the Fatimids and Umayyads of C√≥rdoba, the Idrisids laid the foundation for an independent Moroccan state.  
  * **Maghrawa State:** Led by Ziri ibn Atiyya (d. 1001), the Maghrawa extended their influence to Fez and consolidated their rule in northern and central Morocco. They remained largely loyal to the Umayyads of C√≥rdoba, ruling Fez for about a century until their defeat by the Almoravids.  
  * **Banu Ifran** and **Miknasa**: Other significant Zenata Berber tribes mentioned as operating independently or in alliance with larger powers during this fragmented period.

### **Imperial Dynasties (11th Century \- 17th Century)**

Morocco experienced periods of significant unification and expansion under powerful dynasties, establishing vast empires.

* **Almoravid State (1060-1147 CE):** Originating from the nomadic Sanhaja Berber tribes in the Sahara, the Almoravids were a religious reform movement led by Abdallah ibn Yasin. They founded Marrakesh as their capital in 1069 CE. Under Yusuf ibn Tashfin, the Almoravids unified all of present-day Morocco, parts of western Algeria, and Al-Andalus, establishing the first unified Moroccan state extending from north to south.  
* **Almohad State (1147-1269 CE):** Led by the religious reformer Mahdi Ibn Tumart, the Almohads organized the Masmuda tribes to overthrow the Almoravids, whom they accused of religious deviation. Under Abd al-Mu'min ibn Ali, they seized control of all of the Far Maghreb by 1147 CE and expanded their empire across North Africa and Al-Andalus, creating the largest empire in the Western Mediterranean since the Roman Empire.  
* **Marinid State (1269-1465 CE):** A Zenata Amazigh tribe, the Marinids overthrew the Almohads in 1269 CE, making Fez their capital. They conquered several cities, fought in Al-Andalus, and expanded into Algeria. The Marinids reached their peak under Abu al-Hassan Ali and Abu Inan Faris, who conquered Tlemcen and Tunis. However, they failed to maintain the vast Almohad legacy, and their rule ended with the fragmentation of Morocco into two kingdoms (Fez and Marrakesh) and the loss of coastal cities to Iberian occupation. A revolt in Fez against the last Marinid sultan, Abd al-Haqq, brought their rule to an end.  
* **Wattasid State (1471-1554 CE):** Another Zenata Amazigh dynasty, the Wattasids had served as viziers to the Marinids before seizing power after their decline. Their rule, largely confined to northern Morocco, lasted from 1471 to 1554 CE, facing challenges from internal fragmentation and external pressures.  
* **Saadian State (1510/1525-1659 CE):** This Sharifian dynasty (claiming descent from the Prophet Muhammad) emerged from southern Morocco (Draa region) as a jihadist resistance movement against Iberian occupation of coastal cities. Their military and political strength grew, leading to the capture of Marrakesh in 1525 CE and Fez in 1554 CE, establishing their state. A decisive victory over Portugal at the Battle of Wadi al-Makhazin (Battle of the Three Kings) in 1578 CE restored Morocco's prestige and brought economic benefits from African trade. The Saadian state declined after the death of Sultan Al-Mansour al-Dhahabi in 1603 CE due to internal power struggles.  
* **Alaouite Dynasty (1667-Present):** The current ruling family of Morocco, the Alaouites, trace their lineage to Ali ibn Abi Talib. They established a unified state under Moulay al-Rashid (1664-1672) and Moulay Ismail (1672-1727), who created the 'Abid al-Bukhari army and expelled the English from Tangier (1684) and the Spanish from Larache (1689). After Moulay Ismail's death, Morocco faced fragmentation, which was later reunified by Muhammad III (1757-1790), who also famously made Morocco one of the first countries to recognize the independence of the United States in 1777\. Succeeding rulers, including Muhammad IV and Hassan I, strengthened trade, modernized the army and administration, and navigated complex relations with European powers.

### **European Influence and Modern Era (1830-Present)**

The 19th and 20th centuries were marked by increasing European intervention, leading to colonial rule and eventual independence.

* **Growing European Influence (19th Century):** France developed a significant interest in Morocco after 1830\. The French-Moroccan War in 1844, involving the bombing of Tangier and Essaouira, weakened Morocco, leading Sultan Abd al-Rahman to sign a trade treaty with Britain in 1856\. The Spanish-Moroccan War (1859-1860) further strained Morocco's finances. European powers increased their presence and economic privileges, despite Sultan Hassan I's efforts at the Madrid Conference in 1880 to assert Moroccan sovereignty. German efforts to counter French influence led to the First Moroccan Crisis (1905-1906) and the Agadir Crisis (1911).  
* **French and Spanish Protectorate (1912-1956):** The Treaty of Fez, signed on March 30, 1912, officially made Morocco a French Protectorate. A second treaty on November 27, 1912, granted Spain zones of influence in northern (Rif) and southern (Ifni, Tarfaya, Western Sahara) Morocco. Tangier gained special status as an international zone through the Tangier Convention (1923) and Paris Agreement (1925). This period saw the **Rif War (1920-1927)** led by Muhammad Abd al-Karim al-Khattabi, who established the Rif Republic.  
* **Path to Independence:** The nationalist movement grew, supported by the Arab League and the United States, and notably by Sultan Muhammad V. Muhammad V, who ascended the throne in 1927 at age 18, became a central figure in the struggle for independence. The **Tangier Speech in April 1947** escalated resistance. In 1953, Muhammad V and the royal family were exiled by France, sparking the "Revolution of the King and the People". Their return in 1955 paved the way for independence.  
* **Independent Morocco (1956-Present):**  
  * **Muhammad V's Reign (1956-1961):** French recognition of Morocco's independence came on March 2, 1956, followed by Spanish recognition on April 7, 1956, leading to the reintegration of northern territories. Morocco also regained Tarfaya from Spanish occupation in 1958, and the international status of Tangier was abolished. Muhammad V passed away in 1961\.  
  * **Hassan II's Reign (1961-1999):** Hassan II ascended the throne in 1961, and Morocco adopted its first constitutional monarchy in 1962\. Significant events included the regaining of Sidi Ifni in 1969, and the **Green March on November 6, 1975**, where 350,000 volunteers marched into the Moroccan Sahara, followed by the Madrid Accords on November 14, 1975, which led to Morocco regaining its Saharan provinces. Mauritania later relinquished its share of the Sahara to Morocco in 1979\. Hassan II chaired the Al-Quds Committee from 1979 until his death in 1999\. The Union of Arab Maghreb was founded in Marrakesh in 1989\. A ceasefire between the Moroccan army and the Polisario Front came into effect in 1991 under UN sponsorship. Hassan II passed away on July 23, 1999\.  
  * **Muhammad VI's Reign (1999-Present):** Muhammad VI ascended the throne in 1999\. He initiated reforms, including the release of political prisoners and sentence reductions, and the establishment of a committee to compensate victims of arbitrary detention. Key developments include the adoption of Amazigh language education in primary schools in 2003 and the establishment of the Royal Institute for Amazigh Culture in 2001, following the Agadir Speech where the King affirmed Amazigh as a core component of Moroccan culture. In response to women's rights protests, a new family code (Moudawana) was adopted in 2004\. Morocco has maintained strong international relations, condemning the 9/11 attacks and becoming a major non-NATO ally of the United States in 2004\. The US-Morocco Free Trade Agreement came into effect in 2006\. A referendum on a new constitution was held on July 1, 2011\.

The history of Morocco is a rich tapestry woven through centuries of successive dynasties, each leaving its distinct mark on the nation's political, cultural, and architectural landscape. These dynasties, including the Idrisids, Almoravids, Almohads, Marinids, Saadians, and Alaouites, have stories ranging from founders fleeing persecution to reformist movements evolving into powerful states, ultimately shaping the Morocco recognized today. This historical journey has seen periods of architectural and civilizational flourishing, scientific advancement, and prosperity, alongside destruction, wars, and times of decline and poverty. The policies and challenges faced by these rulers often present striking similarities across different eras, influencing how the Moroccan populace has interacted with and shown loyalty to its governing families.

### **Pre-Islamic and Early Islamic Periods**

Morocco's human history stretches back approximately 400,000 years, with evidence of various civilizations interacting and developing. Early periods include the Acheulean, Mousterian, Aterian, Iberomaurusian, and Capsian cultures. The **Phoenicians** settled the Moroccan coast between the 8th and 6th centuries BCE, ruling over indigenous Amazigh (Berber) inhabitants who had been present for two millennia prior. **Carthage** later dominated the coastal region from the 5th century BCE. Eventually, the **Kingdom of Mauretania** emerged as the first political entity ruled by local kings in ancient Morocco, before its conquest by the Romans in 44 CE. This Roman period was followed by brief independence under the Amazigh Christian Baquates, then successive invasions by the Vandals and Byzantines, until the **Islamic conquest** of Morocco and North Africa by the Umayyad Caliphate in the 8th century CE. However, Morocco quickly detached from Umayyad control after the Berber Revolt led by Maysara al-Matghari in 740 CE, which paved the way for the establishment of independent Amazigh Muslim emirates such as Burghwata, Nekor, and Banu Midrar. The true Islamic state in Morocco, in the sense of a centralized government, was not established until 788 CE with the **Idrisid dynasty**.

### **Idrisid Dynasty (788-974 CE)**

The **Idrisid dynasty** originated from the Battle of Fakh near Mecca in 786 CE, where **Idris ibn Abdallah al-Hasan**, a descendant of Ali ibn Abi Talib, narrowly escaped Abbasid persecution. Fleeing with his Amazigh servant Rashid, Idris reached Egypt, then traversed through Barqa, Tlemcen, the lower Sous, and Tangier before settling in **Volubilis**. There, Rashid contacted Ishaq ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Hamid, the influential leader of the **Awraba Amazigh tribe**, who welcomed Idris. Recognizing his lineage to the Household of the Prophet, the Awraba tribe pledged allegiance to Idris as their caliph on February 15, 788 CE. Idris I subsequently invited surrounding tribes to join his rule, establishing himself as their emir. His primary goal became the spread of Islam among tribes still adhering to Zoroastrianism, Judaism, or Christianity. He led a large army to conquer Chellah (near Rabat), Tamesna, and Tadla, extending his campaigns to mountain tribes and securing Ceuta, where he built its mosque.

Abbasid Caliph Harun al-Rashid was alarmed by Idris's success, particularly his ability to rally Amazigh tribes. Historical accounts vary on Idris I's death; some state he was assassinated by a poisoned perfume sent by al-Rashid in 793 CE, while others suggest he died naturally. After his death, his servant Rashid became regent for Idris II, who was still in his mother's womb. Rashid was later mysteriously killed, and Idris II was raised by another Amazigh elder, Abu Khalid Yazid ibn Elias al-Abdi. **Idris II** took power in 808 CE (192 AH) and brought the state to its peak. He was instrumental in structuring and organizing the nascent state by introducing new institutions like the vizierate, secretariat, judiciary, and imamate. He further developed **Fez**, his father's nascent foundation, and made it the state capital, intentionally opening it to immigrants from Kairouan and al-Andalus to dilute tribal influences and foster a new civilization. This led to Fez becoming a center of cultural, scientific, and religious enlightenment, and the Idrisid state became the most significant political entity in the Maghreb al-Aqsa.

Upon Idris II's death in 828 CE, he left many sons, and his eldest, **Muhammad**, divided the kingdom among them as a family inheritance, resulting in several semi-autonomous regional emirates. This division led to internal conflicts, often triggered by Muhammad's decision to revoke the governors' right to mint coins. However, the populace under Idrisid rule largely remained stable, showing no major revolts. Historians attribute this stability to the **deep reverence and sanctity** accorded to the Idrisid family due to their lineage to the Prophet's Household, combined with their integration into Moroccan society through marriage and local customs, ensuring they were not seen as foreign rulers. This popular respect for the *shurafa* (descendants of the Prophet) evolved into a political principle that endures to this day.

Later Idrisid rulers in Fez, such as Ali ibn Muhammad and Yahya ibn Muhammad, saw periods of peace and prosperity, with extensive construction in Fez, including the expansion of the Al-Andalus and Al-Qarawiyyin Mosques. However, the dynasty faced a major external threat from the **Fatimids** of Ifriqiya in the late 9th century. Masala ibn Habbus al-Miknasi, the Fatimid governor, defeated Idrisid emir Yahya near Meknes, forcing him to become a Fatimid vassal. Masala's cousin, Musa ibn Abi al-Afiyah, then systematically worked to destroy Idrisid power, imprisoning and exiling Yahya, who eventually died of starvation. Musa seized Idrisid lands in northern Morocco, forcing the remaining Idrisids into the stronghold of Hajar al-Nisr in the Rif Mountains. The Idrisids became caught between the rival Fatimid and Umayyad Caliphates. The last Idrisid ruler, **Al-Hasan ibn Kannun**, shifted allegiances multiple times before being captured by the Umayyads and exiled to Egypt. He was later assassinated while attempting to reclaim Morocco with Fatimid support in 985 CE. The Idrisid dynasty's rule ended after approximately 130 years, but their descendants continued to exist through various families, and their legacy lies in establishing the first independent Islamic state in Morocco and fostering a sense of national identity centered around *sharifian* sanctity.

### **Almoravid Dynasty (1060-1147 CE)**

The **Almoravids**, also known as the "Veiled People" (*al-Mulaththam≈´n*) due to their custom of veiling their faces, emerged as a powerful dynasty after the Idrisids. Their story began as a reformist Islamic movement in the 11th century, spreading its influence to West Africa. The movement was initiated by **Sheikh Abdallah ibn Yasin**, a scholar sent by Kairouan's jurists at the request of Yahya ibn Ibrahim, chief of the Juddala tribe in southern Mauritania, to teach proper Islamic principles to a population described as having strayed from religion. After initial rejection, Ibn Yasin retreated to the Sahara, where he established a simple camp. Slowly, followers gathered, initially five, then increasing to a thousand within four years, forming a disciplined community known as the **Almoravids** (from *ribat*, a fortified monastery or military outpost and commitment to jihad). Their mission was to invite the Sanhaja tribes to their movement.

The movement gained significant military strength when **Yahya ibn Umar al-Lamtuni**, chief of the Lamtuna tribe, and later his successor **Abu Bakr ibn Umar al-Lamtuni**, joined with their entire tribe. After Abdallah ibn Yasin's death in 1059 CE, Abu Bakr ibn Umar assumed leadership of the 12,000 Almoravid mujahideen, transforming the movement into the **Almoravid state**. This state expanded rapidly, conquering vast territories from the Atlantic in the west to the Senegal River basin in the south, and eastward towards the Chad basin, while also extending north into the Atlas Mountains and across the Mediterranean into parts of the Iberian Peninsula (al-Andalus). Their empire eventually encompassed over eighteen African countries, including Guinea, Senegal, Sierra Leone, Ivory Coast, Mali, Burkina Faso, Niger, Ghana, Togo, Nigeria, Cameroon, Central African Republic, Gabon, as well as Tunisia, Algeria, Morocco, and Mauritania. Abu Bakr al-Lamtuni initiated this expansion before his death in 1087 CE.

**Yusuf ibn Tashfin** is considered the ruler of the Almoravid's golden age. He founded **Marrakesh** in 1070 CE, which became the dynasty's capital and a major political and cultural center of the Islamic West. The Almoravids constructed numerous religious sites, including mosques like the Koutoubia Mosque, as well as Quranic schools, city walls, and palaces. Moroccans treated the Almoravids with a similar reverence and sanctity as they did the Idrisids.

### **Almohad Dynasty (1145-1248 CE)**

The **Almohads** rose to power under the leadership of **Ibn Tumart**, who presented himself as the "awaited Mahdi" and "infallible Imam," adopting a legal methodology based on direct study of the Qur'an and Sunnah, rather than the prevailing Maliki jurisprudence in Morocco. During his travels, Ibn Tumart preached reform, adherence to Islamic law, and fighting against innovations and immorality, gathering many followers. He met **Abd al-Mu'min Ali al-Kumi**, who became a key figure in the movement. Ibn Tumart established his base in Tinmel and began organizing his followers, dividing his movement into a state-like structure with a ruling council. In March 1122, Ibn Tumart declared his leadership and amassed a large army to overthrow the Almoravids.

After the death of Almoravid Sultan Ali ibn Yusuf in 1142 CE, his successor Tashfin was unable to resist the Almohad army led by Abd al-Mu'min, who captured Tlemcen in 1144 CE. Moroccan cities steadily fell to the Almohads until Marrakesh was conquered on March 24, 1147 CE, where Isaac ibn Ali, the last Almoravid emir, was killed. **Abd al-Mu'min al-Kumi** assumed the title of Caliph and established the **Almohad state**, which then expanded to control vast areas of al-Andalus and all of the Maghreb. Abd al-Mu'min died in 1163 CE.

The Almohad state reached its peak under **Yusuf ibn Abd al-Mu'min** and especially **Yacoub al-Mansour**, who fostered scientific and cultural development. In 1195 CE, the Almohads decisively defeated the Christian kings in the **Battle of Alarcos**. However, under Sultan **Al-Nasir** (who began ruling in 1199 CE), a truce with King Alfonso VIII of Castile was exploited by Alfonso to strengthen his forces and allies. This led to a devastating defeat for the Muslims at the **Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa** (also known as the Battle of al-Hisn al-Iqab) in 1212 CE, after which Muslim power in al-Andalus never recovered. The Almohad state began to collapse rapidly after 1213 CE, with al-Andalus falling after 1228 CE, Tunis to the Hafsids, and Algeria to the Banu Abd al-Wad. From 1244 CE, the Almohads faced campaigns from the Marinids, losing control of Morocco and eventually being completely overthrown by them in 1269 CE.

### **Marinid Dynasty (1244-1465 CE)**

The **Marinids** were a particularly intriguing dynasty, as they did not base their rule on religious sanctity but on tribal principles of obedience. Descending from the Zenata Berber tribe of Banu Marin, they were initially nomadic bedouins. Their opportunity arose with the Almohad defeat at Las Navas de Tolosa in 1213 CE, which signaled the fragmentation of central Almohad authority, coupled with plague and insecurity. The Marinids exploited this weakness, launching successive attacks to expand their territory in the Rif and Gharb regions. Recognizing that Moroccans were accustomed to religiously sanctioned rule, the Marinids initially placed themselves under the nominal authority of the Hafsids.

In 1269 CE, after several wars, the Marinids defeated the Almohads in Marrakesh and established their rule. However, they chose **Fez** as their capital. Their first sultan, **Abu Yusuf Yaqub ibn Abd al-Haqq**, declared independence from the Hafsids and unified Morocco, incorporating Arab tribes to fight against separatist regions. The Marinid state expanded significantly under later sultans like Abu Said I and **Abu al-Hasan al-Marini**, who unified the Greater Maghreb under his banner, controlling lands from the Sous and Sahara in the south to Misrata in the east, and Ronda in al-Andalus in the north. To legitimize their rule, the Marinids sent armies into al-Andalus four times to support the Banu al-Ahmar, fighting fierce wars against the Spanish and conquering cities like Algeciras, Tarifa, Malaga, Ghomara, and Ronda, though many were later ceded by Sultan Yusuf ibn Yaqub.

Internally, the Marinids differed from previous dynasties by basing their rule on their numerical strength and allied Amazigh and Arab tribal organizations, forming the basis of the **Makhzen** (state administration). They allowed freedom of belief and religious schools, leading to the resurgence of the Maliki *madhhab* (school of thought) that the Almohads had suppressed. They revered and honored the Idrisid *shurafa*, rebuilding the shrine of Moulay Idris and reviving celebrations of the Prophet's birthday. The Marinids also welcomed **Andalusian Jews**, who fled al-Andalus. Their era saw significant urban and cultural development, with the construction of new cities like **Fez al-Jadid**, Tetouan, and Mansoura in Morocco, and al-Bunayya in al-Andalus. They built madrasas (religious schools), hospitals, mosques, *ribats*, and endowments. Their administration introduced new military and administrative roles, such as the *mashyakhat al-ghuzat* (leadership of raiders). Fez became a hub for scholars, historians, and literary figures like Ibn Battuta, Ibn al-Khatib, Ibn Khaldun, and Ibn al-Banna al-Marrakushi. The Marinid state left a lasting architectural and civilizational legacy, including palaces, fortresses, schools, mosques, and *zawiyas*.

The decline of the Marinid state began after the Portuguese capture of Ceuta in 1415 CE. This marked an era of collapse due to wars with neighbors, internal strife, power struggles, and continuous clashes with the Kingdom of Granada, compounded by the Marinids' lack of a large fleet and the general imbalance in the Mediterranean. The Marinids were finally defeated by the Portuguese in 1465 CE, bringing an end to their rule and solidifying Morocco's contemporary borders.

### **Wattasid Dynasty (1472-1554 CE)**

Following the Marinids, the **Wattasids**, another branch of the Zenata Amazigh tribe, governed Morocco. They had long served as viziers alongside their Marinid cousins before participating in their overthrow. Although most Wattasid leaders were massacred by the Marinids, two brothers survived, one of whom, **Muhammad al-Sheikh al-Mahdi**, seized power and entered Fez in 1472 CE. Upon his arrival, Portuguese invasions targeted Asilah and other Moroccan coastal areas. Successive Wattasid sultans struggled to lead military campaigns against the Portuguese while simultaneously quelling small emirates that capitalized on the weakened central authority. They failed to recognize the true threat posed by the **Saadians**, who launched campaigns from the Sahara. The last Wattasid sultan was overthrown and killed by the Saadians in 1545 CE, marking the beginning of Saadian rule.

### **Saadian Dynasty (1510/1525-1659 CE)**

The **Saadian dynasty** reinstated the principle of rule through sacred lineage, tracing their descent to the Prophet Muhammad's Household, specifically to Muhammad al-Nafs al-Zakiyyah, who was related to Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib. Some sources trace their lineage to Banu Saad ibn Bakr, Halimah al-Saadiyyah (the Prophet's wet nurse), hence the name "Saadians". Historians generally agree on their descent from the Prophet's Household in Yanbu, Hijaz. Their ancestors migrated from Yanbu to the Draa River valley in the Sous region of southern Morocco in the early 14th century, where they began spreading their call through Sufi orders. Many Moroccans saw **Muhammad al-Qaim bi-Amr Allah al-Saadi** as the leader who could rally them against the Portuguese and Spanish, and he was pledged allegiance in 1510 CE.

The Saadian rule began by mobilizing allied tribes to conquer Taroudant and its fortress, initiating battles against the Spanish and Portuguese who were exploiting the weakened Wattasid state. Muhammad al-Saadi also sent his three sons to travel the country, inviting people to the new state and recruiting fighters. The Saadians definitively ended the Portuguese presence on their lands in 1578 CE, following their victory in the **Battle of Wadi al-Makhazin** (also known as the Battle of Ksar el-Kebir or the Battle of the Three Kings). This battle, initiated by King Sebastian of Portugal, saw the deaths of three monarchs: the deposed Moroccan King Muhammad al-Mutawakkil, the ruling Moroccan King Abu Marwan Abd al-Malik, and King Sebastian. **Ahmad al-Mansour** inherited the Saadian throne in Fez after this decisive victory, which established Saadian political and military dominance across Morocco and the southern Mediterranean. Portugal lost its sovereignty and monarchy, while Morocco experienced stability, prosperity, and a flourishing of knowledge.

However, the Saadian state, despite its strong and rapid rise, also experienced a "resounding fall". Intense disputes within the ruling family and unrest in the country followed the succession of Abu Maali Zidan after the death of Ahmad al-Mansour al-Dhahabi. Conflicts continued among his sons, leading to the assassination of Abd al-Malik in Marrakesh by his brother al-Walid. The state fragmented, with revolts and separatist movements emerging, and independent emirates proliferated. The Sultan's authority barely extended beyond Marrakesh. The Saadians remained in Marrakesh until their state collapsed with the assassination of the last Saadian sultan in Marrakesh in 1659 CE.

### **Alaouite Dynasty (1666-Present)**

The **Alaouite dynasty**, like the Saadians, traces its origins to the Prophet Muhammad's Household. This family was known in the 13th century and beyond as the *Sharifian Sijilmasiyyin*, after the city of Sijilmasa (founded in 757 CE), which was the base of the Tafilalt region before it shifted to Rissani. According to historical texts, the ancestor of the Alaouites, **Hasan ibn al-Qasim**, known as Hasan al-Dakhil, settled in Sijilmasa in 1165 CE and died there in 1277 CE. He came to Morocco at the request of Sijilmasi pilgrims who desired a descendant of the Prophet's Household among them for blessings.

In the early 16th century, Morocco was deeply divided into regional political entities. In 1631 CE, **Sharif ibn Ali ibn Muhammad** emerged in the Tafilalt region, and the Alaouite dynasty began its ascent to power in the 17th century, with Sharif ibn Ali declared Sultan of Tafilalt. His goal was to establish the Alaouite state across Morocco. During the reign of **Muhammad I** (1671-1674 CE), challenges from movements seeking revenge for the Saadians were overcome, ending the era of small, feuding political entities. His son, **Al-Rashid** (1672-1674 CE), unified and pacified the country after a long period of division. He was succeeded by his brother, **Moulay Ismail**, whose reign (1672-1727 CE) was one of the longest in Moroccan history, characterized by strong centralized rule. Moulay Ismail reunified Morocco completely, extending its territory from the Mediterranean coast in the north to the Senegal River in the south.

The Alaouites unified Morocco and maintained state sovereignty by suppressing separatist conflicts and revolts, largely due to their religious and social standing, and the establishment of the **'Abid al-Bukhari army**. This elite military force, named for the oath of loyalty taken on Imam al-Bukhari's book, was initially formed from 2,000 soldiers to fight Moulay al-Rashid. Moulay Ismail significantly expanded this army by recruiting "slaves" from cities and rural areas and bringing soldiers from Sudan, integrating them with various tribes and cities. The 'Abid al-Bukhari's primary duties included tax collection, securing trade routes, and protecting the Alaouite state from rebels and enemies.

After Moulay Ismail's death, internal disputes among his sons nearly led to chaos, but the deep reverence of Moroccans for the Alaouite family prevented major revolts. Order was re-established during the reign of **Muhammad ibn Abdallah** in the latter half of the 18th century. The 19th century saw increasing European influence. Although the Alaouites ruled as absolute sultans until 1912, the Spanish and French protectorates reduced them to symbolic monarchs. With independence in 1956, **Mohammed V**, who had supported the nationalist cause, resumed the Alaouite role in independent state leadership. Shortly after, Mohammed V adopted the title of King instead of Sultan, a title maintained by his successors, **Hassan II** and **Mohammed VI**.

Since their ascension, the Alaouites have preserved the country's unity. They initially established Fez as their capital, which was later moved to Meknes under Moulay Ismail, and finally to **Rabat**, the current capital of Morocco since 1912\. Through this dynasty, the divisions that characterized Morocco during the decline of previous ruling families since the Islamic conquest were brought to an end.

This document provides a comprehensive list of rulers in Morocco from the Islamic conquest to the modern state. Historically, Moroccan rulers were referred to as **Sultans**, a title that transitioned to **King** following independence in 1956\.
